

{0}------------------------------------------------

# Succinct Arguments for BatchQMA and Friends under 6 Rounds<sup>∗</sup> (OR: How to prove soundness of succinct quantum arguments without rewinding)

Rishab Goyal † UW-Madison Aditya Jain ‡ UW-Madison Shashwatha Mitra GB § UW-Madison

#### Abstract

We study the problem of minimizing round complexity in the context of succinct classical argument systems for quantum computation. All prior works either require at least 8 rounds of interaction between the quantum prover and classical verifier, or rely on the idealized quantum random oracle model (QROM). We design:

- 1. A 4-round public-coin (except for the first message) argument system for batchQMA languages. Our results come in two flavors:
  - (a) Under the post-quantum hardness of functional encryption and learning with errors (LWE), we achieve optimal communication complexity (i.e., all message sizes are independent of batch size).
  - (b) Under the post-quantum hardness of LWE, we achieve optimal communication complexity except for the verifier's first message.
- 2. A 6-round private-coin argument system for monotone policy batchQMA languages, under the post-quantum hardness of LWE. The communication complexity is independent of the batch size as well as the monotone circuit size.

One of our main technical contributions is a new approach to prove soundness without rewinding cheating provers. We bring the notion of straight-line partial extractability to argument systems for quantum computation. All previous works crucially relied on rewinding cheating provers, thus needed "state-preserving" succinct arguments of knowledge (AoKs) for NP to prove soundness.

<sup>∗</sup>This is the full version of the CRYPTO 2025 paper.

<sup>†</sup>Email: rishab@cs.wisc.edu. ‡Email: ajain222@wisc.edu. §Email: ghantebanupr@wisc.edu.

{3}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-3-0"></span>1 Introduction

Succinct verification of classical computation is one of the most fundamental concepts in computer science. It has numerous connections across theoretical computer science and is extremely relevant for many practical applications. The first succinct argument was designed by Kilian [\[Kil92\]](#page-79-0), relying on probabilistically checkable proof (PCP) systems [\[BFL91,](#page-76-0) [ALM](#page-75-0)+98, [AS98\]](#page-75-1) and collision-resistant hash functions (CRHFs). Kilian's argument system is an interactive protocol between a prover and a verifier to prove the validity of any NP statement. It requires 4 rounds of interaction, and the communication complexity grows only polylogarithmically with the size of the NP witness.

Starting with Kilian's seminal work, a large body of work (see [\[Mic00,](#page-80-0) [Gro10,](#page-79-1) [BCCT12,](#page-75-2) [BCCT13,](#page-75-3) [SW14,](#page-80-1) [Gro16,](#page-79-2) [BCI](#page-76-1)+13, [BCC](#page-75-4)+17, [BHK17b,](#page-76-2) [KPY19a,](#page-79-3) [BKK](#page-76-3)+18, [JKKZ21,](#page-79-4) [CJJ22,](#page-77-0) [KLVW23,](#page-79-5) [BBK](#page-75-5)+23, [NWW23,](#page-80-2) [JKLV24\]](#page-79-6) and references therein) has investigated computationally sound proof systems (commonly called arguments [\[BCC88\]](#page-75-6)) that have low communication complexity. A large number of these design succinct non-interactive arguments (SNARGs) for NP and its numerous sub-classes (e.g., P, batchNP) in many different setup models (e.g., CRS, random oracles, designated verifier) under the hardness of varying cryptographic assumptions. Such arguments allow a "weak" classical client to delegate a classical computation to a "powerful" server, such that the server can convince the client of correctly performing computation, yet the client can catch a cheating server that runs in classical polynomial time. Although we do not yet know how to design SNARGs for all NP from standard falsifiable assumptions [\[Nao03\]](#page-80-3) in the plain model, and this remains a very active and exciting area of research.

This work. We study the problem of succinct classical verification of quantum computation [\[CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22]. The goal is to design succinct arguments that can be used by a single quantum server to prove correctness of quantum computation to a classical client. We require the verifier to be convinced in polylog-time with only polylog-bits of classical communication, and not get fooled by a cheating server. Prior works [\[CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22, [GKNV24,](#page-78-0) [MNZ24\]](#page-80-4) have successfully constructed designated-verifier SNARGs for QMA (and sub-classes such as batchQMA) with a classical verifier by relying on the post-quantum hardness of the learning with errors (LWE) problem [\[Reg05\]](#page-80-5) in the quantum random oracle model (QROM). As in the classical setting, one of the research goals in this area is to design succinct arguments for QMA and its sub-classes in the standard model, without relying on QROM.

Unfortunately, we do not yet have non-interactive succint arguments for even sub-classes[1](#page-3-1) of QMA in the plain model! Even more embarrassingly, we do not even know how to design interactive succinct arguments for any sub-class of QMA with the same round complexity as Kilian's original 4-round construction. In other words, a tantalizing open question is:

Can we design 4-round succinct arguments with classical verification for QMA (or its sub-classes) from standard falsifiable assumptions in the plain model?

All prior works [\[CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22, [GKNV24,](#page-78-0) [MNZ24\]](#page-80-4) need at least 8 rounds of interaction between the quantum prover and classical verifier. We believe that answering the above question will be instrumental in designing round-optimal succinct arguments for quantum computation with classical verification. Similar approach has been very successful in the classical setting towards designing round-optimal succinct arguments for various sub-classes of NP.

<span id="page-3-1"></span><sup>1</sup>Throughout, by sub-classes of QMA we mean those that cannot be classically simulated.

{4}------------------------------------------------

Our results. We answer the above question affirmatively for the class of batchQMA computations, which corresponds to batched (parallel) verification of many different QMA computations, where the proof size does not grow with the batch size. We also show how to reduce round complexity for the class of monotone policy batchQMA computations, which is a quantum analogue of monotone policy batchNP languages [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23], where the prover could prove an arbitrary monotone circuit over the validity of a batch of QMA computations. Informally, we summarize our results as follows:

- 1. Assuming post-quantum hardness of LWE, there is a 4-round public-coin (except for the first message) argument system for batchQMA. Here, the communication complexity (apart from the verifier's first message[2](#page-4-0) ) is independent of the batch size.
- 2. Assuming post-quantum hardness of LWE and functional encryption[3](#page-4-1) , there is a 4-round public-coin (except for the first message) succinct argument system for batchQMA. Here the total communication complexity is independent of the batch size.
- 3. Assuming post-quantum hardness of LWE, there is a 6-round private-coin argument system for monotone policy batchQMA. Here, the communication complexity is independent of the batch size and the monotone circuit size.

Our interactive arguments significantly depart from all prior works [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22, [GKNV24,](#page-78-0) [MNZ24\]](#page-80-4) that crucially relied on "state-preserving" arguments of knowledge (AoKs) for NP [\[CMSZ22,](#page-78-1) [LMS22\]](#page-79-7) for proving soundness of their argument systems. At a high level, prior works required a composable extraction property due to which they had to rely on a mild variant of Kilian's 4-round protocol to ensure any cheating prover can be rewound without significantly disturbing its state.

The core insight behind our argument systems is that recursive/composable extraction is not necessary to prove soundness for succinct argument systems for quantum computation. That is, we can completely avoid using state-preserving AoKs to design succinct argument systems for many non-trivial sub-classes of QMA. As we briefly discuss in Appendix [A,](#page-81-0) prior works are stuck at 8 rounds due to their usage of Kilian-style (state-preserving) AoKs for NP (and it is unclear whether existing protocols could be improved any further while correctly proving soundness). By avoiding the use of such AoKs, we are able to go under the 8 round mark.

As a side contribution, we believe that our approach significantly reduces the complexity of correlation intractable (CI) hash functions [\[CGH04\]](#page-77-2) that would be sufficient to make our succinct arguments non-interactive by applying the Fiat-Shamir [\[FS86\]](#page-78-2) methodology. Prior works relied on significantly stronger CI hashes, as they need to make the Kilian-style AoKs for NP also noninteractive which is known to be a very hard task [\[GW11\]](#page-79-8). We remark that similar approaches (of reducing the the complexity of necessary CI hashes) have been successful in the classical literature [\[CJJ22\]](#page-77-0). Thus, we believe our simplified template for interactive succinct arguments is an important step towards designing succinct non-interactive arguments for non-trivial classes of QMA in the standard model.

We also formalize a natural technique to make these arguments publicly-verifiable under the nonstandard assumption of differing inputs obfuscation [\[BCP14,](#page-76-5) [ABG](#page-75-7)+13, [Zha23\]](#page-80-6). Next, we provide a high level technical overview of our main ideas.

<span id="page-4-0"></span><sup>2</sup>Here the first round message is reusable, thus one can view the first round message as a common reference string (CRS). Thus, this could also be viewed as a 3-round succinct argument system for batchQMA with a long CRS.

<span id="page-4-1"></span><sup>3</sup> In the main body, we prove it under post-quantum hardness of indistinguishability obfuscation (iO) [\[BGI](#page-76-6)<sup>+</sup>01], but only need iO for circuits with log-length inputs. By relying on known transformations [\[AJ15,](#page-75-8) [BV15,](#page-77-3) [AJS15\]](#page-75-9) from FE [\[BSW11\]](#page-77-4) to iO, we can relax our assumption to only polynomial hardness of FE.

{5}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-5-0"></span>2 Technical Overview

Succinct classical verification of QMA computations is usually formalized as an interactive protocol between a quantum QPT prover exchanging classical messages with a classical PPT verifier. Crucially, the verifier does not have any quantum computing capabilities. Completeness states that an honest verifier must always accept whenever a prover honestly runs the protocol given valid QMA witness(es).[4](#page-5-1) Succinctness requires that the combined size of all messages between the prover and verifier should not grow with the size of the QMA witness.

Soundness for succinct arguments states that no QPT adversary should be able to efficiently compute (classical) responses to an honest verifier during the protocol execution such that the verifier declares the corresponding instance to be valid, when actually the instance is invalid. This can be formalized in different ways, and two most common approaches are non-adaptive and adaptive soundness. The difference between the two can be understood by looking at the information about the cheating instance that must be declared prior to the start of the protocol, i.e. before the verifier's first message. Non-adaptive soundness requires the instance to be fully declared before the protocol starts, while adaptive soundness puts no such constraints. In this work, we mostly stick to non-adaptive soundness, however we also prove some of our succinct arguments to be semiadaptively sound [\[CJJ22\]](#page-77-0), which is known to be strictly stronger than non-adaptive soundness.

The focus of our work is on two popular sub-classes, the class of batched computations [\[RRR16\]](#page-80-7) and monotone policy batched computations [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23]. Batched quantum computations, denoted by batchQMA languages, is defined w.r.t. any QMA language L. Where each instance in the batched language L ⊗k consists of a batch of (say) k instances x1, . . . , xk, and the instance is in the language iff every x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L for i ∈ [k]. Monotone policy batched quantum computations, denoted by monotone-batchQMA, are a powerful generalization of regular batchQMA, where each language is associated with a function f that can be implemented as a monotone (classical) circuit. An instance containing x1, . . . , x<sup>k</sup> is said to be in the language iff there exists a sequence of bits b1, . . . , b<sup>k</sup> such that f(b1, . . . , bk) = 1 and b<sup>i</sup> = 1 only if x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L. That is, it generalizes the predicate defined over the satisfiability of the underlying batch of instances to monotone circuits. Note that batchQMA languages are a specialization of monotone-batchQMA, where f simply corresponds to the and predicate (i.e., fbatchQMA(b1, . . . , bk) = ∧ibi). Colloquially, succinct arguments for batchQMA are also referred to as batch arguments (BARGs) for QMA, and similarly, succinct arguments for monotone-batchQMA are also referred to as monotone policy BARGs for QMA.

BARGs and monotone policy BARGs for NP have received tremendous attention over the last several years. Currently, we have multiple approaches to design such succinct argument systems in the literature. In terms of (plausible) post-quantum secure succinct arguments, [\[CJJ22\]](#page-77-0) designed BARGs for NP as well as [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] designed monotone policy BARGs for NP under the LWE assumption. By relying on post-quantum hardness of LWE, these give us post-quantum noninteractive succinct arguments for both classes. A natural question that we study is:

Can we design succinct arguments, with classical verifiers, for their quantum analogues?

To the best of our knowledge, designing succinct arguments for batchQMA or monotone-batchQMA, with classical verifier, in the plain model from standard assumptions does not face strong barriers [\[GW11\]](#page-79-8).

<span id="page-5-1"></span><sup>4</sup>We inherit the need for polynomially-many copies of the witness from prior works [\[FHM18,](#page-78-3) [CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)<sup>+</sup>22].

{6}------------------------------------------------

What do we currently know? Succinct interactive arguments for QMA in the plain model were first constructed in [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] using (post-quantum) indistinguishability obfuscation (iO) and the LWE problem. Moreover, they designed interactive BARGs for QMA under the hardness of LWE problem (i.e., without relying on iO). They could also make their protocols public-coin except the first message. By this we mean that the verifier's messages (except the first round message) are simply random coins with no inherent structure. They also describe how to transform their protocols into succinct non-interactive arguments in the Quantum Random Oracle model (QROM). In two very recent works [\[GKNV24,](#page-78-0) [MNZ24\]](#page-80-4), the dependence on post-quantum iO was removed. This led to the first construction for succinct interactive arguments for QMA, without iO, from the post-quantum hardness of LWE. Although these works relied on diverging techniques, they directly build on the protocols designed in [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22]. All these protocols required 8 or more rounds of interaction. (We discuss the 8-round limitation further in Appendix [A.](#page-81-0))

In this work, we go beyond the 8-round barrier in the plain model. To explain our main ideas, we provide a brief recap of the original design from [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22].

Recapping succinct interactive arguments. A typical classical verification protocol for quantum computation uses two key building blocks: the FHM protocol [\[FHM18\]](#page-78-3) and Mahadev's measurement protocol [\[Mah18\]](#page-80-8). The FHM protocol is a QMA verification protocol with a quantum verifier. The verifier measures a quantum state presented to it by the prover and runs a classical algorithm to complete the verification. Mahadev [\[Mah18\]](#page-80-8) constructed a classical verification protocol, where a classical verifier can retrieve measurement results from a (possibly malicious) quantum prover. This is an interactive protocol which requires the prover to submit 'classical' commitments to a quantum state. Following this, the verifier queries the prover and verifies the response using the commitment information it already possesses. Consequently, these two protocols can be combined together to build a QMA verification protocol with a classical verifier. Later on, [\[ACGH20\]](#page-75-10) further improved this template and made the setup (selection of basis for measurement) instanceindependent by showing that the same soundness guarantees can be reached by having a constant number of additional parallel repetitions and using random basis choices.

At a high level, the main idea in [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] was to design a verifier-succinct interactive protocols with classical verification. By verifier-succinct, it means that the verification protocol is such that the verifier's messages are fully succinct. That is, the prover's responses could be very large, but the verifier only sends short messages. [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] showed that by combining ideas from FHM as well as Mahadev's measurement protocol, one can design 'verifier-succinct' interactive succinct arguments for QMA by using iO to compress the verifier's messages. Moreover, for the case of interactive batch arguments for QMA, they showed that iO was not necessary and simply reusing the verifier's message for every instance in the underlying batch is sufficient.

They [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] showed that any verifier-succinct interactive protocols can be generically boosted to a fully-succinct protocol by relying on succinct arguments of knowledge (AoK) for classical computation. In a few words, the idea to make the prover's messages short was for the prover to send a short hashed digest instead of the full message and run an AoK to establish the existence of the pre-image (satisfying the desired predicate). Quite clearly, to prove security, one needs the AoKs to be fully extractable. Since the argument system is succinct, thus it is necessary to rewind a cheating prover. However, given that a cheating prover is a QPT adversary, each rewinding could significantly damage its state, thereby rendering extraction impossible. However, [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] proved that by relying on recent results in state-preserving AoKs [\[CMSZ22,](#page-78-1) [LMS22\]](#page-79-7), the above paradigm can be successfully used to design and prove the soundness of classical verification protocols for

{7}------------------------------------------------

QMA. We note that recent works follow a similar template, thus we refer the reader to [\[GKNV24\]](#page-78-0) for a detailed discussion.

The canonical BARG protocol. Below, we sketch the BARG protocol from [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] obtained by instantiating the tools as described above. Afterwards, we will discuss our improvement to reduce the round complexity of BARGs. Consider a QMA language L and a batch of instances x = (x1, . . . , xk). As in all prior works, we consider that a prover holds polynomially many copies of each quantum witness |ϕ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ ∈ RL(x<sup>j</sup> ).

- 1. The verifier samples fresh randomness r, computes the (pk,sk) pair for the Mahadev protocol, and sends pk to the prover.
- 2. For each j ∈ [k], the prover computes the proof state |ψ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ from the FHM protocol and then commits to it using the Mahadev protocol to get (y<sup>j</sup> , |st<sup>j</sup> ⟩). Here it reuses pk for every instance x<sup>j</sup> . It then computes a short digest ˆy = H(y1, . . . , yk) and sends ˆy to the verifier. This is followed by an AoK for the statement: ∃w1, . . . , w<sup>k</sup> s.t. ˆy = H(w1, . . . , wk).
- 3. The verifier samples a random challenge bit c ∈ {0, 1} and sends it to the prover.
- 4. For each j ∈ [k], the prover computes the opening using the Mahadev protocol to get z<sup>j</sup> . Subsequently, the prover computes another short digest ˆz = H(z1, . . . , zk) and sends ˆz to the verifier. This is followed by another AoK for the statement: ∃w1, . . . , w<sup>k</sup> s.t. ˆz = H(w1, . . . , wk).
- 5. Finally, the verifier sends the randomness r that it used to sample (pk,sk) in the beginning.
- 6. Let us denote the joint FHM and Mahadev instance verification predicate as VerifyInstance. The prover runs this for each instance and initiates a final AoK for the statement: ∃w (1) 1 , . . . , w (1) k and ∃w (2) 1 , . . . , w (2) k s.t. ˆy = H(w (1) 1 , . . . , w (1) k ) and ˆz = H(w (2) 1 , . . . , w (2) k ), and the joint verification procedure accepts, i.e., VerifyInstance(x<sup>j</sup> , w (1) j , w (2) j , c; r) = 1 for all j ∈ [k].

An immediate observation is that the verifier only performs classic operations, and the message size in each round does not scale with the batch size, k. Moreover, as already mentioned, soundness relies on the full extractability of all three state-preserving AoKs. Basically, through repeated rewindings, we can extract all the AoK witnesses. This means we can extract y1, . . . , y<sup>k</sup> and z1, . . . , zk, and then later, we could use this to reduce the security to that of FHM and Mahadev protocols.

It is quite evident that the full extraction property is extremely crucial, but it also turns out to be a major source of inefficiency. This is because each AoK requires at least 2 rounds of interaction. (Technically, it has four rounds, but the first round is reusable and can be merged with the current first round of the verifier, and the second round of Kilian can be merged with the prover's last message it sends before starting the AoK.) Therefore, the above protocol needs at least 10 rounds of interaction between the prover and the verifier. (Please refer to Appendix [A](#page-81-0) for more discussion.)

A natural thought and why it fails. A natural strategy would be to simply design and rely on a state-preserving non-interactive AoK? Clearly, this would immediately bring down the round complexity to 6 rounds by removing 4 unnecessary rounds of interaction due to the interactive AoKs. Unfortunately, non-interactive AoK for NP is a very challenging problem, and faces very strong barriers [\[GW11,](#page-79-8) [CGKS23\]](#page-77-5). Thus, it is unclear whether the above strategy of using non-interactive AoK can be successfully instantiated.

Although full extraction in a single round appears to be a significant roadblock, our starting observation is that partial extraction might still be possible. Moreover, this might not even need 

{8}------------------------------------------------

rewinding the prover, thus the notion of state-preserving arguments could potentially be totally bypassed.

Our observation draws inspiration from the classical setting, where similar issues were previously encountered and bypassed from standard cryptographic assumptions. Specifically, in the context of designing BARGs for NP, Choudhuri, Jain, and Jin [\[CJJ21,](#page-77-6) [CJJ22\]](#page-77-0) introduced the concept of somewhere extractable batch arguments (seBARGs) for NP. They designed such batch arguments from standard falsifiable assumptions, and demonstrated that known barriers surrounding adaptive full extraction [\[BHK17b\]](#page-76-2) no longer apply for somewhere extractability.

Briefly, somewhere extractability for BARGs states that, from an accepting proof π, we can extract a single witness w<sup>i</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> for the i ⋆ -th instance x<sup>i</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> in the corresponding batch of instance. Here i ⋆ is a special extraction index that can be programmed in the CRS of the BARG protocol, and the CRS does not leak any information about i ⋆ . A special property of somewhere extraction is that it can be performed in a straightline fashion, i.e. without rewinding a cheating prover. By relying on such a somewhere extractability (and index hiding), [\[CJJ21,](#page-77-6) [CJJ22\]](#page-77-0) and numerous follow-ups have designed BARGs for NP with semi-adaptive soundness. Semi-adaptive soundness is a notion that lies between non-adaptive and adaptive soundness, wherein a cheating prover must only commit an index i at the start of the protocol such that x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L/ . That is, rather than requiring the adversary to commit to the entire batch of instances at the beginning (like in the non-adaptive case), this provides more flexibility to the attacker thereby providing a stronger soundness guarantee.

Partial extraction suffices. Our first idea is a rather simple observation. That is, full extraction is not necessary for designing BARGs for QMA. Basically, we can replace each AoK in the canonical BARG protocol with an appropriate seBARG. This does not affect completeness since we only need succinct classical arguments for batchNP-style languages in the canonical protocol. Thus, doing so would ensure that we can bring down the number of rounds to 6 as well as we can still prove semi-adaptive soundness of the resulting protocol. The idea behind the soundness proof is that we can simply extract the i ⋆ -th witness from every seBARG proof without rewinding, and all such witnesses combined together can be used as a classical pseudo-witness proving QMA satisfiability of the i ⋆ -th instance x<sup>i</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> . By a classical pseudo-witness, we mean a classical proof in the sense of an appropriate combination of the FHM and Mahadev measurement protocol.

Consequently, this gives us a 6-round private-coin interactive BARG for QMA with semi-adaptive soundness. We provide more details in Section [4.](#page-29-0) We remark that it is an interesting question whether we could prove adaptive security of our protocol under slightly stronger assumptions. In the classical setting, recent works [\[BWW24,](#page-77-7) [Goy24\]](#page-78-4) partially address this problem by showing that the standard complexity leveraging technique can be used to prove adaptive soundness under subexponential hardness assumptions. It is unclear whether similar ideas could be used in the quantum setting, and we leave further exploration as an interesting open problem.

We want to highlight that the above BARG protocol still needs 6 rounds of interaction and is private-coin. Our intuition is that if we can make this protocol public-coin, then that could be a good step towards reducing the number of messages. Moreover, we know that for applying the Fiat-Shamir transformation, we anyways need the protocol to be public-coin. And, given how successful Fiat-Shamir transformation has been in the classical setting, it is natural to expect a similar strategy could be very influential in the quantum setting too.

We remark that many prior works [\[CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22] relied on the same strategy, which was they utilized a folklore generic template to transform their private-coin protocol into a publiccoin protocol in QROM, by leveraging post-quantum fully homomorphic encryption (FHE) [\[Gen09,](#page-78-5) 

{9}------------------------------------------------

[BV14\]](#page-77-8). We draw ideas from such a folklore private-to-public-coin paradigm to make our protocol public-coin and, along the way, further reduce its round complexity.

Getting BARGs down to 4-rounds and making them public-coin. The critical insight behind the transformation is to use FHE to encrypt the verifier's (secret) randomness at the start of the protocol and send the encrypted randomness to the prover. Given the FHE ciphertext, the prover can homomorphically perform all computations over the FHE ciphertext, and send back the encrypted output to the verifier who can decrypt and check for correctness. Clearly, this way the protocol's round complexity goes down as the verifier no longer needs to wait until the 5-th round to send the randomness r for the Mahadev protocol.

In a bit more detail, consider any succinct private-coin protocol where the verifier's per-round computation can be split into two parts – a deterministic computation that depends on the verifier's secret key; and a public-coin computation that is independent of the secret key. Note that the canonical BARG protocol fits such an abstraction. In such a scenario, the verifier can delegate the key-dependent deterministic computation to the prover. This is done by providing the prover a homomorphic encryption of the secret key so that the prover can run the deterministic computation homomorphically. The remainder of the protocol proceeds identically, except the prover and verifier also interact in another AoK so that the verifier can check that the homomorphic computation was performed correctly. The verifier finally decrypts the output FHE ciphertext to verify that it encrypts 1, to denote success of verification.

In the above template, it is essential that we use an AoK to prove that the homomorphic computation has been performed correctly over the FHE ciphertext, encrypting the verifier's key. This is because without the full extraction property of AoKs, it is unclear how could we catch a cheating prover. To emphasize the technical hurdle, let us simply use seBARGs instead of AoKs similar to how we used them earlier.

Attempt 1: Using seBARGs + FHE for making it public-coin. First of all, we mention that unlike the private-coin protocol, the AoK that is needed for making succinct arguments public coin is not batchNP-friendly. This is because the prover must: first, homomorphically evaluate the VerifyInstance circuit for the j-th instance on the FHE ciphertext encrypting verifier's secret randomness r for every j ∈ [k]; and second, it must homomorphically compute an and gate over all these ciphertexts to preserve succinctness and ensure that all individual ciphertexts were encrypting 1 (denoting successful verification).

Now the first part is clearly batchNP-friendly, but second part is not necessarily batchNPfriendly directly. Due to the fact that it is joint computation over all individually computed ciphertexts. While this appears to be a major issue, we can solve it by combining the entire batch of k FHE ciphertexts in a tree-based fashion. That is, at each level of the tree we simply take two FHE ciphertexts from the previous layer and homomorphically add them. In other words, we can design an FHE based AND-tree that accumulates the validity of all instances in the batch. And, the special feature about this AND-tree is that, similar to Merkle hash trees, we can efficiently prove that the resulting ciphertext at the root of the tree can be recomputed by just combining ciphertexts along a Merkle-tree-style opening path in the AND-tree. The point is such an implementation, based on FHE, turns the second part of the computation into a batchNP-friendly computation.

We describe the construction a bit more formally below.

1. The prover commits to the witnesses, creates a digest of the commitments, and sends an seBARG proof to the verifier to prove that it has created commitments to the witnesses and 

{10}------------------------------------------------

the digest contains a well-formed hash of all the commitments.

- 2. If the seBARG proof accepts, the verifier samples a random bit c and sends it to the prover.
- 3. The prover runs the verification circuit under FHE using the encrypted secret key. It then constructs an AND-tree of ciphertexts, which has a Merkle tree style opening for each instance. The prover sends an seBARG proof that shows that the AND tree was constructed correctly and the ciphertext corresponding to the root node of the AND tree.
- 4. The verifier accepts if the seBARG proof gets accepted and the ciphertext decrypts to 1.

Clearly, the above protocol satisfies completeness and succinctness as well as it makes the protocol public-coin. Only the first message of the verifier[5](#page-10-0) requires private randomness. At first glance, one might think that the above approach should also be sufficient to prove soundness by using similar somewhere extraction techniques that we previously discussed. Unfortunately, this is not the case. There is a subtle technical issue that prevents us from proving its soundness.

seBARGs are not enough. Let us start by stating the high-level strategy for proving soundness. Essentially, we wish to reach a hybrid where we can extract the witness for a single instance from all seBARGs and replace the FHE decryption of the root ciphertext with running the VerifyInstance circuit on the randomness r as in the [\[Mah18,](#page-80-8) [FHM18,](#page-78-3) [ACGH20\]](#page-75-10) protocols. Our hope is that once we can reach such a hybrid, then we could simply replace the original FHE ciphertext from an encryption of the verifier's secret randomness to an encryption of all zeros. Thus, any successful cheating prover must be breaking the soundness of the underlying non-succinct classic verification protocols [\[Mah18,](#page-80-8) [FHM18,](#page-78-3) [ACGH20\]](#page-75-10).

The issue that we face is: how to get to a hybrid where we can replace running the FHE decryption on the root ciphertext with running the VerifyInstance circuit? While it might appear to be a minor technical hurdle, it really is a fundamental bottleneck. Basically, in such a hybrid, if we do not rely on FHE decryption at all, then we clearly lose the ability to check whether the "non-extracted" instances have valid proofs or not. That is, since we only have somewhere extraction, thus we can only check the validity of the instance that we extract, but not all other instances.

As a result, replacing AoKs with seBARGs is inadequate, due to the lack of full extraction capabilities. The soundness issue outlined above is significant; thus highlights the necessity of using AoKs in the template used by [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22]. One of our main technical contributions is developing a new proof technique to smoothly transition to a hybrid where we no longer need FHE key for the target instance x<sup>i</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> , but can still check validity of proofs for rest of the instances efficiently.

Bypassing the verification problem via inner layer of FHE bootstrapping. Our approach to navigate around this issue is to consider a separate FHE key for every node of the AND tree. That is, instead of performing entire AND tree computation under a single FHE key, we will put one FHE key at each node. Our intuition is that by carefully modifying the protocol design, we can ensure that we can replace the final verification check from decrypting the root ciphertext under the root's secret key to decrypting FHE ciphertexts at the inner nodes corresponding to a single path (to the cheating leaf node) as per the Merkle-tree-style opening of the FHE AND tree. In this way, we can replace the FHE decryption check for the only the cheating leaf node, while keeping rest of th FHE decryption checks as is. After this, we can replace the encryption of the verifier's randomness to an encryption of all zeros for the cheating instance. Observe that throughout these

<span id="page-10-0"></span><sup>5</sup>Note that the first message can be viewed as a one-time setup or a CRS.

{11}------------------------------------------------

hybrids, we are assured of the validity of all other instances, while we check for the validity of the cheating instance locally.

We summarize our protocol next. The verifier performs a one-time setup, where it samples k FHE public keys, and encrypts the verification randomness for each instance individually. The verifier samples additional FHE public keys corresponding to the inner nodes in the AND tree, and encrypts them to enable desired FHE bootstrapping effect. It sends the set of FHE public keys, encryption of the verifier's randomness, and the FHE bootstrapping ciphertexts to the prover.

- 1. On receiving all the information, the prover commits to the witnesses and sends an seBARG proof to the verifier that shows that the computation was performed correctly.
- 2. If the seBARG proof accepts, the verifier samples a random bit c and sends it to the prover.
- 3. The prover runs the verification circuit under FHE using the encrypted secret key. It then constructs an AND tree of ciphertexts using each FHE public key. The prover sends an seBARG proof that shows that the AND tree was constructed correctly and the ciphertext corresponding to the root node of the AND tree. (Here it crucially relies on the bootstrapping ciphertext to generate the root ciphertext as well as prove correctness of computation of the root ciphertext in a batchNP-friendly way.)
- 4. The verifier accepts if the seBARG proof gets accepted and the root ciphertext decrypts to 1.

The soundness proof for the construction follows the strategy outlined above by carefully organizing the hybrids. Note that all messages (except the first round message) and proofs are succinct, independent of the batch size. The verifier's first round message is quite large, since we need FHE public keys that are twice the size of the number of instances. Thus, this gives us our first result for interactive batch arguments for QMA with long first message, and 3 short messages under the post-quantum hardness of LWE. We provide additional details on both the construction and the soundness proof in Section 5.

We remark that the first round message in the above protocol is reusable, thus a verifier could consider it as a long CRS (i.e., incur a one-time large setup cost for succinct interactive batch arguments for QMA).

Achieving optimal succinctness in 4 rounds via obfuscation. Our final idea is to rely on a post-quantum indistinguishability obfuscator (iO) [BGI<sup>+</sup>01] to make the verifier's first message succinct. Recall iO allows us to compiler any classical circuit C into an obfuscated circuit  $\hat{C}$  such that C and  $\hat{C}$  are functionally identical, yet an attacker cannot distinguish  $\hat{C}$  from another obfuscated circuit  $\hat{C}'$  for any other functionally identical circuit C'.

Note the first verifier message is non-succinct just because the verifier needs to send about 2k FHE keys and k classical-quantum commitment keys. Recall the reason the verifier cannot send just one of them is because we are unable to design a reduction in that case. Our insight is that the reduction algorithm in the previous protocol only needs to know about  $\log k$  FHE keys at any point during the intermediate hybrids. Thus, the rest of  $2k - \log k$  FHE keys could be selected arbitrarily. The reason we cannot simply design  $\log k$  keys like that in our previous protocol is because we don't know which  $\log k$  out of 2k FHE keys will be needed, and moreover, we need to hide our choice of those  $\log k$  keys. However, our observation is that we can do possibly do this under the obfuscation hood. That is, we can define a short program that has a PRF key K hardwired, and it uses K to deterministically generate all 2k FHE keys (and k commitment keys as well as other auxiliary material). Now by obfuscation security, it will not immediately reveal K.

{12}------------------------------------------------

Thus, by relying on punctured programming techniques [\[SW14\]](#page-80-1), we can simply hardwire the log k that we need to be securely generated, and leave the rest to be pseudorandomly generated. We provide more details later in Section [6.](#page-45-0)

We highlight that although we rely on iO in the above overview (as well as later in the main body), our protocol requires iO for only log k-bit inputs (i.e., iO for really short inputs). It turns out for such parameter settings iO can be designed from polynomial hardness of FE [\[AJ15,](#page-75-8) [BV15,](#page-77-3) [AJS15\]](#page-75-9), which is slightly weaker assumption. It remains an open question on whether we could relax our assumptions further to just post-quantum learning with errors. We remark that recursive composition techniques [\[BHK17a,](#page-76-7) [KPY19b,](#page-79-9) [WW22\]](#page-80-9) have been useful in the non-interactive setting to reduce the first-message/CRS size, but it remains an intriguing challenge to achieve similar results in the interactive setting. Any progress along this direction might readily give us fullysuccinct 4-round batch arguments for QMA.

Additionally, it is also an interesting question to explore whether we could use known correlation intractable hash functions to make these protocols completely non-interactive. We believe that our four-round protocol is very suitable for applying the Fiat-Shamir transform since the verifier's online message is a random string containing the challenge bit for the [\[Mah18,](#page-80-8) [FHM18,](#page-78-3) [ACGH20\]](#page-75-10) protocol. We leave this an exciting open question for future research.

## Generalizing to Monotone Policy batchQMA

The next goal of our work is to go beyond BARGs and design better classical succinct argument for every language in QMA. While the designing 4-round succinct arguments for all of QMA, in the plain model, is beyond the reach of our current work, we have identified a very interesting sub-class of QMA, that we refer to as monotone-batchQMA. Along the theme of our work, we design new succinct arguments for monotone-batchQMA that require only 6-rounds of interaction, under the post-quantum hardness of LWE and in the plain model.

Let us first formally define the class of monotone policy batchQMA. This is a quantum analogue of the class of monotone policy batchNP [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23]. Each language L (k) f in this class is associated with a predicate f, that can be implemented by a classical monotone circuit C, and another QMA language L (with a quantum relation R). Formally, it is defined as:

$$\mathcal{L}_f^{(k)} = \left\{ (x_1, \dots, x_k) : \exists (|\phi_1\rangle, \dots, |\phi_k\rangle) \text{ s.t } f(b_1, \dots, b_k) = 1, \text{ where } b_i = \mathcal{R}(x_i, |\phi_i\rangle) \right\}$$

where |ϕi⟩ is the quantum witness for instance x<sup>i</sup> and R(x<sup>i</sup> , |ϕi⟩) is used to denote whether x<sup>i</sup> ∈ L. In words, it defines non-deterministic generalization of batchQMA. Here the non-determinism stems from the fact that unlike vanilla batchQMA, where a prover is trying to prove validity of every instance in the batch, a prover corresponding to monotone policy batchQMA is only trying to prove that it knows an input assignment to the monotone circuit such that the circuit accepts, and if i-th bit of input assignment is 1, then the corresponding instance is satisfiable.

A private-coin Monotone BARG for QMA. We initially set our sights on a private coin construction. We recall that the private coin BARG template is to have the prover send a succinct digest of the computation and use an seBARG/AoK to prove the correctness of the computation. An intuitive idea would be to replace each of these seBARG/AoK with a monotone policy BARG by [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23]. Since instance verification occurs in the final round of interaction, monotone SNARGs should enable us to prove that the batch satisfies the monotone circuit C. However, it 

{13}------------------------------------------------

remains unclear which monotone circuit should be used for the second and fourth rounds of the protocol, where we essentially prove that the digest was computed correctly.

Upon closer inspection of our private coin BARG construction, we observe that we do not really need seBARGs for a very complicated language. But instead, we only need seBARGs for the the hash membership language. Such seBARGs are commonly referred to as somewhere extractable hash (SEH) functions in the literature. Thus, our first observation is that we could replace the second and fourth rounds seBARG proof with a hash digest computed using an SEH instead. Thus, we only need to use monotone policy BARG for the final round, where the monotone circuit is the same as the circuit for the underlying montone policy batchQMA language.

What circuits to use for monotone SNARGs? Unfortunately, we run into significant challenges while proving the soundness of the above construction. This is in part due to the subtleties that are introduced by the monotone policy BARG. A cheating prover in the previous BARG construction is far more restricted due to the simpler nature of the monotone circuit (essentially an AND tree) compared to a cheating prover for a general monotone circuit C. Indeed, we do not know where to set the index of extraction for the somewhere extractable hash function in order to catch such a cheating prover. This issue was also encountered in the classical setting. To navigate this complication, [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] use non-uniform reductions, aiming for non-adaptive soundness, and develop a proof system that allowed extraction on an index i from a special set J. The special set J is called the necessary set which consists of a subset of indices corresponding to the input wires of the monotone circuit C, that need to be set to one for the circuit to be satisfied. Although a non-uniform reduction could receive the description of such a necessary set J for C, extracting a valid witness from the proof remains an issue.

In [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23], they also introduced a powerful new type of hash function called predicate extractable hash function (PEHash) for bit-fixing predicates to enable such an extraction process. These hash functions allow for the extraction of an index j in the necessary set J along with the extraction for the predicate evaluation, i.e. f(x), on an input x. [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] also define an extension to PEHash called PEHash with tags (PEHT) where each bit of the input x can have an associated tag t. The PEHT hash function is also extractable on the tag value. Using these two primitives, [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] were able to appropriately extend somewhere extractability property to predicate extractability for any predicate f with a monotone circuit C.

Armed with this powerful primitive, we can replace the somewhere extractable hash function with a predicate extractable hash function with tags. Let s denote a string of length k s.t. the j th bit s<sup>j</sup> is set to true iff the j th instance belongs to the language L. We use PEHT in the second and fourth rounds of the protocol to hash the value of s with the commitment string y<sup>j</sup> and the opening string z<sup>j</sup> as tags for the index j. We retain the monotone SNARG for NP as is in the final round. For our reductions, we will make the hash values of s extractable on the non-uniform advice of the necessary set J and set the bit-fixing predicate string to a string of all zeros. Any cheating prover since must set one of the indices in J to be one to have the circuit C evaluate to one. We can use tag extraction to extract the values and perform the checks locally to catch the cheating prover.

How to handle inconsistent extraction? Although this approach seems to work, there is a fundamental problem with the above construction. How do we ensure that the witnesses extracted in each round for a cheating prover correspond to the same index from the necessary set J? Basically, what prevents a cheating prover from using three different values s, s ′ , s ′′ for creating the digest across the three rounds of interaction? The source of this problem is that PEHTs and PEHashs only guarantee that the extraction of the index and tag occurs for some index j in the necessary set J. 

{14}------------------------------------------------

A cheating prover can potentially evade capture by exploiting this property of PEHash extraction, which makes proving the security of the construction difficult.

Our approach way to navigate this issue is to additionally add an accompanying seBARG proof that ensures a cheating prover is committed to use the same string s in all three rounds. That is, it proves that the value of s used in each round of the prover computation is consistent. The prover sends such seBARG proofs in rounds four and six, demonstrating that the same value s was utilized in both rounds two and four, as well as in rounds four and six. Any cheating prover is restricted to produce openings for a consistent value of s throughout the protocol. Thus, our insight is that by appropriately using soundness property of the seBARGs, we can ensure that a cheating prover can not evade by using different s values across different rounds.

A final lynchpin to tie together all proofs. While the construction above guards against cheating provers who aim to exploit the nature of index extraction with inconsistent values for s, we still are yet to address the issue of differing extracted indices. We cannot catch any cheating prover if the witnesses are extracted at different locations. In search of more structure within the extraction output, we studied the PEHT construction provided in [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] more closely. After a careful inspection, we observed that their construction obeys a very special consistency property that allows our hybrids to go through. Since [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] did not require such a consistency property, thus they did not make it explicit. We observe that the index extraction procedure for PEHTs (and by extension PEHash) always extracts the lexicographically smallest index from the necessary set J, where the input x differs from the bit-fixing predicate string. This implies that, in our reductions, we can argue that the PEHT extracts the lexicographically smallest index j in the set J, whose value is set to one since the bit-fixing predicate string is set to all zeros. By combining this with the fact that, by carefully using additional seBARG proofs we can ensure consistency across the various rounds of the protocol, we are able to prove soundness of our argument system for monotone policy batchQMA.

We hide a lot of important technical details in the above overview. For instance, we are unable to argue consistency of extracted indices if we use the monotone SNARG construction by [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] as a black-box. Essentially, we have to open up their construction, argue the desired consistency properties. However, for the purposes of this overview we ignore these details, and defer further discussion to the main body. We refer the reader to Section [7](#page-53-0) for more details. The above concludes a brief overview of our 6-round private-coin protocol for monotone-policy batchQMA, without AoK. Unfortunately, unlike for batchQMA, we are unable to go below 6-rounds for monotone-policy batchQMA languages. In part, this is because the Merkle-tree-style FHE computation techniques we relied on for our BARG construction do not generalize as well to monotone-policy batchQMA. We leave this as an interesting open problem.

We remark that we can make our above protocol public-coin by relying on a state-preserving AoK, while keeping at 6-round. While this does improve the current state of the art, we only consider this as a supplementary result, and briefly describe it in Section [8.](#page-64-0)

## Other improvements and related work

Non-interactive protocols in QROM. It is well-known that any public coin interactive protocol can be compiled to a non-interactive protocol by following the Fiat-Shamir transformation under the QROM model [\[DFMS19,](#page-78-6) [DFM20\]](#page-78-7). [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] used the same principle to show that their succinct argument for QMA (and thus BARGs) can be compiled with [\[DFMS19,](#page-78-6) [DFM20\]](#page-78-7) to get a noninteractive succinct argument of QMA. For completeness, in Section [9,](#page-69-0) we also show that our 

{15}------------------------------------------------

protocols can also be compiled to get non-interactive (monotone policy) BARGs for QMA in the QROM model.

Making protocols publicly verifiable via diO. We also provide a folklore approach to compiler any privately-verifiable (designated-verifier) proof system to a publicly-verifiable proof system using the non-falsifiable assumption of post-quantum Differing Inputs Obfuscation (diO) [\[Zha23\]](#page-80-6). The key idea is to have the obfuscated programs of the prover key generation algorithm and the secret key generation step followed by verification algorithm as a part of the CRS and let the prover and verifier run the obfuscated programs. We can achieve non-adaptive soundness by modifying the program to behave differently for the adaptively chosen instance and relying on security of diO for extraction. The details of the construction are given in Section [10.](#page-71-0)

Related Work. In the classical setting, we have a rich body of results for the various classes of problems that are considered for this work. Starting with [\[KPY19a\]](#page-79-3) using a non-standard pairing based assumption, we have recent outstanding works by [\[CJJ21,](#page-77-6) [CJJ22,](#page-77-0) [WW22\]](#page-80-9) in designing BARGs for NP. In the pursuit for SNARGs for NP, [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23] and [\[NWW23\]](#page-80-2) build batched arguments for Monotone policy batchNP language.

The succinct classical verification of quantum computation, recently formalized by [\[CCY20,](#page-77-1) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22], remains an active area of research with recent contributions from [\[GKNV24\]](#page-78-0) and [\[MNZ24\]](#page-80-4). The work by [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] builds on the Mahadev measurement protocol [\[Mah18\]](#page-80-8) and achieves an 8 round succinct argument for QMA using state-preserving AoKs and post-quantum secure indistinguishability obfuscation (iO). [\[GKNV24\]](#page-78-0) extend this previous work to build a succinct and binding commitment scheme to quantum states, facilitating an 8 round public coin succinct argument without the need for iO. Orthogonally, [\[MNZ24\]](#page-80-4) use compiled non-local games to achieve information theoretic question-succinctness and thus also design a classical argument system for QMA. However, [\[MNZ24\]](#page-80-4) also relies on state-preserving AoKs for their construction, thereby achieving the same round complexity.

# <span id="page-15-0"></span>3 Preliminaries

Notation We will let PPT denote probabilistically polynomial time and QPT a quantum polynomial time algorithm. We denote the set of all positive integers upto n as [n] := {1, 2, . . . , n}. We use the notation {0, 1} n to denote binary strings of length n, while we use 1<sup>t</sup> to denote a unary string of length t. We denote any polynomial function of an input variable λ as poly(λ). Similarly, we denote all negligible functions as negl(λ). Throughout the paper, we use the notation Primitive.Function to denote the cryptographic primitive and the corresponding function being used. Similarly, we use prim.members with shortened lowercase letters to denote the cryptographic primitive and the members (parameters) being used. The transcript of an interaction between two entities A, B is denoted as ⟨A, B⟩ and the output of such a transcript is denoted by Out⟨A, B⟩.

Quantum Information Let H be a finite dimensional Hilbert space. A pure state is a unit vector |ϕ⟩ ∈ H. Let D(H) denote the set of all positive semi definite operators on H with trace 1. A mixed state is an operator ρ ∈ D(H), and is often called the density matrix. We abuse notation slightly and switch between the mixed state representation and the pure state representations. A ℓ-qubit quantum state ρ ∈ D(H), where H ≃ (C 2 ) ⊗ℓ , we denote the shorthand M(h, ρ) as the distribution resulting from measuring each qubit i of ρ in the basis determined by h ∈ {0, 1} ℓ . By convention, h<sup>i</sup> = 0 corresponds to a measurement of the i th register in the standard basis {|0⟩, |1⟩}, 

{16}------------------------------------------------

while  $h_i = 1$  corresponds to a measurement in the Hadamard basis  $\{|+\rangle, |-\rangle\}$ . We sometimes divide the  $\mathcal{H}$  into named registers with pre-set uppercase calligraphy letters, e.g.  $\mathcal{H} = \mathcal{A} \otimes \mathcal{B} \otimes \mathcal{C}$  where the function of each register is either explained or implied by the context. We denote the trace operator on a register  $\mathcal{A}$  of a quantum state  $|\phi\rangle$  as  $\mathsf{Tr}_{\mathcal{A}}(|\phi\rangle)$ .

#### <span id="page-16-0"></span>3.1 Hash Family with Succinct Local Openings

A Hash Family with Succinct Local Openings (HT) [Mer87] is a tuple of algorithms (Gen, Hash, Open, Verify), where each algorithm is defined as follows.

 $\mathsf{Gen}(1^N,1^\lambda) \to \mathsf{hk}$ : A PPT algorithm that uses the security parameter  $\lambda$  and the length of input  $1^N$  to generate the hash key  $\mathsf{hk}$ .

 $\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{hk},\mathbf{y}) \to \hat{y}$ : A deterministic poly-time algorithm that computes the hash of input  $\mathbf{y} \in \{0,1\}^N$  using hash-key hk to produce a hash-root  $\hat{y} \in \{0,1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda,\log N)}$ .

Open(hk,  $\mathbf{y}, j) \to (b, \mathsf{op})$ : A deterministic poly-time algorithm that outputs a bit  $b \in \{0, 1\}$  and openings  $\mathsf{op} \in \{0, 1\}^{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda, \log N)}$  to b using the hash-key hk, the input string  $\mathbf{y}$ , and an index  $j \in [N]$ .

Verify(hk,  $\hat{y}, j, b, op$ )  $\rightarrow 0/1$ : A deterministic poly-time algorithm verifies that the openings (b, op) are valid w.r.t  $(\hat{y}, j)$  for some  $j \in [N]$ .

<span id="page-16-2"></span>**Definition 3.1.** A Hash Family with Succinct Local Openings HT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify) satisfies the following properties along with collision resistance with respect to openings and values.

**Opening completeness**: For all  $n \leq 2^{\lambda}$ , for all  $\mathbf{y} \in \{0,1\}^n$ , for all  $j \in [n]$ , and for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{cc} b = y_j \wedge \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \hat{y}, j, b, \mathsf{op}) = 1 \end{array} \right. : \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{hk} \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda) \\ \vdots \\ \hat{y} \leftarrow \mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{hk}, \mathbf{y}) \\ (b, \mathsf{op}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{hk}, \mathbf{y}, j) \end{array}\right] = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Computational binding w.r.t openings: For any poly size adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , and for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \hat{y}, j, 0, \mathsf{op}_0) = 1 \ \land \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \hat{y}, j, 1, \mathsf{op}_1) = 1 \end{array} \right] : \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{hk} \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda) \\ (1^n, \hat{y}, j, \mathsf{op}_0, \mathsf{op}_1) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk}) \end{array}\right] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

**Theorem 3.2** ([Mer87]). Assuming the existence of collision resistant hash family, there exists a hash family with local openings (according to Definition 3.1)

## <span id="page-16-1"></span>3.2 Predicate Extractable Hash functions with Tags for Bit-fixing predicates

A Predicate Extractable Hash Function PEHT [BBK+23] is a hash function that is associated with a bit-fixing predicate class  $\mathcal{F}$  and a vector of tags  $\vec{t}$  s.t the hash output is **binding** to a specific predicate  $f = (J, y) \in \mathcal{F}$  and we can extract f(x), an index  $j \in J$  and the tag t associated with location j. The PEHT family is a tuple (Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex, ExtractTag) where each algorithm has the description of a monotone circuit C for f as an implicit input. The algorithms are defined as follows:

{17}------------------------------------------------

 $\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, N, f = (J, y)) \to (\mathsf{hk}, \mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{td})$ : A PPT setup algorithm that takes a security parameter  $1^\lambda$ , message length N and a bit-fixing predicate  $f \in \mathcal{F}$  defined by a set  $J \subseteq [N]$  and a string  $y \in \{0, 1\}^J$ . It outputs a hash key  $\mathsf{hk}$ , a verification key  $\mathsf{vk}$ , and a trapdoor  $\mathsf{td}$ .

 $\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{hk}, x, \vec{t}) \to \mathsf{v}$ : A poly-time deterministic algorithm that takes input a hash-key  $\mathsf{hk}, x \in \{0, 1\}^N$ , and tags  $\vec{t} = (t_1, t_2, \dots, t_N) \in (\{0, 1\}^T)^N$ . It outputs a hash-value  $\mathsf{v} \in \{0, 1\}^{T \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ .

Open(hk,  $x, \vec{t}, j$ )  $\rightarrow \rho$ : A poly-time deterministic algorithm that takes as input a hash key hk,  $x \in \{0,1\}^N$ , tags  $\vec{t} = (t_1, t_2, \dots, t_N)$ , and an index  $j \in [N]$  to output an opening  $\rho \in \{0,1\}^{T \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ .

Verify(vk, v, j, b, t,  $\rho$ )  $\to$  0/1: A poly-time deterministic algorithm that checks if the opening  $\rho$  is valid w.r.t. (v, j, b, t) for some  $j \in [N]$ 

 $\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) \to u$ : A deterministic extraction algorithm that uses the trapdoor  $\mathsf{td}$  and the hash value  $\mathsf{v} \in \{0,1\}^{T \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$  to output a bit u.

ExtractIndex(td, v)  $\rightarrow j$ : A deterministic extraction algorithm that uses the trapdoor td and the hash value  $v \in \{0,1\}^{T \cdot poly(\lambda)}$  and outputs an index  $j \in [N]$ 

ExtractTag(td, v)  $\to t$ : A deterministic extraction algorithm that uses the trapdoor td and the hash value  $v \in \{0,1\}^{T \cdot poly(\lambda)}$  and outputs a tag  $t \in \{0,1\}^T$ 

<span id="page-17-0"></span>**Definition 3.3** (PEHT). A Predicate Extractable Hash Family with Tags w.r.t. a bit-fixing predicate class  $\mathcal{F}$ , is the tuple PEHT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex, ExtractTag), satisfies the following that properties

**Opening Completeness**: For any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , any  $N \leq 2^{\lambda}$ , any predicate  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ , any index  $j \in [N]$ , any set of tags  $\vec{t} = (t_1, t_2, \dots, t_N) \in \{0, 1\}^{T \cdot N}$ , and any  $x \in \{0, 1\}^N$ , we must have

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,f) \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},j,x_j,t_j,\rho) = 1 \ : \ \mathsf{v} = \mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{hk},x,\vec{t}) \\ \rho = \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{hk},x,\vec{t},j) \end{array}\right] = 1$$

**Succinctness**: In the completeness experiment above, the size of the verification key vk and the hash value v is  $poly(\lambda)$ . The size of the hash key hk is at most  $|f| \cdot poly(\lambda, \log N)$ 

Computational binding w.r.t openings: For any poly size adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , any polynomial  $N = N(\lambda)$ , and any predicate  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ , there exists a negligible function  $negl(\cdot)$  s.t for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},j,0,t,\rho_0) = 1 \ \land \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},j,1,t,\rho_1) = 1 \end{array} \right. : \\ \frac{(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,J,y)}{(\mathsf{v},j,\rho_0,t,\rho_1) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk})} \right] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

**Prediate Hiding**: For any poly-sized adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , any polynomial  $N = N(\lambda)$ , and any two predicates  $f_0, f_1 \in \mathcal{F}$  s.t  $|f_0| = |f_1|$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , we have

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk}) = b \ : \ \frac{b \leftarrow \{0,1\}}{(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,f_b)} \end{array}\right] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

{18}------------------------------------------------

**Extraction Correctness**: For any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , any  $N \leq 2^{\lambda}$ , any predicate  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ , any set of tags  $\vec{t} = (t_1, t_2, \dots, t_N) \in \{0, 1\}^{T \cdot N}$ , any  $x \in \{0, 1\}^N$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$ , s.t for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , we must have

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} f(x) \neq \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td}, \mathsf{v}) \ : \ \dfrac{(\mathsf{hk}, \mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, N, f)}{\mathsf{v} = \mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{td}, x, \vec{t})} \end{array}\right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Index Extraction Correctness: For any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , any  $N \leq 2^{\lambda}$ , any predicate  $f = (J, y) \in \mathcal{F}$  s.t  $J \neq \emptyset$ , and any hash value  $\mathsf{v} \in \{0, 1\}^{T \cdot \mathsf{poly}(\lambda)}$ , we must have

$$\Pr \left[ \text{ ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{td}, \mathsf{v}) \in J \ : \ (\mathsf{hk}, \mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, N, J, y) \ \right] = 1$$

Consistency of Extraction: For any poly-sized adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , it holds for any polynomial  $N = N(\lambda)$  and any bit-fixing predicate described by a set  $J \subset [N]$  and a string  $y \in \{0, 1\}^J$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$ , s.t for any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{cc} j \in J \ \land \ \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) = 1 \ \land \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},j,1-y_j,t,\rho) = 1 \end{array} \right. : \\ \frac{(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,J,y)}{(\mathsf{v},j,t,\rho) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk})} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

and

$$\begin{aligned} & \text{Pr} \left[ \begin{array}{l} \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td}, \mathsf{v}) = 0 \ \land \\ & \mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{td}, \mathsf{v}) = j \ \land \\ & \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk}, \mathsf{v}, j, y_j, t, \rho) = 1 \end{array} \right] & \overset{\mathsf{(hk, vk, td)}}{} \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, N, J, y) \\ & (\mathsf{v}, j, t, \rho) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk, vk}) \end{aligned} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Consistency of Tag Extraction: For any poly-sized adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , it holds for any polynomial  $N = N(\lambda)$  and any bit-fixing predicate described by a set  $J \subseteq [N]$  and a string  $y \in \{0,1\}^J$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t for any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) = j \ \land \\ \mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) \neq t \ \land \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},j,1-y_j,t,\rho) = 1 \end{array} \right] \\ : \frac{(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,J,y)}{(\mathsf{v},j,t,\rho) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk})} \\ \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Remark 3.4. [BBK+23] also define the notion of the Predicate Extractable Hash family for Bit Fixing predicates (without the tags) as follows PEHash(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex). The syntax for the PEHash family remains the same as that of PEHT, but we omit the need for tags. Alternatively, PEHash can be considered as a PEHT family with each tag as  $\bot$ . The PEHash family satisfies all the properties defined in Definition 3.3 except the Consistency of Tag Extraction.

Remark 3.5. PEHTs [BBK<sup>+</sup>23] also satisfy a Lexicographically Smallest Index Extraction property by correctness of FHE. We define and prove this property in Section 7.2.

{19}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-19-0"></span>3.3 Somewhere Extractable BARG (seBARG) for NP

A Somewhere Extractable Batch Argument or seBARG [CJJ22] for an NP language  $\mathcal{L}$  (corresponding relation  $\mathcal{R}$ ) is a tuple (Setup, Prove, Verify, Extract), where each algorithm is defined as follows.

 $\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^k, 1^n, i^*) \to (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{td})$ : A PPT algorithm that takes security parameter  $1^{\lambda}$ , batch size k, input length n, and index  $i^* \in [k]$ . It outputs a  $\mathsf{crs}$  and trapdoor  $\mathsf{td}$ .

Prove(crs,  $\{x_j\}_{j\in[k]}$ ,  $\{w_j\}_{j\in[k]}$ )  $\to \pi$ : A PPT algorithm takes in the crs, the batch of statements  $x_1, \ldots, x_k$  with corresponding witnesses  $w_1, \ldots, w_k$  and produces proof  $\pi$ .

Verify(crs,  $\{x_j\}_{j\in[k]}, \pi$ )  $\to 0/1$ : A PPT algorithm that verifies the proof  $\pi$  generated by Prove and outputs 1 if the proof is valid (0 if not).

Extract(td,  $\{x_j\}_{j\in[k]}, \pi$ )  $\to w^*$ : A PPT algorithm that takes in a trapdoor td, an index  $i^* \in [k]$ , proof  $\pi$ , and extracts the corresponding witness  $w^*$  at the index specified by  $i^*$  during crs generation.

<span id="page-19-1"></span>**Definition 3.6** (seBARG). A somewhere-extractable batch argument scheme (seBARG) for  $\mathcal{L}$  is required to satisfy the following properties:

**Efficiency**: The length of the crs and proof  $\pi$  is at most  $poly(\lambda, \log k, n, m)$ , where m is the length of a witness.

**Completeness:** For any  $k = k(\lambda)$  instances  $x_1, \ldots, x_k$  and witnesses  $w_1, \ldots, w_k$  of length m s.t  $(x_i, w_i)_{i \in [k]} \in \mathcal{R}$ , any index  $i^* \in [k]$ , any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , and any polynomial  $n = n(\lambda)$ , we must have

$$\Pr\left[ \quad \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \{x_j\}_{j \in [k]}, \pi) = 1 \quad : \quad \frac{(\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^n, i^*)}{\pi \leftarrow \mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{crs}, \{x_j\}_{j \in [k]}, \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]})} \quad \right] = 1$$

**Index Hiding**: For any polynomial time adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$ , s.t  $\forall \lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  we have,

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} i_0, i_1 \in [k], \ b \leftarrow \{0, 1\}, \\ b' = b \ : \ (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^n, i_b) \\ b' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{crs}) \end{array}\right] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

**Somewhere Argument of Knowledge**: For any polynomial time adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  and for any index  $i^* \in [k]$ , the witness extracted at  $i^*$  using the trapdoor td is a valid witness, i.e there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} i^* \leftarrow \mathcal{A} \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{crs}, \{x_j\}_{j \in [k]}, \pi) = 1 \ \land \\ (x_{i^*}, w^*) \notin \mathcal{R} \end{array} \right. : \begin{array}{c} i^* \leftarrow \mathcal{A} \\ (\mathsf{crs}, \mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^n, i^*) \\ (x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k, \pi) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{crs}) \\ w^* \leftarrow \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td}, i^*, \pi) \end{array} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

{20}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-20-0"></span>3.4 Fully Homomorphic Encryption

A Fully Homomorphic Encryption (FHE) scheme allows for computation to be performed on encrypted data. Let  $\mathcal{C} = \{C_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  with n input wires be an ensemble of circuits. A FHE-scheme for  $\mathcal{C}$  is a tuple (KeyGen, Eval, Enc, Dec), where each algorithm is defined as follows

 $\mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}) \to (\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk})$ : A PPT algorithm that takes a security parameter  $\lambda$  and outputs a public/secret key pair.

 $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk},x) \to \mathsf{ct}$ : A PPT algorithm that takes message  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$  and produces ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}$ .

 $\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}, C, \mathsf{ct}) \to \hat{\mathsf{ct}}$ : A PPT algorithm that takes ciphertext  $\hat{\mathsf{ct}}$  and outputs ciphertext  $\hat{\mathsf{ct}}$ 

 $\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk},\mathsf{ct}) \to x$ : A deterministic algorithm that takes the secret key  $\mathsf{sk}$  and a ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}$  to output a corresponding message x.

<span id="page-20-1"></span>**Definition 3.7** (FHE Properties). Let  $\mathcal{C} = \{C_{\lambda}\}_{{\lambda} \in \mathbb{N}}$  be an ensemble of circuits. A FHE scheme (KeyGen, Eval, Enc, Dec) for  $\mathcal{C}$  satisfies the following properties

**Correctness**: For any circuit  $C \in \mathcal{C}$ , for every  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$ , for any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  we must have

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^\lambda) \\ C(x) = \alpha \ : \ c \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk},x) \\ c' \leftarrow \mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk},C,c) \\ \alpha \leftarrow \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{sk},c') \end{array}\right] = 1$$

**Compactness**: The size of  $\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{pk}, C, \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk}, x))$  is fixed  $\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)$  and is independent of |C|, |x| for all circuits  $C \in \mathcal{C}$ .

**Semantic Security**: For every poly-size adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  and for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}) \\ b' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{ct}) \\ \land b = b' \end{array} : \begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}) \\ \vdots \\ b \in_R \{0,1\} \\ \mathsf{ct} \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{pk},x_b) \end{array}\right] \leq \frac{1}{2} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

**Theorem 3.8** (Post-quantum security of FHE [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). Under circular-secure variants of the learning with errors assumption, there exists a fully homomorphic encryption scheme for all polynomial-time computable functions. If the circular LWE variant is post-quantum, then so is the FHE scheme.

Under the standard LWE assumption, there exists a FHE scheme for all polynomial-size circuits of depth  $d(\lambda)$ , where the scheme has compactness  $poly(\lambda, d)$ .

{21}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-21-0"></span>3.5 Puncturable Pseudo-random Functions (PPRF)

<span id="page-21-3"></span>Definition 3.9 (Puncturable PRFs [\[BW13,](#page-77-9) [BGI14,](#page-76-8) [KPTZ13,](#page-79-10) ?]). A puncturable PRF family is a family of functions

$$\mathcal{PF} = \left\{ F_{\lambda,s} : \{0,1\}^{\nu(\lambda)} \to \{0,1\}^{\mu(\lambda)} \right\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, \ s \in \{0,1\}^{\ell(\lambda)}}$$

with the associated tuple of algorithms PF(Eval, Puncture, PuncEval) satisfying

- 1. For all x ∈ {0, 1} <sup>ν</sup>(λ) and all s ∈ {0, 1} ℓ(λ) , PF.Eval(s, x) = Fλ,s(x).
- 2. For all distinct x, x′ ∈ {0, 1} <sup>ν</sup>(λ) and all s ∈ {0, 1} ℓ(λ) ,

$$\mathcal{PF}$$
.PuncEval $(\mathcal{PF}$ .Puncture $(s, x), x') = \mathcal{PF}$ .Eval $(s, x')$ 

For ease of notation, we will use Fs(x) to denote PF.Eval(s, x), and we write s{x} to denote PF.Puncture(s, x). We define the security of PPRFs as follows

Definition 3.10 (Security of PPRFs). PF is said to be (s, δ) secure if for every {x (λ) ∈ {0, 1} <sup>ν</sup>(λ)}λ∈N, the following two distribution ensembles (indexed by λ) are δ(λ) indistinguishable to circuits of size s(λ):

$$\left(S\{x^{(\lambda)}\}, F_S(x^{(\lambda)})\right)$$
 and  $\left(S\{x^{(\lambda)}\}, U\right)$ 

where S ← {0, 1} <sup>ℓ</sup>(λ) and U ← {0, 1} µ(λ) .

Theorem 3.11 ([\[BW13,](#page-77-9) [BGI14,](#page-76-8) [KPTZ13,](#page-79-10) ?, [GGM86\]](#page-78-8)). If { polynomially secure, subexponentially secure } one-way functions exist, then for all µ : N → N (with 1ν(λ) polynomial computable from 1<sup>ν</sup> ), and all δ : N → [0, 1] with δ(ν) ≤ 2 −poly(ν) , there are polynomials ℓ(λ), ν(λ) and a { polynomially, 1 <sup>δ</sup>(ν(λ)) , δ(ν(λ)) -secure } puncturable PRF family

$$\mathcal{PF}_{\mu} = \left\{ F_{\lambda,s} : \{0,1\}^{\nu(\lambda)} \to \{0,1\}^{\mu(\nu(\lambda))} \right\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, \ s \in \{0,1\}^{\ell(\lambda)}}$$

## <span id="page-21-1"></span>3.6 Indistinguishability Obfuscation (iO)

<span id="page-21-2"></span>Definition 3.12 (iO). An indistinguishability obfuscator is an algorithm iO that takes input a circuit C and satisfies the following properties

1. Functional Equivalence: for any polynomial size circuit C : {0, 1} <sup>n</sup> → {0, 1} <sup>m</sup> and any input x ∈ {0, 1}) n , we have

$$i\mathcal{O}(C)(x) = C(x)$$

2. Security: For any pair of functionally equivalent circuits (C0, C1), we have

$$i\mathcal{O}(C_0) \approx_c i\mathcal{O}(C_1)$$

Remark 3.13. We use the candidates for iO that are plausibly post-quantum secure [\[BGMZ18,](#page-76-9) [CVW18,](#page-78-9) [BDGM20,](#page-76-10) [WW21,](#page-80-11) [GP21,](#page-78-10) [DQV](#page-78-11)+21]. We also use the perfect completeness variant [\[BV17\]](#page-77-10) to simplify our analysis.

{22}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-22-0"></span>3.7 Differing Inputs Obfuscation (diO)

There are various notions of distributional Differing Inputs Obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$  for classical adversaries in the literature [BCP14, ABG<sup>+</sup>13]. Previous attempts at defining  $di\mathcal{O}$  for the quantum setting were done in [GZ20, CLLZ21] on the closely related topic of extractable witness encryption. We will be considering the definition presented in [Zha23] as it compiles well with our constructions. We define  $di\mathcal{O}$  as follows

<span id="page-22-1"></span>**Definition 3.14** (Post-quantum Differing Inputs Obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$ ). A post quantum Differing Inputs Obfuscator is a PPT algorithm  $di\mathcal{O}$  s.t

- 1.  $di\mathcal{O}(1^{\lambda}, C)$  is equivalent to C with overwhelming probability over the randomness of  $di\mathcal{O}$ .
- 2. For every QPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  and sampler  $\mathcal{S}$ , there exists a QPT extractor  $\mathcal{E}$  s.t for every inverse polynomial function  $\epsilon = \epsilon(\lambda)$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Fail}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon}\right] < \mathsf{negl}(\lambda), \qquad \Pr\left[\mathsf{Live}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon} \land \neg \, \mathsf{Success}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon}\right] < \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where  $\mathsf{Fail}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon}$ ,  $\mathsf{Live}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon}$ ,  $\mathsf{Success}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}_{\epsilon}$  are defined w.r.t a security game as defined below

**Definition 3.15** (Security Game for  $di\mathcal{O}$ ). Let  $m(\lambda), n(\lambda), s(\lambda)$  be polynomials. Consider any QPT sampler  $\mathcal{S}(1^{\lambda})$  which samples a pair of classical circuits  $C_0, C_1$  of size  $s(\lambda)$ , input length  $n(\lambda)$ , and output length  $m(\lambda)$ , and an auxiliary quantum side information aux. Consider any quantum distinguishing adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ . Consider any distinguishing operation on aux as follows:

- 1. Choose a random bit  $b \leftarrow \{0, 1\}$
- 2. Run  $\hat{C} \leftarrow \operatorname{di}\mathcal{O}(1^{\lambda}, C_b)$  to get the obfuscation of a random choice between  $C_0$  and  $C_1$ .
- 3. Run  $b' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\hat{C}, C_0, C_1, \mathsf{aux})$
