- 4. Output 1 iff b' = b, otherwise output 0

Let  $\mathcal{M}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}} = \{M_0, M_1\}$  be the POVM given by this operation. [Zha23] uses a projective implementation of  $\mathcal{M}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}} = \{M'_p\}_p$  where each  $M'_p$  corresponds to the probability distribution on  $\{0, 1\}$  that is 1 with probability p. Consider an extractor  $\mathcal{E}$  given inputs  $(C_0, C_1, \mathsf{aux}, 1^{1/\epsilon})$  for any inverse polynomial function  $\epsilon(\lambda)$ . The security game for  $\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}$  characterized by  $\mathcal{S}, \mathcal{A}, \mathcal{E}$  as well as the function  $\epsilon(\lambda)$  is defined as follows:

- 1. Run  $(C_0, C_1, \mathsf{aux}) \leftarrow \mathcal{S}(1^{\lambda})$ . Let  $\mathcal{H}$  be the register containing  $\mathsf{aux}$ .
- 2. Apply the projective implementation of  $\mathcal{M}^{\mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}}$  to  $\mathcal{H}$ , resulting in probability p.
- 3. If  $p > \frac{1}{2} + \epsilon(\lambda)$ , the event  $\mathsf{Live}^{\mathsf{di}}_{\epsilon}\mathcal{O}$  has occured.
- 4. Run  $\mathcal{E}$  on  $\mathcal{H}$ , obtaining an input x or the symbol  $\perp$ .
- 5. If  $x \neq \perp$  but  $C_0(x) = C_1(x)$ , the event  $\mathsf{Fail}^{\mathsf{di}}_{\epsilon} \mathcal{O}$  has occured.
- 6. If  $x \neq \perp$  and  $C_0(x) \neq C_1(x)$ , the event  $Success_{\epsilon}^{di}\mathcal{O}$  has occurred.

{23}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-23-0"></span>3.8 State Preserving Arguments of Knowledge

To establish the soundness of our public-coin Monotone policy BARG and similar constructions from [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22, [GKNV24,](#page-78-0) [MNZ24\]](#page-80-4), a full extraction of the witness is required. Traditionally, extraction involves rewinding a successful attacker to retrieve a claimed witness, but with quantum states, rewinding risks irreversibly altering the prover state, resulting in flawed extraction. However, a recent development in [\[LMS22\]](#page-79-7) introduces a state-preserving argument of knowledge, enabling complete extraction without compromising the quantum state. We define it as follows:

<span id="page-23-2"></span>Definition 3.16 (ϵ-State Preserving Arguments of Knowledge AoK). A publicly verifiable argument system Π for an NP language L (with the corresponding relation RL) is an ϵ-state preserving argument-of-knowledge if it satisfies the following properties

Succinctness: When invoked on a security parameter λ ∈ N and an instance n and a relation that is decidable in time T, the communication complexity of the protocol is poly(λ, log T). The computational complexity of the verifier is poly(λ, log T) + O˜(n).

ϵ-State-Preserving Extraction: There exists an extractor E(·) (x, ϵ) with the following properties.

- Efficiency: E(·) (x, ϵ) runs in time poly(λ, n, 1/ϵ) as a quantum oracle algorithm (with the ability to apply controlled U-gates given an oracle U(·)). It outputs a classical transcript ˜τ and a classical string w
- State Preserving: Let |ψ⟩ ∈ A ⊗ I be any poly(λ)-qubit pure state and let ρ = TrA(|ψ⟩) ∈ D(I). Consider the following two games:
  - Game 0 (Real): Generate a transcript τ by running P ⋆ (ρI, x) with the honest verifier V. Output τ along with the residual state on A ⊗ I.
  - Game 1 (Simulated): Generate a transcript-witness pair (˜τ, w) ← E<sup>P</sup> <sup>⋆</sup>(ρ<sup>I</sup> ,x) . Output τ˜ and the residual state on A ⊗ I

The output distributions of Game 0 and Game 1 are computationally ϵ-indistinguishable to any quantum distinguisher

• Extraction Correctness: For any P <sup>⋆</sup> as above, the probability that ˜τ is an accepting transcript but RL(x, w) = 0 is at-most ϵ + negl(λ)

Theorem 3.17 ([\[LMS22\]](#page-79-7)). Assuming the post-quantum poly(λ, 1/ϵ) hardness of Learning with Errors LWE, there exists a (4 message, public coin) ϵ-state preserving succinct argument of knowledge.

## <span id="page-23-1"></span>3.9 Classical Verification for QMA

In this subsection, we define the class QMA and the building blocks required to construct a classical verification protocol. This includes the Mahadev measurement protocol [\[Mah18\]](#page-80-8) and the noninteractive post hoc verification protocol [\[FHM18\]](#page-78-3) (referred to as the FHM) protocol.

## 3.9.1 The Complexity Class QMA

The Quantum Merlin-Arthur (QMA) complexity class can be roughly construed as the quantum analogue of NP. QMA consists of decision problems L = (Lyes,Lno) between a computationally 

{24}------------------------------------------------

powerful quantum prover: Merlin and a less computationally powerful quantum verifier: Arthur. Let the poly-sized quantum circuits corresponding to Merlin and Arthur be P and V, respectively. For any statement  $x \in \{0,1\}^n$  in the language  $\mathcal{L}$  and quantum proof  $|\phi\rangle$  on p(n) qubits, we must have:

**Completeness**: For all  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{yes}$ , there exists a quantum witness  $|\psi\rangle$  on at most p(n) qubits

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{V}(\left|\phi\right\rangle,x) = 1:\left|\phi\right\rangle \leftarrow \mathsf{P}(\left|\psi\right\rangle,x)\right] \geq 2/3$$

**Soundness**: For all  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{no}$  and all quantum states  $|\psi\rangle$  on at most p(n) qubits,

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{V}(\ket{\phi},x)=1:\ket{\phi}\leftarrow\mathsf{P}(\ket{\psi},x)\right]\leq 1/3$$

#### 3.9.2 Interactive Arguments for QMA

**Definition 3.18** ([BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). An interactive argument (P, V) for a language  $\mathcal{L} = (\mathcal{L}_{yes}, \mathcal{L}_{no}) \in$  QMA with relation  $\mathcal{R}(x)$  is a (classical) 2-party interactive protocol between a QPT prover P and a PPT verifier V, with the following completeness and soundness guarantees:

**Completeness**: For all  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{yes}$ , for all  $|\phi\rangle \in \mathcal{R}(x)$ , for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and polynomials  $k = k(\lambda)$ , there exists a negligible function  $negl(\cdot)$  such that:

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Out}\left\langle\mathsf{P}(\ket{\phi}^{\otimes k(\lambda)},x),\mathsf{V}(x)\right\rangle=1\right]\geq 1-\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where  $\langle P, V \rangle$  denotes the process of interaction between P and V

**Soundness**: For all  $x \in \mathcal{L}_{no}$  and all non-uniform QPT provers  $P^*$ , we must have  $\forall \lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Out}\left\langle\mathsf{P}^*(x),\mathsf{V}(x)\right\rangle=1\right]\leq\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

#### <span id="page-24-0"></span>3.9.3 Interactive Batch Arguments for QMA

We define Batch arguments for QMA and expect it to satisfy the following properties

**Definition 3.19** (BARG Completeness). An interactive argument (P, V) for a batch language  $\mathcal{L} = \mathcal{L}_1 \times \cdots \times \mathcal{L}_k \in QMA$  with relation  $\mathcal{R}(x)$  satisfies completeness if for any honest prover P, for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and all polynomials  $k = k(\lambda)$ , there exists a negligible function  $negl(\cdot)$  such that:

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Out}\left\langle\mathsf{P}(x),\mathsf{V}(x)\right\rangle=1\ :\ \forall i\in[k]\ x_i\in\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{yes}}\right]\geq 1-\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

<span id="page-24-1"></span>**Definition 3.20** (BARG Semi-adaptive Soundness). An interactive argument (P, V) for a batch language  $\mathcal{L} = \mathcal{L}_1 \times \cdots \times \mathcal{L}_k \in QMA$  with relation  $\mathcal{R}(x)$  is semi-adaptively-sound if for any quantum adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and all polynomials  $k = k(\lambda)$ , there exists an index  $i^* \in [k]$  and there exists a negligible function  $negl(\cdot)$  such that:

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{Out}\left\langle\mathsf{P}^*(x),\mathsf{V}(x)\right\rangle=1\ :\ x_{i^*}\in\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{no}}\right]\leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

{25}------------------------------------------------

#### 3.9.4 Commit-and-Measure Protocols

The notion of commit and measure procotols was first formally introduced in [Mah18]. However, we use the abstraction presented in [BKL<sup>+</sup>22] for our constructions. The protocol allows a classical verifier to obtain measurement results of an N-qubit quantum state  $\sigma$  held by an untrusted quantum-capable prover.

<span id="page-25-0"></span>**Definition 3.21** (Commit-and-Measure Protocol Syntax [BKL $^+$ 22]). An N-qubit commit-and-measure protocol between a quantum polynomial-time prover P = (Com, Open) and a classical probabilistic polynomial-time verifier V = (Gen, Test, Out) has the following syntax.

- 1. The verifier samples  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, C)$ , where  $C : [N] = \{0, 1\}^{\log N} \to \{0, 1\}$  represents a basis vector  $h \in \{0, 1\}^N$ , obtaining public parameters  $\mathsf{pk}$  and secret parameters  $\mathsf{sk}$ . It sends the public parameters  $\mathsf{pk}$  to the verifier.
- 2. The prover computes  $(y, \rho) \leftarrow \mathsf{Com}(\mathsf{pk}, \sigma)$ , obtaining a classical "commitment" string y and a private quantum state  $\rho$ . It sends y to the verifier.
- 3. The verifier samples a random challenge bit  $c \leftarrow \{0,1\}$  and sends c to the prover; c=0 corresponds to a "test round" and c=1 corresponds to a "measurement round".
- 4. The prover computes  $z \in \mathsf{Open}(\boldsymbol{\rho}, c)$ , obtaining a classical string z that it sends to the verifier.
- 5. If c = 0, the verifier computes  $\{\mathsf{acc}, \mathsf{rej}\} \leftarrow \mathsf{Test}(\mathsf{pk}, (y, z))$ .

  If c = 1, the verifier computes  $m \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{sk}, (y, z))$  to obtain a classical string  $m \in \{0, 1\}^N$  of measurement outcomes.

<span id="page-25-1"></span>**Definition 3.22** (Commit-and-Measure Protocol Completeness [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). A commit-and-measure protocol is required to satisfy the two completeness properties.

1. (Test Round Completeness) For all  $C:[N] \to \{0,1\}$  and N-qubit states  $\sigma$ :

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{c} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda},C) \\ \mathsf{Test}(\mathsf{pk},(y,z)) = \mathsf{acc} \ : \ (y,\pmb{\rho}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Com}(\mathsf{pk},\pmb{\sigma}) \\ z \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\pmb{\rho},0) \end{array}\right] = 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

2. (Measurement Round Completeness) For all  $C:[N] \to \{0,1\}$  and N-qubit states  $\sigma$ :

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{ccc} (\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda},C) \\ \mathsf{Out}(\mathsf{sk},(y,z)) = m & : & (y, \pmb{\rho}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Com}(\mathsf{pk},\pmb{\sigma}) \\ & z \leftarrow \mathsf{Open}(\pmb{\rho},1) \end{array}\right] \approx_c M(h,\pmb{\sigma})$$

where  $h \in \{0,1\}^N$  is such that  $h_i = C(i)$  for all  $i \in [N] = \{0,1\}^{\log N}$  and  $M(h, \boldsymbol{\sigma})$  represents the actual measurement output in the basis defined by h.

Now, to state the soundness definition, we first specify some notation.

•  $\mathcal{P}$ : quantum register containing the public parameters pk

{26}------------------------------------------------

- $\mathcal{Y}$ : quantum register containing the classical commitment string y
- $\mathcal{Z}$ : quantum register containing the classical opening string z
- $\bullet$   $\mathcal{I}$ : the prover's initial state and internal working registers

A non-uniform cheating prover can be characterised as follows [BKL<sup>+</sup>22].

$$\mathsf{P}^* = (\boldsymbol{\rho_0}, U_{\mathsf{Com}^*}, U)$$

<span id="page-26-0"></span>**Definition 3.23** (Commit-and-Measure Protocol Soundness [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). There exists an efficient classical algorithm  $\mathsf{SimGen}(1^\lambda)$  and an efficient quantum algorithm  $\mathsf{Extract}^{U,\rho}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk},y)$  with blackbox access to an attacker parameterized by a state  $\rho$  and a unitary U that takes as input classical strings  $(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{sk},y)$ , and satisfies the following property. Consider any non-uniform  $\mathsf{QPT}$  cheating prover  $\mathsf{P}^* = (\rho_0, U_{\mathsf{Com}^*}, U)$  that passes the test round with probability  $1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  for all  $h \in \{0,1\}^N$ . Then, for all  $h \in \{0,1\}^N$  with circuit representation C, the following two distributions are computationally indistinguishable.

#### • Real

- 1. Sample parameters  $(\mathsf{pk}, \mathsf{sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}, C)$ .
- 2. Run the attacker  $\mathsf{P}^*$  on  $\mathsf{pk}$  to obtain the classical commitment string y (i.e. apply  $U_{\mathsf{Com}^*}$  and then measure the register containing y). Denote the post-measurement state as  $\rho \in D(\mathcal{Z} \otimes \mathcal{I})$ , where  $\mathcal{Z}$  corresponds to the registers that will eventually be measured to obtain the prover's final message, and  $\mathcal{I}$  contains all of the other internal registers of the prover.
- 3. Apply the prover's attack unitary U. This yields the state  $\rho' := U \rho_{\mathcal{Z},\mathcal{I}} U^{\dagger}$ . Measure the  $\mathcal{Z}$  register of  $\rho'$  in the Hadamard basis to obtain the prover's opening string z.
- 4. Compute  $m \leftarrow \text{Out}(\mathsf{sk}, (y, z))$  and output m.

#### • Sim

- 1. Sample parameters  $(pk, sk) \leftarrow SimGen(1^{\lambda})$ .
- 2. Run the attacker  $\mathsf{P}^*$  on  $\mathsf{pk}$  to obtain a classical commitment string y (i.e. apply  $U_{\mathsf{Com}^*}$  and then measure the register containing y). Denote the post-measurement state as  $\rho \in D(\mathcal{Z} \otimes \mathcal{I})$ .
- 3. Run Extract  $U, \rho(pk, sk, y) \to \tau$  to obtain an N-qubit state  $\tau$ .
- 4. Measure each qubit of  $\tau$  according to the bases specified by  $h \in \{0,1\}^N$  (i.e., qubit i is measured in the Hadamard basis if  $h_i = 1$  and the standard basis if  $h_i = 0$ ) and output the result.

## 3.9.5 Quantum Commit-Challenge-Response Protocols

<span id="page-26-1"></span>**Definition 3.24** (Quantum Commit-Challenge-Response Protocols [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). Consider any *commit-challenge-measure* protocol between a quantum prover P and a classical verifier V, with the following three phases.

• Commit:  $P(1^{\lambda})$  and  $V(1^{\lambda}; r)$  engage in a (potentially interactive) commitment protocol, where r are the random coins used by V.

{27}------------------------------------------------

- Challenge: V samples a random bit  $b \leftarrow \{0,1\}$  and sends it to P.
- Response: P computes a (classical) response z and sends it to V.

After receiving the response, V accepts or rejects the execution.

We now describe some notations that are relevant for further definitions and discussion.

- Consider any non-uniform QPT prover  $P^*$  and let  $\left|\psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\right\rangle_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{C}}$  be the (purified) state of the prover after interacting with  $V(1^{\lambda};r)$  in the commit phase, where  $\mathcal{C}$  holds the (classical) prover messages output during this phase, and  $\mathcal{A}$  holds the remaining state.
- The remaining strategy of the prover can be descrived by family of unitaries  $\left\{U_{\lambda,0}^{P^*}, U_{\lambda,1}^{P^*}\right\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ , where  $U_{\lambda,0}^{P^*}$  is applied to  $\left|\psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\right\rangle$  if challenge is 0 and  $U_{\lambda,1}^{P^*}$  if challenge is 1. This step is followed by a measurement yielding z.
- Let  $V_{\lambda,r,0}$  (resp.  $V_{\lambda,r,1}$ ) denote the accept/reject predicate applied by the verifier to the prover messages when b=0 (resp. b=1), written as a projection to be applied to the registers holding the prover messages. We define the following projectors on  $\mathcal{A} \otimes \mathcal{C}$ :

$$\Pi_{\lambda,r,0}^{P^*} := U_{\lambda,0}^{P^*\dagger} V_{\lambda,r,0} U_{\lambda,0}^{P^*}$$

$$\Pi_{\lambda,r,1}^{P^*} := U_{\lambda,1}^{P^*\dagger} V_{\lambda,r,1} U_{\lambda,1}^{P^*}$$

**Definition 3.25** (Computationally Orthogonal Projectors [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]). A commit-challenge-response protocol has computationally orthogonal projectors if for any QPT prover  $P^*$ ,

$$\mathbb{E}_r \Big[ \Big\langle \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*} \Big| \Pi_{\lambda,r,0}^{P^*} \Pi_{\lambda,r,1}^{P^*} \Pi_{\lambda,r,0}^{P^*} \Big| \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*} \Big\rangle \Big] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

<span id="page-27-0"></span>**Lemma 3.26** ([ACGH20]). Consider a *commit-challenge-response* protocol with the following properties.

- 1.  $V_{\lambda,r,0}$  does not depend on r (that is, it is publicly computable given the transcript).
- 2. For any  $P^*$ , if  $\mathbb{E}_r\left[\left\langle \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\middle|\Pi_{\lambda,r,0}^{P^*}\middle|\psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\right\rangle\right]=1-\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , then  $\mathbb{E}_r\left[\left\langle \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\middle|\Pi_{\lambda,r,1}^{P^*}\middle|\psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*}\right\rangle\right]=\mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

Then, the protocol has computationally orthogonal projectors.

<span id="page-27-1"></span>**Theorem 3.27** ([ACGH20]). Consider the  $\lambda$ -fold parallel repetition of any *commit-challenge-response* protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors. The probability that the verifier accepts all  $\lambda$  parallel repetitions of the protocol is  $negl(\lambda)$ .

#### 3.9.6 The local Hamiltonian Problem

**Definition 3.28** (2-local ZX-Hamiltonian Problem [ACGH20, BL08, CM16]). The 2-local ZX-Hamiltonian promise problem  $zx = (zx_{yes}, zx_{no})$ , with parameters  $a, b \in \mathbb{R}$ , b > a, and gap  $(b-a) > poly(n)^{-1}$ , is defined as follows. An instance is a local Hamiltonian

$$H = \sum_{i < j} J_{ij} (X_i X_j + Z_i Z_j)$$

where each  $J_{ij}$  is a real number such that  $\sum_{i < j} |J_{ij}| = 1$  and each  $X_i$  (resp.  $Z_i$ ) is a Pauli X (resp. Pauli Z) gate acting on the  $i^{th}$  qubit. For  $H \in \text{zx}_{yes}$ , the smallest eigenvalue of H is at most a, while if  $H \in \text{zx}_{no}$ , the smallest eigenvalue of H is at least b.

{28}------------------------------------------------

#### 3.9.7 Non-Interactive Post-Hoc Verification

An information-theoretic non-interactive QMA verification protocol is proposed in [\[FHM18\]](#page-78-3) for verifying statements in all languages L = (Lyes,Lno) ∈ QMA. Consider zxa,b, the corresponding instance of the 2-local Hamiltonian Problem.

Definition 3.29 (FHM Protocol [\[FHM18,](#page-78-3) [ACGH20\]](#page-75-10)). The FHM Protocol consists of a QPT algorithm P and a PPT algorithm V such that for a polynomial ℓ(λ) in the time T(λ) required to verify instances of size λ, we have the following.

- P(x, |ϕ⟩) → |π⟩: A QPT algorithm that uses the instance x and the witness state |ϕ⟩ to produce the ℓ(λ) qubit state |π⟩ which functions as a proof for x ∈ Lyes.
- V(x, S, m) → {0, 1}: A PPT algorithm that uses a term S of the Hamiltonian (sampled using a particular distribution), corresponding measurement results m to either accept or reject the instance at hand.

We direct the reader to [\[ACGH20\]](#page-75-10) for complete details. A high-level execution of this protocol proceeds as follows. The prover prepares the ground state of the 2-local Hamiltonian at hand using the P algorithm and sends it to the verifier. The verifier samples a Hamiltonian term S from a particular problem-dependent distribution π. Subsequently, the verifier creates a basis string h such that if the observable for i-th qubit in S is Z (resp. X), then h<sup>i</sup> is 0 (resp. 1). The received state |π⟩ is then measured in the basis determined by h to get the measurement outcome m. Finally, the verifier outputs the result of V(x, S, m). This protocol has an inverse polynomial error. Therefore, we make use of an r-fold parallel repetition to achieve negligible soundness error when r = ω( log λ (b−a) <sup>2</sup> ). Moreover, the above protocol uses instance-dependent basis string h; the protocol we describe now also gets around this shortcoming by virtue of some additional repetitions.

<span id="page-28-0"></span>Definition 3.30 (κ-fold FHM Protocol with Instance Independent Setup [\[ACGH20\]](#page-75-10)). The protocol consists of the following algorithms.

- PFHM(x, |ϕ⟩ ⊗κ ) → ρ: A QPT algorithm that uses the instance x and the witness state |ϕ⟩ to produce the ℓ(λ) qubit state ρ (κ copies of the ground state |π⟩) which functions as a proof for x ∈ Lyes.
- VFHM(x, m, h) → {0, 1}: A PPT algorithm that uses the measurement string m of qubits of ρ to either accept or reject the instance at hand. Internally, this algorithm works as follows.
  - 1. Sample κ terms S1, . . . , S<sup>κ</sup> ← π.
  - 2. Parse (h1, . . . , hκ) = h and (m1, . . . , mκ) = m.
  - 3. Form a subset A ⊂ [κ] of consistent copies. A copy i ∈ A means h<sup>i</sup> and S<sup>i</sup> are consistent w.r.t. observables. Note that since we are dealing with 2-local Hamiltonians, about κ/4 copies are expected to be consistent for large enough κ.
  - 4. For each copy i ∈ A, the we set v<sup>i</sup> := V(x, S<sup>i</sup> , mi).
  - 5. Output 1 if Pv<sup>i</sup> ≥ (1 − a − b)|A|/4 otherwise 0.

The protocol proceeds as follows.

• Setup: The verifier samples κ random basis strings h1, . . . , h<sup>κ</sup> ← {0, 1} <sup>κ</sup> uniformly.

{29}------------------------------------------------

- Round 1: The prover creates ρ ← PFHM(x, |ϕ⟩ ⊗κ ) and sends ρ to the verifier.
- Round 2: The verifier measures qubits of ρ in the basis defined by the string h = (h1, . . . , hκ) to get a measurement string m = (m1, . . . , mκ). It then outputs the result of VFHM(x, m, h).

Remark 3.31. As discussed in the above definition, for large enough κ, |A| = κ/4. Moreover, we have already established that to achieve negligible soundness error, we require |A| = r = ω( log λ (b−a) <sup>2</sup> ). Consequently, κ = 4 × ω( log λ (b−a) <sup>2</sup> ). In other words, for Instance Independent Setup, we require a constant number of repetitions of the r-fold FHM protocol.

<span id="page-29-1"></span>Definition 3.32 (κ-fold FHM Protocol Completeness and Soundness [\[FHM18,](#page-78-3) [ACGH20,](#page-75-10) [BKL](#page-76-4)+22]). The FHM protocol has the following security properties.

• Completeness: For all x ∈ Lyes, for all valid witnesses |ϕ⟩ ∈ RL(x) and for all basis choices h ∈ {0, 1} ℓ(λ) , there exists a negligible function negl(·) such that the following holds.

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x,m,h) = 1 : \boldsymbol{\rho} \leftarrow \mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x,|\phi\rangle^{\otimes \kappa})\right] \geq 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where m is the result of measuring qubits of ρ in the basis defined by h.

• Soundness: For every quantum adversary A, for all x ∈ Lno and for all basis choices h ∈ {0, 1} ℓ(λ) , there exists a negligible function negl(·) such that the following holds.

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x,m,h) = 1: \pmb{\rho}^* \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(x)\right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where m is the result of measuring qubits of ρ ∗ in the basis defined by h.

# <span id="page-29-0"></span>4 Interactive Batch Arguments for QMA

In this section, we present a six-round (five-round with setup) interactive batch argument system for QMA from the post-quantum hardness of learning with errors. We note that this is a designatedverifier BARG. More formally, the result can be stated as follows.

Theorem 4.1 (Designated Verifier Interactive Batch Arguments for QMA). Assuming the postquantum hardness of the learning with errors problem (LWE), there is a designated verifier 6-round interactive argument system for any batch of k T-time quantum computations on inputs x1, . . . , x<sup>k</sup> and corresponding N = poly(T) qubit witnesses |ϕ1⟩, . . . , |ϕk⟩, where

- 1. The Prover P is a QPT algorithm that runs in time poly(k, N)
- 2. The Verifier V is a PPT algorithm that runs in time poly(log k, N)
- 3. The length of each message in the protocol is bounded by poly(log k, N)

The Interactive argument satisfies the BARG completeness and semi-adaptive computational soundness as defined in Section [3.9.3](#page-24-0)

{30}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-30-0"></span>4.1 Construction

We start with the BARG construction in [\[BKL](#page-76-4)+22] which makes use of state preserving arguments of knowledge. The insight here is that these arguments of knowledge can be replaced with seBARG to achieve a reduction in the number of rounds down to 6.

#### Ingredients

- 1. Somewhere Extractable BARG or seBARG(Setup, Prove, Verify, Extract)
- 2. Hash Family with Succinct Local Openings HT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify)
- 3. Commit-and-Measure Protocol Maha(Gen, Com, Open,Test, Out) as defined in definition [3.21.](#page-25-0)
- 4. The κ-fold FHM post-hoc verification protocol (PFHM, VFHM) as defined in definition [3.30](#page-28-0) where κ is a polynomial in instance length.

<span id="page-30-1"></span>To simplify the presentation, we make use of an instance verification predicate defined as follows.

Definition 4.2 (Instance Verification Circuit (VerifyInstance)). The circuit VerifyInstance(x, y, z, c, r) essentially runs the following operations using the input.

- 1. Sample a basis string h using randomness r.
- 2. Generate public and secret parameters for the commit-and-measure protocol.

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}) = \mathsf{Maha}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}, h; r)$$

- 3. If c = 0, output the result of Maha.Test(pk, y, z).
- 4. If c = 1, perform the following and output res.

$$\begin{split} u &= \mathsf{Maha.Out}(\mathsf{mh.sk}, y, z) \\ \mathsf{res} &= \mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x, u, h) \end{split}$$

We make use of the following languages.

Definition 4.3 (Knowledge Language). A Knowledge Language Ghk is defined as follows.

$$\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{hk}} = \{(i, \alpha) \mid \exists z, \mathsf{op}_z \text{ such that HT.Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \alpha, i, z, \mathsf{op}_z) = 1\}$$

Additionally, let R<sup>G</sup> denote a relation s.t RG(i, α) = 1 iff (i, α) ∈ Ghk. Let the corresponding circuit encoding this relation be CG.

Definition 4.4 (Knowledge Language with Instance Verification). A Knowledge Language with Instance Verification Khk is defined as follows.

$$\begin{split} \mathcal{K}_{\mathsf{hk}} = \{ (i, x, \alpha, \beta, c, r) \mid \exists y, \mathsf{op}_y \text{ s.t. HT.Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \alpha, i, y, \mathsf{op}_y) = 1 \\ & \wedge \exists z, \mathsf{op}_z \text{ s.t. HT.Verify}(\mathsf{hk}, \beta, i, z, \mathsf{op}_z) = 1 \\ & \wedge \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x, y, z, c, r) = 1 \} \end{split}$$

Additionally, let R<sup>K</sup> denote a relation s.t RK(i, x, α, β, c, r) = 1 iff (i, x, α, β, c, r) ∈ Khk. Let the corresponding circuit encoding this relation be CK.

{31}------------------------------------------------

## Protocol Specification

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 k , 1 <sup>N</sup> , j<sup>∗</sup> ) → (crs, vk)
  - Setup the commit-and-measure protocol Maha. Sample randomness r \$← {0, 1} <sup>λ</sup> and subsequently sample the basis string h using the randomness r.

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}) = \mathsf{Maha}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}; r)$$

– Setup HT as follows.

$$\mathsf{ht}.\mathsf{hk} \leftarrow \mathsf{HT}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^k,1^\lambda)$$

– Generate (sB.crs<sup>i</sup> ,sB.tdi) corresponding to seBARG<sup>i</sup> for i ∈ [2] and any random index j ∈ [k] as follows.

$$\begin{split} &(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.td}_1) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}|}, j) \\ &(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \mathsf{sB.td}_2) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{K}}|}, j) \end{split}$$

– Outputs the following.

$$\mathsf{crs} = (\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{ht.hk}, \mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.crs}_2)$$
  
 $\mathsf{vk} = (\mathsf{mh.sk}, r)$ 

- Prove(crs, {xj}j∈[k] , {|ϕ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ ⊗κ }j∈[k] ): Here, the quantum state |ϕi⟩ is the quantum witness to the statement x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes for L ∈ QMA.
  - Round 1: Perform the following and send (ˆy, πy) to the verifier.

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{ht.hk}, \mathsf{mh.pk}) &= \mathsf{crs} \\ &\forall j, \ |\psi_j\rangle \leftarrow \mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_j, |\phi_j\rangle^{\otimes \kappa}) \\ &\forall j, \ (y_j, |\mathsf{st}_j\rangle) \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Com}(\mathsf{mh.pk}, |\psi_j\rangle) \\ &\hat{y} &= \mathsf{HT.Hash}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \\ &\forall j, \ \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}} &= \mathsf{HT.Open}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]}, j) \\ &\pi_y \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \{(j, \hat{y})\}_{j \in [k]}, \{y_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}}\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{split}$$

– Round 3: Receive a bit c ∈ {0, 1}. Perform the following and send (ˆz, πz) to the verifier.

$$\begin{split} \forall j, \ z_j \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Open}(\ket{\mathsf{st}_j}, c) \\ \hat{z} &= \mathsf{HT.Hash}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \{z_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \\ \forall j, \ \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{z}} &= \mathsf{HT.Open}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \{z_j\}_{j \in [k]}, j) \\ \pi_z \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \{(j, \hat{z})\}_{j \in [k]}, \{z_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{z}}\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{split}$$

– Round 5: Receive a string r. Parse sB.crs<sup>2</sup> from crs. Perform the following and send π<sup>f</sup> to the verifier.

$$\begin{split} \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]} &= \{(y_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}}, z_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{z}})\}_{j \in [k]} \\ &\pi_f \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \{(j, x_j, \hat{y}, \hat{z}, c, r)\}_{j \in [k]}, \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{split}$$

{32}------------------------------------------------

- Verify(crs, vk, {xj}j∈[k] , πy, πz, π<sup>f</sup> ): Works as follows under normal (honest) execution.
  - Round 2: Receive (ˆy, πy). Parse sB.crs<sup>1</sup> from crs. Check if

$$\mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\{(j,\hat{y})\}_{j\in[k]},\pi_y) = 1$$

If yes, sample bit c and send it to the prover. If no, reject.

– Round 4: Receive (ˆz, πz) from the prover. Check if

$$\mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\{(j,\hat{z})\}_{j\in[k]},\pi_z)=1$$

If yes, parse vk to get r and send r to the prover. Otherwise, reject.

– Final Verification: Receive π<sup>f</sup> from the prover. Parse sB.crs<sup>2</sup> from crs. Accept if

$$\mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \{(j, x_j, \hat{y}, \hat{z}, c, r)\}_{j \in [k]}, \pi_f) = 1$$

Otherwise, reject.

Remark 4.5. We use P to denote the above protocol and P<sup>λ</sup> to denote the λ-fold repetition of P.

## <span id="page-32-0"></span>4.2 Security Analysis

Completeness Completeness of P follows immediately from the completeness properties of the underlying building blocks. If the instances x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes for all i ∈ [k], we must have, by the completeness properties of the Commit and Measure protocol (Definition [3.22\)](#page-25-1) and the FHM protocol (Definition [3.32\)](#page-29-1), that VerifyInstance(x<sup>i</sup> , ·, ·, ·, ·) = 1 with non-negligible probability. By the completeness of seBARGs (Definition [3.6\)](#page-19-1), we must have the proofs πy, πz, π<sup>f</sup> always accepted for their respective languages Ghk and Khk. Therefore, the overall protocol accepts with a non-negligible probability if x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes for all i ∈ [k]. Furthermore, completeness of P<sup>λ</sup> then follows using a union-bound argument.

Round Complexity We observe that P and (therefore) P<sup>λ</sup> both have 5 rounds with setup. Alternatively, the verifier can do the setup and crs can be sent to the prover as the verifier's first message to get a 6 round interactive BARG for QMA.

Soundness The protocol P<sup>λ</sup> satisfies the notion of semi-adaptive computational soundness as defined in Definition [3.20.](#page-24-1) We now provide the soundness proof.

Theorem 4.6. Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure protocol (Definition [3.23\)](#page-26-0) and the FHM protocol (Definition [3.32\)](#page-29-1), collision resistance w.r.t. opening of the hash tree family HT, and semi-adaptive somewhere computational soundness of seBARG, the protocol P<sup>λ</sup> satisfies semi-adaptive somewhere computational soundness as defined in definition [3.20.](#page-24-1)

Proof. The proof strategy is as follows. We note that P is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol as per Definition [3.24.](#page-26-1) For semi-adaptive, we first receive an index i <sup>∗</sup> ∈ [k] from a QPT attacker A. We begin by setting a bunch of hybrid experiments and show that the attacker's advantage has changed negligibly. Moreover, we note that the final hybrid satisfies the conditions of Lemma [3.26](#page-27-0) through the soundness properties of the Commit and Measure protocol and FHM protocol. Thus, a parallel repetition of the final hybrid must have negligible soundness through Theorem [3.27.](#page-27-1) Since the protocol P and the final hybrid have negligibly close advantage, it must be the case that a λ-fold repetition of P must have negligible soundness.

{33}------------------------------------------------

**Hybrid 0**: Normal execution of the protocol

**Hybrid 1**: Same as previous execution except that we now enable extraction on index  $i^*$  (instead of a random index j)

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}|}, i^\star) \\ (\mathsf{sB.crs}_2^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{K}}|}, i^\star)$$

**Hybrid 2**: We now update the protocol by making the following extractions at appropriate places on the verifier's end.

$$\begin{aligned} y'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}'^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star} \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star, \{(i,\hat{y})\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_y) \\ z'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}'^{\hat{z}}_{i^\star} \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star, \{(i,\hat{z})\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_z) \\ y^\star_{i^\star}, z^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\hat{z}}_{i^\star} \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star, \{(i,x_i,\hat{y},\hat{z},c,r)\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_f) \end{aligned}$$

In each round, reject if the extracted witnesses aren't valid by checking

$$\begin{aligned} &\mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{y}, i^\star, y'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}'^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star}) = \mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{y}, i^\star, y^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star}) = 1 \\ &\mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{z}, i^\star, z'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}'^{\hat{z}}_{i^\star}) = \mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{z}, i^\star, z^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\hat{z}}_{i^\star}) = 1 \\ &\mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_{i^\star}, y'_{i^\star}, z'_{i^\star}, c, r) = \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_{i^\star}, y^\star_{i^\star}, z^\star_{i^\star}, c, r) = 1 \end{aligned}$$

**Hybrid 3**: Same as previous execution except that now we add the following additional check in the final round of the protocol. We reject if the following are not true.

$$y_{i^{\star}}^{\star} = y_{i^{\star}}^{\prime}, \qquad z_{i^{\star}}^{\star} = z_{i^{\star}}^{\prime}$$

We now prove that the attacker's advantage changes negligibly through the sequence of hybrids assuming the soundness properties of the cryptographic building blocks being used. Subsequently, we show that **Hybrid 3** is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol that has orthogonal projectors. We then show that since the attacker's advantage has changed negligibly from **Hybrid 0** to **Hybrid 3**,  $\mathfrak{P}^{\lambda}$  cannot have non-negligible soundness. To begin, let us assume that the attacker's advantage in breaking the semi-adaptive somewhere computational soundness property of **Hybrid 0** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ . We wish to show that  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$  is a negligible function.

<span id="page-33-0"></span>**Lemma 4.7.** Assuming seBARG satisfies the index hiding property, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**.

*Proof.* Let us assume that the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 1** has a non-negligible advantage over an attacker in **Hybrid 0**. We can construct a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  to break the index-hiding property of seBARGs using  $\mathcal{A}$ . Let  $\mathcal{C}$  be the challenger for the index-hiding property for seBARG<sub>1</sub>. We perform the following operations

$$i^{\star} \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{k}), \quad i \leftarrow \mathcal{B}(1^{\lambda}, 1^{k})$$
$$(\mathsf{crs}') \leftarrow \mathcal{C}(i, i^{\star})$$
$$(\pi'_{y}, \pi'_{z}) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{crs}')$$

{34}------------------------------------------------

We observe that if the index i was used to generate the  $\operatorname{crs}'$ , we are operating in  $\operatorname{Hybrid} 0$ . If the proofs  $(\pi'_y, \pi'_z)$  are accepted, then  $\mathcal{B}$  can guess that the  $\operatorname{crs}'$  was generated using index  $i^*$  rather than i, which contradicts the index-hiding property of seBARG (Definition 3.6). We can extend the same proof to show that the attacker cannot distinguish the  $\operatorname{crs}$  generated for index  $i^*$  for seBARG<sub>2</sub>. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_1(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in  $\operatorname{Hybrid} 1$  is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-34-0"></span>**Lemma 4.8.** Assuming seBARG satisfies the somewhere argument of knowledge property and HT satisfies opening completeness, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2**.

*Proof.* For contradiction, assume there is a non-negligible difference in the attacker's advantages in the two hybrids. We can design a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that uses the QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  to break the **Somewhere Argument of Knowledge** property of seBARGs. Let  $\mathcal{C}$  be the seBARG challenger for the language  $\mathcal{G}_{hk}$ . We perform the following operations:

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{sB.crs}^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}^\star) &\leftarrow \mathcal{C}(i^\star) \\ &\qquad \qquad (\hat{y}^\star, \pi_y^\star) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{crs}^\star) \\ &\qquad \qquad (y_{i^\star}^\star, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\hat{y}^\star}) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG.Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}^\star, i^\star, \pi_y^\star) \end{split}$$

 $\mathcal{B}$  forwards the value of  $\hat{y}^*$  to  $\mathcal{C}$  along with  $\pi_y$ . By the definition of  $\mathcal{G}_{hk}$ , the Challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  performs the check

$$\mathsf{seBARG.Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}^\star, \{(i, \hat{y}^\star)\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_y^\star) = 1 \ \land \ \mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{y}^\star, i^\star, y_{i^\star}^\star, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\hat{y}^\star}) = 1$$

Observe that the solitary check for the validity of the proof  $\pi_y^*$  operates in **Hybrid 1**, while the full check operates in **Hybrid 2**. We also have  $\mathcal{C}$  rejecting whenever  $\mathcal{B}$  rejects. Assuming opening completeness of HT (Definition 3.1), we must have that  $(y_{i^*}^*, \mathsf{op}_{i^*}^{\hat{y}^*})$  is not a valid witness to the language  $\mathcal{G}_{\mathsf{hk}}$  whenever  $\mathcal{B}$  rejects. This contradicts the **Somewhere Argument of Knowledge** property of seBARGs (Definition 3.6). The same proof can be extended to the proofs  $\pi_z$  and  $\pi_f$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_2(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 2** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-34-1"></span>**Lemma 4.9.** Assuming the collision resistance of the hash function used in HT with respect to the openings and hashed values, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3**.

*Proof.* A non-negligible advantage across these two hybrids implies that one of  $y_{i^*}^* \neq y_{i^*}'$  or  $z_{i^*}^* \neq z_{i^*}'$  is true. This contradicts the collision resistance of the hash function used in HT with respect to the openings and values. Therefore, we can quantify the advantage gain of  $\mathcal{A}$  using a negligible function  $\mu_3(\lambda)$  as  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-34-2"></span>We take a break from the hybrid discussions and show that the protocol in **Hybrid 3**, hence referred to as  $\mathfrak{P}'$ , must have computationally orthogonal projectors. This result is crucial for us to show that the starting protocol  $\mathfrak{P}$  also satisfies this property and thus has negligible soundness (through Theorem 3.27).

{35}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 4.10.** Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure Protocol (Maha) and the FHM protocol, **Hybrid 3** is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors.

Proof. To show this, we consider the two conditions stated in lemma 3.26. Condition 1 follows from the structure of the Verifylnstance predicate. For Condition 2, we consider a non-uniform QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}^*$  which passes the test round with probability  $1 - \text{negl}(\lambda)$ . Let a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  for the instance  $x_{i^*}$  be given access to  $\mathcal{A}^*(|\rho_0\rangle, U_{\text{Com}^*}, U)$ . We let a challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  generate the (mh.pk, mh.sk) pair and send mh.pk to  $\mathcal{B}$ . We run the following operations to get the Sim distribution

$$\begin{aligned} (\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}) &\leftarrow \mathcal{B}.\mathsf{SimGen}(1^\lambda) \\ y' &\leftarrow \mathcal{A}^\star(\mathsf{mh.pk}) \\ |\tau\rangle &\leftarrow \mathsf{Extract}^{\mathcal{A}^\star}(\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}, y') \end{aligned}$$

Let m' be the measurement of  $|\tau\rangle$  in the basis specified by mh.pk. The reduction algorithm runs  $V_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_{i^*}, m', h)$ . For the **Real** distribution, the reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  uses  $\mathcal{A}^*$  to output (y, z) using the mh.pk sent by the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$ . If the verifier accepts and the local FHM verification check doesn't, then  $\mathcal{B}$  differentiates between the **Real** and **Sim** experiments which contradicts the soundness of the Commit and Measure Protocol as in Definition 3.23.

Based on the above, we conclude that there exists a state  $|\psi'_{i^*}\rangle$  such that the probability with which the verifier accepts in the measurement round is  $\operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$ -close to the probability that  $V_{\mathsf{FHM}}$  accepts. Moreover, by the soundness of the FHM Protocol, since  $x_{i^*} \in \mathcal{L}_{no}$  it must be the case that:

$$\Pr\left[\mathsf{V}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_{i^*}, m, h) = 1\right] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where m lies in the distribution of the measurement outcomes of  $|\psi'_{i^*}\rangle$  corresponding to basis string  $h = (h_1, \ldots, h_{\kappa})$  consisting of basis choices for each copy. Consequently, we must have:

$$\mathbb{E}_r \Big[ \Big\langle \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*} \Big| \Pi_{\lambda,r,1}^{P^*} \Big| \psi_{\lambda,r}^{P^*} \Big\rangle \Big] = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

In conclusion, we have shown that both conditions required for lemma 3.26 hold and as a result **Hybrid 3** has computationally orthogonal projectors.

<span id="page-35-0"></span>**Lemma 4.11.** The  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of **Hybrid 3**, i.e  $(\mathfrak{P}')^{\lambda}$ , has negligible soundness.

*Proof.* This follows from Theorem 3.27.

<span id="page-35-1"></span>**Lemma 4.12.** The  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{P}$ , i.e  $\mathfrak{P}^{\lambda}$ , has negligible soundness.

*Proof.* We started with a QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  who has non-negligible soundness advantage  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ . Through Lemma 4.7, Lemma 4.8, and Lemma 4.9 we have shown that for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  for instance  $i^*$  has at least  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  chance of success in breaking the soundness of  $\mathfrak{P}'$ , where,

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) = (\mu_1 + \mu_2 + \mu_3)(\lambda)$$

{36}------------------------------------------------

A λ-fold independent parallel repetition of P has advantage (ε(λ))<sup>λ</sup> . Thus, the attacker A has the following advantage for the λ-fold parallel repetition of P′

$$\prod_{\lambda} \left( \varepsilon(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \right) \sim \left( \varepsilon(\lambda) \right)^{\lambda} - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

which is non-negligible if (ε(λ))<sup>λ</sup> is non-negligible contradicting Lemma [4.11.](#page-35-0) Therefore, we must have ε(λ) = negl(λ). Therefore, we note that P<sup>λ</sup> satisfies the semi-adaptive soundness property for BARGs

# <span id="page-36-0"></span>5 Public Coin Batch Arguments for QMA

In this section, we outline a public coin batch argument system for QMA. The protocol is a public coin except for the verifier's first message, i.e. the setup phase. This property, as we describe in Section [9,](#page-69-0) allows for round collapsing under the Quantum Random Oracle Model. More formally, this result can be stated as follows.

Theorem 5.1 (Designated Verifier Public Coin Interactive Batch Arguments for QMA). Assuming the post-quantum hardness of the learning with errors problem (LWE) and post-quantum fully homomorphic encryption (FHE), there is a designated verifier public coin 4-round interactive argument system for any batch of k T-time quantum computations on inputs x1, . . . , x<sup>k</sup> and corresponding poly(T)-qubit witnesses |ϕ1⟩, . . . , |ϕk⟩, where

- 1. The Prover P is a QPT algorithm that runs in time poly(k, N)
- 2. The Verifier V is a PPT algorithm that runs in time poly(log k, N)
- 3. The first message in the protocol has length poly(k, N) while all other messages have length poly(log k, N).

The public coin protocol satisfies the BARG completeness and semi-adaptive somewhere computational soundness properties as defined in Section [3.9.3](#page-24-0)

## <span id="page-36-1"></span>5.1 FHE based AND Tree Construction

We now take a break from the BARG discussion and present three algorithms that builds an AND tree (ATree) using FHE ciphertexts. These algorithms are useful in ensuring all prover messages after Round 1 (or Setup) of the protocol are succinct with respect to the batch size k. We first provide a definition that sets expectations from each of these algorithms. The high level idea is to bootstrap a FHE based binary tree using logical AND operations starting from ciphertexts encrypting booleans in the leaves. By decrypting the root, one can obtain the AND of all the input ciphertexts. Moreover, the tree structure, inspired from [\[Mer87\]](#page-80-10), can be used to generate and verify succinct openings. Like in software development, we bundle each algorithm under the common name of ATree for a simplified presentation.

<span id="page-36-2"></span>Definition 5.2 (FHE based AND Tree). An FHE based AND tree ATree is a tuple (Build, Open, Verify) of algorithms defined as follows.

{37}------------------------------------------------

- Build(pk, aux, ct)  $\rightarrow$  av: A deterministic poly-time algorithm that takes input n FHE ciphertexts ct =  $\{ct_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ , a set of FHE public keys pk, and some auxiliary information aux and computes the tree root av as the logical AND of all bits encoded by the input ciphertexts.
- Open(pk, aux, ct, j)  $\rightarrow$  op: A deterministic poly time algorithm that outputs an opening op using a set of FHE public keys pk, some auxiliary information aux, the set of ciphertexts ct =  $\{ct_i\}_{i\in[n]}$ , and an index  $j\in[n]$ .
- Verify(pk, aux, av, ct<sub>j</sub>, j, op)  $\rightarrow$  {0,1}: A deterministic poly-time algorithm that verifies that the opening op is valid with respect to (av, j) for some index  $j \in [n]$  and ciphertext ct<sub>j</sub>.

<span id="page-37-0"></span>**Definition 5.3** (ATree.Build procedure). We define a procedure ATree.Build(pk, aux, ct =  $\{ct_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ ) that aims to compute the logical AND of all bits encoded under  $ct_i$  for all  $i \in [n]$ . The output is succinct w.r.t. n. Let ATree.Build perform the following computations:

- 1. Without loss of generality, assume  $n = 2^h$  for some h. If not, pad the input with encryption of 1's to get to the next largest power of 2.
- 2. Consider a binary tree of height h whose vertices are  $v \in \{0,1\}^{\leq h}$ . The root of the binary tree is the empty string  $\epsilon$ .
- 3. Parse  $pk = \{fhe.pk_v\}$  for all binary strings  $v \in \{0,1\}^{\leq h-1}$ .
- 4. Parse  $aux = \{aux_v = (aux_{v,0}, aux_{v,1})\}$  for all  $v \in \{0, 1\}^{\leq h-1}$ .
- 5. For all  $j \in [n]$ , write j as a string of bits  $v \in \{0,1\}^h$ . Parse  $\mathsf{ct} = \{\mathsf{ct}_v\}$
- 6. For  $\ell = h$ , set  $\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_v = \mathsf{ct}_v$ .
- 7. For every  $0 \le \ell \le h-1$ , for every vertex  $v \in \{0,1\}^{\ell}$ , compute

$$\begin{aligned} &\mathsf{aux}_v &= (\mathsf{aux}_{v,0}, \mathsf{aux}_{v,1}) \\ &\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{v,\beta} &= \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}\left(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\cdot, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_w), \mathsf{aux}_{v,\beta}\right), \forall \beta \in \{0,1\} \end{aligned}$$

where  $w = v \| \beta$  is appending  $\beta$  to v. Finally compute for the binary logical AND circuit C,

$$\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_v = \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}\left(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, C, (\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{v,0}, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{v,1})\right)$$

8. Output  $av = \widehat{ct}_{\epsilon}$  associated with the root of the tree.

<span id="page-37-1"></span>**Definition 5.4** (ATree.Open procedure). We define a procedure ATree.Open(pk, aux, ct, j) that produces an opening op to a ciphertext ct<sub>j</sub> for some  $j \in [n]$ . Let ATree.Open perform the following operations:

- 1. For all  $i \in [n]$ , write i as a string of bits  $v \in \{0,1\}^h$ . Parse  $\mathsf{ct} = \{\mathsf{ct}_v\}$
- 2. Let the binary representation of j be u, i.e  $j \in \{0,1\}^h$ .
- 3. For every  $0 \le \ell \le h$ , let  $v_{\ell}$  be the vertex on the path from root  $\epsilon$  to j at the level  $\ell$ . Let  $v'_{\ell}$  be the sibling node of  $v_{\ell}$ .
- 4. Compute the tree as in ATree.Build and store all the ciphertexts  $\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_v$ .
- <span id="page-37-2"></span>5. Output op =  $\left(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_u, \{\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{v'_{\ell}}\}_{0 \le \ell \le h}\right)$

{38}------------------------------------------------

**Definition 5.5** (ATree.Verify procedure). We define a procedure ATree.Verify(pk, aux, av, ct<sub>j</sub>, j, op) that determines whether the opening op is valid w.r.t. (av, j) for some  $j \in [n]$ . Let ATree.Verify perform the following computations:

- 1. Parse  $pk = \{fhe.pk_v\}$  for all  $v \in \{0,1\}^{\leq h-1}$ .
- 2. Parse  $aux = \{aux_v = (aux_{v,0}, aux_{v,1})\}$  for all  $v \in \{0, 1\}^{\leq h-1}$ .
- 3. Parse op =  $\left(\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_u^{\star}, \{\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_{v'_{\ell}}^{\star}\}_{0 \leq \ell \leq h}\right)$
- 4. Accept if all of the following checks pass:
  - (a) Let the binary representation of j be u. Check if  $\widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_u^{\star} = \mathsf{ct}_u$ . Let  $\mathsf{ct}_u' = \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}_u^{\star}$ .
  - (b) For every  $0 \le \ell \le h 1$ , compute

$$\begin{aligned} &\mathsf{aux}_{v_\ell} &= (\mathsf{aux}_{v_{\ell,0}}, \mathsf{aux}_{v_{\ell,1}}) \\ &\mathsf{ct}'_{v_\ell,0} &= \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}\left(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}, \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\cdot, \mathsf{ct}'_{v_{\ell+1}}), \mathsf{aux}_{v_\ell,0}\right) \\ &\mathsf{ct}'_{v_\ell,1} &= \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}\left(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}, \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\cdot, \widehat{\mathsf{ct}}^\star_{v'_{\ell+1}}), \mathsf{aux}_{v_\ell,1}\right) \\ &\mathsf{ct}'_{v_\ell} &= \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}, C, \mathsf{ct}'_{v_\ell,0}, \mathsf{ct}'_{v_\ell,1}) \end{aligned}$$

where C is the logical AND circuit. We continue this procedure until  $\ell=0$  at which point we have  $\mathsf{ct}'_{v_0}$ .

(c) Check if  $\mathsf{ct}'_{v_0} = \mathsf{av}$ .

#### <span id="page-38-0"></span>5.2 Construction

#### Ingredients

- 1. Somewhere Extractable BARG or seBARG(Setup, Prove, Verify, Extract)
- 2. Fully Homomorphic Encryption or FHE(KeyGen, Eval, Enc, Dec)
- 3. Hash Family with Succinct Local Openings HT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify)
- 4. The FHE based AND Tree ATree(Build, Open, Verify) as in Definition 5.2.
- 5. Commit-and-Measure Protocol Maha(Gen, Com, Open, Test, Out) as defined in Definition 3.21.
- 6. The  $\kappa$ -fold FHM post-hoc verification protocol ( $P_{\text{FHM}}, V_{\text{FHM}}$ ) as defined in Definition 3.30 where  $\kappa$  is a polynomial in instance length.

In addition to the *Knowledge Language* defined in Section 4, we make use of another extension of this notion. This can be defined as follows.

**Definition 5.6** (FHM Knowledge Language). An FHM knowledge language  $\mathcal{G}$  with hard-coded parameters  $\mathsf{pk}$ ,  $\mathsf{hk}$ ,  $\mathsf{ct}_r$ ,  $\mathsf{aux}$  is defined as follows.

$$\begin{split} \mathcal{G} &= \{(i,x,\alpha,\beta,\gamma,c) \mid & \exists \ y, \mathsf{op}_y \ \text{s.t. HT.Verify}(\mathsf{hk},\alpha,i,y,\mathsf{op}_y) = 1 \\ & \land & \exists \ z, \mathsf{op}_z \ \text{s.t. HT.Verify}(\mathsf{hk},\beta,i,z,\mathsf{op}_z) = 1 \\ & \land & \exists \ \mathsf{op}_\mathsf{ct} \ \text{s.t. ATree.Verify}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{aux},\gamma,\mathsf{ct},i,\mathsf{op}_\mathsf{ct}) = 1 \\ & \land & \mathsf{ct} = \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x,y,z,c,\cdot),\mathsf{ct}_r) \} \end{split}$$

Additionally, let  $\mathcal{R}'_{\mathcal{G}}$  denote a relation s.t  $\mathcal{R}'_{\mathcal{G}}(i, x, \alpha, \beta, \gamma, c) = 1$  iff  $(i, x, \alpha, \beta, \gamma, c) \in \mathcal{G}$ . Let the corresponding circuit encoding this relation be  $\mathcal{C}'_{\mathcal{G}}$ 

{39}------------------------------------------------

## Protocol Specification

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 k , 1 <sup>N</sup> , j<sup>∗</sup> ) → (crs, vk): The Setup algorithm generates a crs that grows as poly(λ, k, N). We use two indexing schemes: the standard integer indexing scheme and a binary string indexing scheme. The conversion between them, unless explicitly mentioned, is implied by the context.
  - Setup the commit-and-measure protocol Maha. For each i ∈ [k], Sample randomness ri \$← {0, 1} λ . Sample basis string h<sup>i</sup> using randomness r<sup>i</sup> .

$$\forall i \in [k], \; (\mathsf{mh.pk}_i, \mathsf{mh.sk}_i) \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, h_i; r_i)$$

– Let the FHE keys be indexed by binary strings v ∈ {0, 1} ≤log k , where v = ϵ represents the root of a binary tree. For all v, setup FHE as follows

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_v) \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda)$$

For all i ∈ [k], use the binary representation of u ∈ {0, 1} log <sup>k</sup> and perform the following.

$$\mathsf{ct}_{r_i} \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_i, r_i)$$

Compute aux<sup>v</sup> for all binary strings v ∈ {0, 1} <sup>≤</sup>log <sup>k</sup>−<sup>1</sup> as follows:

$$\mathsf{aux}_v = \left(\mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_{v\parallel 0}), \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_{v\parallel 1})\right)$$

– Setup HT as follows.

$$\mathsf{ht}.\mathsf{hk} \leftarrow \mathsf{HT}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^k,1^\lambda)$$

– Generate sB.crs<sup>i</sup> for seBARG<sup>i</sup> for i ∈ [2] as follows for any j ∈ [k]

$$\begin{split} &(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.td}_1) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}|}, j) \\ &(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \mathsf{sB.td}_2) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}'|}, j) \end{split}$$

– Let l = log k. Set outputs as follows.

$$\begin{array}{ll} \textbf{mh.pk} &= (\mathsf{mh.pk}_{0^l}, \ldots, \mathsf{mh.pk}_{1^l}) \\ \textbf{fhe.pk} &= (\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{\epsilon}, \ldots, \mathsf{fhe.pk}_{1^l}) \\ \textbf{aux} &= (\mathsf{aux}_{\epsilon}, \ldots, \mathsf{aux}_{1^{l-1}}) \\ \textbf{ct}_{\boldsymbol{r}} &= (\mathsf{ct}_{r_1}, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_{r_k}) \\ \mathsf{crs} &= (\textbf{mh.pk}, \textbf{fhe.pk}, \textbf{aux}, \mathsf{ht.hk}, \mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \textbf{ct}_{\boldsymbol{r}}) \\ \mathsf{vk} &= \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{\epsilon} \end{array}$$

• Prove(crs, {xj}j∈[k] , {|ϕ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ ⊗κ }j∈[k] ): Here, the quantum state |ϕi⟩ is the quantum witness to the statement x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes for L ∈ QMA.

{40}------------------------------------------------

– Round 1: Perform the following and send (ˆy, πy) to the verifier.

$$\begin{array}{lll} (\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\mathsf{ht.hk},\mathbf{mh.pk}) &= \mathsf{crs} \\ (\mathsf{mh.pk}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{mh.pk}_k) &= \mathbf{mh.pk} \\ &\forall j,\ |\psi_j\rangle &\leftarrow \mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_j,|\phi_j\rangle^{\otimes\kappa}) \\ &\forall j,\ (y_j,|\mathsf{st}_j\rangle) &\leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Com}(\mathsf{mh.pk}_j,|\psi_j\rangle) \\ &\hat{y} &= \mathsf{HT.Hash}(\mathsf{ht.hk},\{y_j\}_{j\in[k]}) \\ &\forall j,\ \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}} &= \mathsf{HT.Open}(\mathsf{ht.hk},\{y_j\}_{j\in[k]},j) \\ &\pi_y &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\{(j,\hat{y})\}_{j\in[k]},\{y_j,\mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}}\}_{j\in[k]}) \end{array}$$

- Round 3: Receive bit c ∈ {0, 1}. Parse fhe.pk to get the public keys at level ℓ = log k. Perform the following and send (ct<sup>∗</sup> , πct) to the verifier.
  - 1. Parse the crs

$$\begin{aligned} (\textbf{fhe.pk}, \textbf{aux}, \textbf{ct}_{\boldsymbol{r}}, \textbf{sB.crs}_2) &= \textbf{crs} \\ (\textbf{fhe.pk}_{0^\ell}, \textbf{fhe.pk}_2, \dots, \textbf{fhe.pk}_{1^\ell}) &= \textbf{fhe.pk} \\ (\textbf{ct}_{r_1}, \textbf{ct}_{r_2}, \dots, \textbf{ct}_{r_k}) &= \textbf{ct}_{\boldsymbol{r}} \end{aligned}$$

2. Run Maha.Open

$$\forall j, \ z_j \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Open}(\ket{\mathsf{st}_j}, c)$$

3. Build ATree and compute the openings for each j ∈ [k]. Write all numbers j as binary strings v of length log k.

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{ct}_v & \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{pk}_v,\mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_v,y_v,z_v,c,\cdot),\mathsf{ct}_{r_v}) \\ \mathsf{ct}^\star & = \mathsf{ATree}.\mathsf{Build}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{aux},\{\mathsf{ct}_v\}) \\ \mathsf{op}_v^{\mathsf{ct}^\star} & = \mathsf{ATree}.\mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{pk},\mathsf{aux},\{\mathsf{ct}_v\},v)) \end{split}$$

4. Compute the proof πct as follows

$$\begin{split} \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]} &= \{(y_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{y}}, z_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\hat{z}}, \mathsf{ct}_j, \mathsf{op}_j^{\mathsf{ct}^\star})\}_{j \in [k]} \\ &\pi_{\mathsf{ct}} &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \{(j, x_j, \hat{y}, \hat{z}, \mathsf{ct}^\star, c)\}_{j \in [k]}, \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{split}$$

- Verify(crs, vk, {xj}j∈[k] , y, π ˆ <sup>y</sup>, πct, ct<sup>⋆</sup> ): Works as follows under normal (honest) execution.
  - Round 2: Receive (ˆy, πy). Parse sB.crs<sup>1</sup> from crs. Check if

$$\mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\{(j,\hat{y})\}_{j\in[k]},\pi_y) = 1$$

If yes, sample bit c and send it to the prover. If not, reject.

– Final Verification: Receive (ct<sup>⋆</sup> , πct) from the verifier. Parse fhe.sk<sup>ϵ</sup> from vk and sB.crs<sup>2</sup> from crs. Accept if

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \{(j, x_j, \hat{y}, \hat{z}, \mathsf{ct}^\star, c)\}_{j \in [k]}, \pi_\mathsf{ct}) &= 1 \land \\ \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_\epsilon, \mathsf{ct}^\star) &= 1 \end{split}$$

Remark 5.7. We denote the above protocol as M and M<sup>λ</sup> is a λ-fold repetition.

{41}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-41-0"></span>5.3 Security Analysis

Completeness Completeness of  $\mathfrak{M}$  follows immediately from the completeness properties of the underlying building blocks. If the instances  $x_i \in \mathcal{L}_{\text{yes}}$  for all  $i \in [k]$ , we must have, by the completeness properties of the Commit and Measure protocol (Definition 3.22) and the FHM protocol (Definition 3.32), that VerifyInstance $(x_i, \cdot, \cdot, \cdot, \cdot) = 1$  with non-negligible probability. By the correctness of the post quantum FHE-scheme (Definition 3.7), we must have that the leaves of the AND-tree be encryption of 1. By the correctness of ATree.Build (Definition 5.3), we must have the root ct\* of the FHE based AND-tree be an encryption of 1. By the completeness of seBARGs (Definition 3.6), correctness of ATree.Open (Definition 5.4), and ATree.Verify (Definition 5.5), we must have the proofs  $\pi_y, \pi_{\text{ct}}$  always being accepted. Therefore, the overall protocol accepts with non-negligible probability if  $x_i \in \mathcal{L}_{\text{yes}}$  for all  $i \in [k]$ . Furthermore, completeness of  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  then follows using a union bound argument.

**Round Complexity** We observe that  $\mathfrak{M}$  and (therefore)  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  both have **3 rounds** with setup. Alternatively, the verifier can do the setup and the crs can be sent to the prover as the first message of the verifier to get a **4 round** Interactive **Public Coin** BARG for QMA.

**Soundness** The protocol  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  satisfies the notion of *semi-adaptive computational soundness* as defined in definition 3.20. We now provide the soundness proof.

<span id="page-41-1"></span>**Theorem 5.8.** Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure protocol (Definition 3.23) and the FHM protocol (Definition 3.32), collision resistance w.r.t. opening of the hash tree family HT, semi-adaptive computational soundness of seBARG, and the semantic security of the post-quantum fully homomorphic encryption (FHE) scheme, the protocol  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  satisfies semi-adaptive soundness as defined in Definition 3.20.

Proof. We begin by setting a bunch of hybrid experiments and replacing the final round FHE decryption step by running the Verifylnstance predicate on the instance  $i^*$  chosen by the QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ . Next, we show that the final hybrid must have computationally orthogonal projectors as defined in Definition 3.24. Finally, we claim that the  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{M}$  must have negligible soundness since the attacker gained negligible advantage to reach the final hybrid.

**Hybrid 0**: Normal execution of the protocol.

**Hybrid 1**: Same as previous execution except enable extraction on index  $i^*$  (instead of a random index j), i.e

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star) \quad \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}|}, i^\star) \\ (\mathsf{sB.crs}_2^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star) \quad \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}'|}, i^\star)$$

**Hybrid 2**: We now update the protocol by making the following extractions at appropriate places on the verifier's end.

$$\begin{aligned} y'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}'^{\hat{y}} &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star, \{(i,\hat{y})\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_y) \\ y^\star_{i^\star}, z^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{ct}^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\hat{y}}, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\hat{c}t}, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\mathsf{ct}^\star} &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star, \{(i, x_i, \hat{y}, \hat{z}, \mathsf{ct}^\star)\}_{i \in [k]}, \pi_f) \end{aligned}$$

{42}------------------------------------------------

In the final round, reject if the extracted witnesses aren't valid by checking the following condition.

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{y}, i^\star, y'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}'^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star}) &= 1 \land \\ \mathsf{HT.Verify}(\mathsf{ht.hk}, \hat{y}, i^\star, y^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\hat{y}}_{i^\star}) &= 1 \land \\ \mathsf{ATree.Verify}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{aux}, \mathsf{ct}^\star, i^\star, \mathsf{ct}^\star_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{\mathsf{ct}^\star}_{i^\star}) &= 1 \end{aligned}$$

**Hybrid 3**: Same as previous execution except that now we add the following additional check in the final round of the protocol. We reject if the following is not true

$$y_{i^{\star}}^{\star} = y_{i^{\star}}'$$

**Hybrid 4**: This hybrid will also be referred as **Hybrid (4,0)**. Let u be the binary representation of  $i^*$ . Parse

$$\left(\mathsf{ct}_u^\star, \{\mathsf{ct}_{v_\ell}^\star\}_{0 \leq \ell \leq \log k} \right) = \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{\mathsf{ct}^\star}$$

Perform the following in the final round along with the seBARG proof verifications

$$\mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_\epsilon,\mathsf{ct}^\star) = 1 \ \land \ \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_{v_0'},\mathsf{ct}_{v_0'}^\star) = 1$$

We introduce  $\log k + 2$  intermediate hybrids where the challenger decrypts the ciphertexts received with the opening  $\operatorname{\mathsf{op}}_{i^{\star}}^{\mathsf{ct}^{\star}}$ . Let the  $\ell^{\mathsf{th}}$  intermediate hybrid be  $\operatorname{\mathbf{Hybrid}}$  (4,  $\ell$ ), where  $0 \leq \ell \leq \log k + 1$ . We define  $\ell^{\mathsf{th}}$  hybrid to be the same as  $(\ell - 1)^{\mathsf{th}}$  hybrid but with the additional check

$$\mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_{v'_{\ell}},\mathsf{ct}^{\star}_{v'_{\ell}}) = 1$$

Thus, we have **Hybrid**  $(4, \log k)$  checking the following:

$$\mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_\epsilon, \mathsf{ct}^\star) = 1 \land \ldots \land \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_u, \mathsf{ct}_u^\star) = 1$$

We define **Hybrid** (4,  $\log k + 1$ ) as a hybrid where we drop the decryption of  $\mathsf{ct}^*$ , i.e., we run the following check

$$\mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_u, \mathsf{ct}_u^\star) = 1 \ \land \ \ldots \ \land \ \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v_0'}, \mathsf{ct}_{v_0'}^\star) = 1$$

**Hybrid 5**: We replace the FHE.Dec for index  $i^*$  by performing the check

VerifyInstance
$$(x_{i^{\star}}, y_{i^{\star}}^{\star}, z_{i^{\star}}^{\star}, c, r_{i^{\star}}) = 1$$

**Hybrid 6**: We now replace  $\mathsf{ct}_{r_{i^*}}$  in the crs with  $\mathsf{ct}_0 = \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{i^*}, 0^\lambda)$  and let the protocol continue as in the previous hybrid.

<span id="page-42-0"></span>We show that **Hybrid 6** is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol that has orthogonal projectors. We then show that since the attacker's advantage has changed negligibly from **Hybrid 0** to **Hybrid 6**,  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  cannot have non-negligible soundness. To begin, let us assume that the attacker's advantage in breaking the semi-adaptive somewhere computational soundness property of **Hybrid 0** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ . We wish to show that  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$  is a negligible function.

{43}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 5.9.** Assuming seBARG satisfies the index hiding property, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**.

*Proof.* The proof of this lemma follows from Lemma 4.7. We can find a negligible function  $\mu_1(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 1** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 5.10.** Assuming seBARG satisfies the somewhere argument of knowledge property and HT satisfies opening completeness, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2**.

*Proof.* The proof of this lemma follows from Lemma 4.8. Consequently, we can find a negligible function  $\mu_2(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 2** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 5.11.** Assuming the collision resistance of the hash function used in HT with respect to the openings and hashed values, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3**.

*Proof.* The proof of this lemma follows from Lemma 4.9. Therefore, we can quantify the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 3** using a negligible function  $\mu_3(\lambda)$  as  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-43-0"></span>**Lemma 5.12.** Assuming the correctness of FHE evaluation, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 3** and **Hybrid 4** 

*Proof.* Let the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  have non-negligible advantage across the two hybrids. This implies that

$$\mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{fhe}.\mathsf{sk}_{v_0'},\mathsf{ct}_{v_0'}^{\star}) = 0$$

However, since the ATree.Build procedure produces the logical AND of the input ciphertexts, we must have FHE.Dec(fhe.sk<sub>\epsilon</sub>, ct<sup>\*</sup>) = 0, a contradiction. Therefore, we can quantify the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 4** using a negligible function  $\mu_4(\lambda)$  as  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 5.13.** Assuming the correctness of FHE evaluation, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is the indistinguishable between **Hybrid (4,**  $\ell - 1$ ) and **Hybrid (4,**  $\ell$ ) for all  $1 \le \ell \le \log k$ .

*Proof.* The follows from the proof of Lemma 5.12. Therefore, we can quantify the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** (4,  $\ell$ ) using a negligible function  $\nu_{\ell}(\lambda)$  as  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \sum_{i=0}^{\ell} \nu_i(\lambda)$ 

**Lemma 5.14.** Assuming the correctness of FHE evaluation, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable between **Hybrid (4,** log k) and **Hybrid (4,** log k+1)

*Proof.* The follows from the proof of Lemma 5.12. Therefore, we can quantify the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** (4,  $\log k + 1$ ) using a negligible function  $\nu_{\log k + 1}(\lambda)$  as  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \sum_{i=0}^{\log k + 1} \nu_i(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 5.15.** Assuming the correctness of the post-quantum FHE scheme, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid (4,**  $\log k + 1$ ) and **Hybrid 5**.

{44}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Let the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  have non-negligible advantage across the two hybrids. This implies that

$$\mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_u, y_u^\star, z_u^\star, c, r_j) = 0 \ \land \ \mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk}_u, \mathsf{ct}_u^\star) = 1$$

which contradicts the evaluation correctness of the FHE scheme. Therefore, the advantage of the attacker across **Hybrid (4,**  $\log k + 1)$  and **Hybrid 5** can be characterized by a negligible function  $\nu_{\log k+2}(\lambda)$ . Let  $\mu_5(\lambda) = \sum_{i=0}^{\log k+2} \nu_i(\lambda)$ . Thus, the advantage of attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 5** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \mu_5(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-44-0"></span>**Lemma 5.16.** Assuming the semantic security of the post-quantum FHE scheme, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 5** and **Hybrid 6**.

*Proof.* Assume the contrary. We show that there exists an efficient reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that breaks FHE semantic security.  $\mathcal{B}$  interacts with challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  for the FHE security game as follows.

- ${\mathcal B}$  receives the index  $i^\star$  from the attacker  ${\mathcal A}$
- $\mathcal{B}$  samples randomness  $r_{i^*}$  and sends  $(r_{i^*}, 0^{\lambda})$  to  $\mathcal{C}$ .
- $\mathcal{C}$  replies with  $\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}$ , a random encryption of one of the two messages.
- $\mathcal{B}$  replaces  $\mathsf{ct}_{r_{i^{\star}}}$  in the crs with  $\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}$  and interacts with  $\mathcal{A}$  to obtain  $(\hat{z}, \pi_z, \mathsf{ct}^*, \pi_{\mathsf{ct}})$ .

Observe that based on which of the two messages is encrypted in  $\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}$ , we could be operating in different hybrids. Now, if  $\mathcal{A}$  gets accepted, then  $\mathcal{B}$  can guess  $r_{i^*}$  as the message under the encryption of  $\widetilde{\mathsf{ct}}$ , breaking the semantic security of the FHE scheme. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_6(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage for the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 6** must be  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \mu_5(\lambda) - \mu_6(\lambda)$ .

At this point, the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  has at least  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$  chance of success, where

$$negl(\lambda) = (\mu_1 + \mu_2 + \mu_3 + \mu_4 + \mu_5 + \mu_6)(\lambda)$$

<span id="page-44-1"></span>**Lemma 5.17.** Assuming the soundness of the Commit and Measure Protocol (Maha) and the FHM Protocol, **Hybrid 6**, hence referred to as  $\mathfrak{M}'$ , is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors.

*Proof.* The proof follows from Lemma 4.10.

<span id="page-44-2"></span>**Lemma 5.18.** The  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{M}'$ , i.e  $(\mathfrak{M}')^{\lambda}$  has negligible soundness.

*Proof.* This follows from Theorem 3.27.

<span id="page-44-3"></span>**Lemma 5.19.** The  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{M}$ , i.e  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$ , has negligible soundness.

*Proof.* This proof follows the same arguments as Lemma 4.12. Therefore, we must have  $\varepsilon(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Therefore, we note that  $\mathfrak{M}^{\lambda}$  is a public coin BARG that satisfies the semi-adaptive soundness property.

{45}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-45-0"></span>6 Making 4-round Batch Arguments for QMA fully-succinct

As outlined in the construction above, the first message of the verifier scales linearly with the number of instances. We need 2k + 1 FHE public keys, 2k encryption of secret keys for internal nodes of the ATree, k public keys for the Commit-and-Measure protocol, and finally k encryption of the randomness r for each instance  $x_i$ . We proceed with our construction as below.

#### <span id="page-45-1"></span>6.1 Construction

We construct the batS algorithm by obfuscating the batchSetup algorithm outlined in the construction of  $\mathfrak{M}$ . We rely on PPRF security and  $i\mathcal{O}$  security to achieve a semi-adaptive notion of soundness. We need the following additional ingredients when compared to the construction in Section 5.

#### Ingredients

- 1. Indistinguishability Obfuscation  $i\mathcal{O}$  as defined in Definition 3.12.
- 2. Two puncturable PRF families PPRF(Eval, Puncture, PuncEval) with output lengths  $\ell(\lambda)$  and  $2\ell(\lambda)$  respectively, as defined in Definition 3.9.

#### Batched Setup Algorithm : Let

$$F : \{0,1\}^{\lambda} \times \{0,1\}^{\leq \log k} \to \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$$
$$F' : \{0,1\}^{\lambda} \times \{0,1\}^{\leq \log k} \to \{0,1\}^{2\lambda}$$

be two PPRF functions. We batch  $\mathfrak{M}.\mathsf{Setup}$  procedure by hardwiring  $\lambda, N, k$ , PPRF key  $K_1 \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  associated with F and PPRF keys  $K_2, K_3 \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  associated with F' respectively. The batchSetup algorithm is presented in Fig. 1. Finally, to complete the succinct key generation setup, we obfuscate the batchSetup program, sample the crs for the seBARGs (for any random index  $j^* \in [k]$ ) and the hash function for the HT

$$\begin{split} \widetilde{\mathsf{batS}} &\leftarrow \mathsf{i}\mathcal{O}(\mathsf{batchSetup}) \\ \mathsf{ht.hk} &\leftarrow \mathsf{HT.Gen}(1^\lambda, 1^k) \\ \mathsf{sB.crs}_1 &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}|}, j^\star) \\ \mathsf{sB.crs}_2 &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{G}}'|}, j^\star) \\ \mathsf{crs} &= (\widetilde{\mathsf{Setup}}, \mathsf{ht.hk}, \mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.crs}_2) \end{split}$$

To complete the protocol, the prover uses the program  $\widetilde{\mathsf{batS}}$  to generate all the required public information for each binary index  $v \in \{0,1\}^{\leq \log k}$  of the ATree. The leaf nodes of the tree represent the instances. The prover uses the computed public information and performs the rest of the protocol as in Section 5.

**Remark 6.1.** We denote this protocol as  $\mathfrak{M}_s$  and  $\mathfrak{M}_s^{\lambda}$  is a  $\lambda$ -fold repetition.

{46}------------------------------------------------

#### Program batchSetup

<span id="page-46-1"></span>Constants: λ, N, k, K1, K2, K<sup>3</sup> Input: Index v ∈ {0, 1} ≤log k . Output: Public information PK<sup>v</sup>

1. If length of v is less than log k:

(a) Let 
$$r_v = F_{K_1}(v)$$
,  $\{r_{v||\beta} = F_{K_1}(v||\beta)\}_{\beta \in \{0,1\}}$ , and  $(r'_{v||0}, r'_{v||1}) = F'_{K_2}(v)$ .

(b) Perform the following operations

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_v) &= \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; r_v) \\ (\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v \parallel \beta}, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v \parallel \beta}) &= \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; r_{v \parallel \beta}), \ \beta \in \{0, 1\} \\ \forall \beta \in \{0, 1\} \ \mathsf{aux}_{v, \beta} &= \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v \parallel \beta}; r'_{v \parallel \beta}) \\ \mathsf{aux}_v &= (\mathsf{aux}_{v, 0}, \mathsf{aux}_{v, 1}) \end{split}$$

- (c) Output PK<sup>v</sup> = (fhe.pk<sup>v</sup> , auxv)
- 2. Else:
  - (a) rv, r′′ <sup>v</sup> = F ′ K<sup>3</sup> (v), r ′ <sup>v</sup> = FK<sup>1</sup> (v).
  - (b) Setup the Commit-and-Measure protocol Maha as follows

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}_v, \mathsf{mh.sk}_v) = \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^{\lambda}; r_v)$$

(c) Sample fhe.pk<sup>v</sup> and compute ctr<sup>v</sup> as follows

```
(fhe.pkv
        , fhe.skv) = FHE.KeyGen(1λ
                                         ; r
                                            ′
                                            v
                                             )
              ctrv = FHE.Enc(fhe.pkv
                                          , rv; r
                                                ′′
                                                v
                                                  )
```

(d) Output PK<sup>v</sup> = (mh.pk<sup>v</sup> , fhe.pk<sup>v</sup> , ctr<sup>v</sup> )

Figure 1: Batched Setup algorithm

## <span id="page-46-0"></span>6.2 Security Analysis

Completeness : The completeness of the protocol follows from the perfect completeness of iO as defined in Definition [3.12.](#page-21-2)

Round Complexity : We observe that M<sup>s</sup> and therefore M<sup>λ</sup> <sup>s</sup> both have 3 rounds with setup. Moreover, if the crs is considered as the first message sent by the verifier, we observe that the message is succinct (independent of k) and we get a 4 round Interactive Designated Verifier BARG for QMA

Soundness : The protocol satisfies semi-adaptive notion of soundness as defined in Definiton [3.20.](#page-24-1) We now provide the soundness proof.

Theorem 6.2.
