Assuming the security of iO (Definition [3.12\)](#page-21-2), Puncturable PRFs (Defintion [3.9\)](#page-21-3), and the soundness of the non-succinct designated verifier BARG for QMA protocol M, the protocol M<sup>λ</sup> s satisfies semi-adaptive soundness as defined in Definition [3.20\)](#page-24-1)

{47}------------------------------------------------

Proof. We carefully organize a sequence of hybrids that modifies the Setup algorithm such that it remains functionally equivalent, but allows a challenger to sample  $\log k + 1$  FHE keys needed to verify the opening from the ATree. Using the security of PPRF and  $i\mathcal{O}$ , we argue that any cheating prover cannot differentiate between any two successive programs. Finally, we follow the hybrids and proof of Theorem 5.8 to show a  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{M}_s$  must have negligible soundness. Assume a binary string based indexing scheme. Let u be the binary representation of  $i^*$  using  $\log k$  bits. Let the nodes along the path from the root  $\epsilon = v_0$  to u be  $\{v_\ell\}$  and let the sibling nodes be  $\{v'_\ell\}$  for  $0 \le \ell \le \log k$ . We have the following sequence of hybrids

Hybrid 0: Normal execution of the protocol.

**Hybrid 1**: For each  $0 \le \ell \le \log k - 1$ , we have four intermediary hybrids:

(a) **Hybrid** (1,  $\ell$ , 0): Sample fhe.pk<sub> $v_{\ell}$ </sub> as follows

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v_\ell}) = \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; F_{K_1}(v_\ell))$$

Puncture  $K_1$  at  $v = v_\ell$  and run  $\mathsf{batchSetup}_0^{(\ell)}$  as in Fig. 2 by hardwiring  $\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}$ 

(b) **Hybrid** (1,  $\ell$ , 1): Sample fhe.pk<sub> $v_{\ell}$ </sub> using fresh randomness  $r \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  as follows:

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v_\ell}, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v_\ell}) = \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; r)$$

Run batchSetup<sub>0</sub><sup>( $\ell$ )</sup> as in Fig. 2 by hardwiring fhe.pk<sub> $v_{\ell}$ </sub>.

(c) **Hybrid** (1,  $\ell$ , 2): Perform the following operations for  $v = v_{\ell}$  with  $(r'_{v\parallel 0}, r'_{v\parallel 1}) = F'_{K_2}(v)$ 

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v\parallel\beta}, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v\parallel\beta}) &= \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; F_{K_1}(v\parallel\beta)), \ \beta \in \{0,1\} \\ \forall \beta \in \{0,1\}, \ \mathsf{aux}_{v,v\parallel\beta} &= \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v\parallel\beta}; r'_{v\parallel\beta}) \\ \mathsf{aux}_v &= (\mathsf{aux}_{v\parallel0}, \mathsf{aux}_{v\parallel1}) \end{split}$$

Puncture  $K_2$  at  $v = v_\ell$  and run batchSetup<sub>1</sub><sup>(\ell)</sup> as in Fig. 3 by hardwiring  $\mathsf{aux}_v$ .

(d) **Hybrid** (1,  $\ell$ , 3): Perform the following operations for  $v = v_{\ell}$  using fresh randomness  $r'_{v||0}, r'_{v||1} \leftarrow \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ :

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{v\parallel\beta},\mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v\parallel\beta}) &= \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda;F_{K_1}(v\parallel\beta)), \ \beta \in \{0,1\} \\ \forall \beta \in \{0,1\}, \ \mathsf{aux}_{v,v\parallel\beta} &= \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_v,\mathsf{fhe.sk}_{v\parallel\beta};r'_{v\parallel\beta}) \\ \mathsf{aux}_v &= (\mathsf{aux}_{v\parallel0},\mathsf{aux}_{v\parallel1}) \end{split}$$

Run batchSetup<sub>1</sub><sup>( $\ell$ )</sup> as in Fig. 3 by hardwiring  $aux_v$ .

**Hybrid 2**: We have two intermediate hybrids:

(a) **Hybrid** (2, 0): Sample fhe. $pk_u$  as follows

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_u, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_u) = \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^\lambda; F_{K_1}(u))$$

Puncture  $K_1$  at v=u and run batchSetup<sub>2</sub><sup>(log k)</sup> as in Fig. 4 by hardwiring fhe.pk<sub>u</sub>

{48}------------------------------------------------

(b) **Hybrid** (2, 1): Sample fhe.pk<sub>u</sub> using fresh randomness  $r \leftarrow \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  as follows

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_u, \mathsf{fhe.sk}_u) = \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^{\lambda}; r)$$

Run batch $\mathsf{Setup}_2^{(\log k)}$  as in Fig. 4 by hardwiring  $\mathsf{fhe.pk}_u$ .

**Hybrid 3**: We have two intermediate hybrids:

(a) **Hybrid** (3, 0): Perform the following operations  $r_u, r''_u = F'_{K_3}(u)$ 

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}_u, \mathsf{mh.sk}_u) = \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^\lambda; r_u)$$
  $\mathsf{ct}_{r_u} = \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_u, r_u; r_u'')$ 

Puncture  $K_3$  at v=u and run batchSetup<sub>3</sub><sup>(log k)</sup> as in Fig. 5 by hardwiring (mh.pk<sub>u</sub>, ct<sub>ru</sub>).

(b) **Hybrid** (3,1): Perform the following operations using fresh randomness  $r, r'' \leftarrow \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ 

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}_u, \mathsf{mh.sk}_u) = \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^\lambda; r)$$
  $\mathsf{ct}_{r_u} = \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}_u, r; r'')$ 

Run batchSetup<sub>3</sub><sup>(log k)</sup> as in Fig. 5 by hardwiring (mh.pk<sub>u</sub>, ct<sub>r<sub>u</sub></sub>).

Hybrid 4-9: Follows Hybrid 1-6 in Theorem 5.8.

We now prove that the attacker's advantage negligibly changes through the sequence of hybrids assuming the soundness properties of the underlying building blocks. Subsequently, we show, like in Theorem 5.8 that **Hybrid 9** is a Commit-and-Measure protocol with orthogonal projects. Thus any parallel repetition of **Hybrid 9** has negligible soundness, thus showing that  $\mathfrak{M}_s^{\lambda}$  has negligible soundness. For contradiction, assume that a cheating prover has non-negligible advantage  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$  in breaking **Hybrid 0**. Consider the following lemmas.

<span id="page-48-0"></span>**Lemma 6.3.** Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation  $i\mathcal{O}$ , for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid (1,0,0)** 

Proof. We observe that the input/output behavior of batchSetup<sub>0</sub><sup>(0)</sup> is identical to that of batchSetup. We only puncture the PPRF key  $K_1$  at  $v = \epsilon$  and replace it with the hardwired value fhe.pk<sub>\epsilon</sub>. For contradiction, assume  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid (1,0,0)**. Consider a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that receives the obfuscated program batS from the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  where batchSetup or batchSetup<sub>0</sub><sup>0</sup> is chosen uniformly at random. Since  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across the two hybrids,  $\mathcal{B}$  uses  $\mathcal{A}$  trivially to guess which program was obfuscated, contradicting the security of i $\mathcal{O}$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\nu_{0,0}(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid (1,0,0)** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \nu_{0,0}(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-48-1"></span>**Lemma 6.4.** Assuming the security of PPRF, for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid**  $(1, \ell, 0)$  and **Hybrid**  $(1, \ell, 1)$  for all  $0 \le \ell \le \log k - 2$ 

Proof. The only change between **Hybrid**  $(1, \ell, 0)$  and **Hybrid**  $(1, \ell, 1)$  is the randomness used to generate  $\mathsf{fhe.pk}_{\epsilon}$ . For contradiction, assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across the two hybrids. Construct a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that interacts trivially with  $\mathcal{A}$  to produce distributions  $(F_{K_1\{\epsilon,\ldots,\nu_\ell\}}(v),r)$  and successfully differentiate between the two, contradicting the security of PPRFs. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\nu_{\ell,1}(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid**  $(1, \ell, 1)$  is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\ell-1} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda)) - \nu_{\ell,0}(\lambda) - \nu_{\ell,1}(\lambda)$ .

Lemma 6.5. Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation iO, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (1, ℓ, 1) and Hybrid (1, ℓ, 2) for all 0 ≤ ℓ ≤ log k − 2

Proof. By the same reasoning as in Lemma [6.3](#page-48-0) for key K<sup>2</sup> and programs batchSetup(ℓ) 0 and batchSetup(ℓ) 1 , there exists a negligible function νℓ,2(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid (1, ℓ, 2) is ε(λ) − ( Pℓ−<sup>1</sup> <sup>k</sup>=0 νk(λ) + ηk(λ)) − νℓ,0(λ) − νℓ,1(λ) − νℓ,2(λ).

Lemma 6.6. Assuming the security of PPRF, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (1, ℓ, 2) and Hybrid (1, ℓ, 3) for all 0 ≤ ℓ ≤ log k − 2

Proof. By the same reasoning as Lemma [6.4](#page-48-1) for key K2, there exists a negligible function νℓ,3(λ)

s.t the advantage of the attacker 
$$\mathcal{A}$$
 in **Hybrid (1,**  $\ell$ , **3)** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\ell-1} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda)) - \nu_{\ell,0}(\lambda) - \nu_{\ell,1}(\lambda) - \nu_{\ell,2}(\lambda) - \nu_{\ell,3}(\lambda) = \varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\ell-1} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda)) - \nu_{\ell}(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-50-1"></span>**Lemma 6.7.** Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation i $\mathcal{O}$ , for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid (1, \ell, 3)** and **Hybrid (1, \ell + 1, 0)** for all  $0 \le \ell \le \log k - 2$ 

*Proof.* By the same reasoning as Lemma 6.3 for key 
$$K_1$$
, there exists a negligible function  $\eta_{\ell}(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** (1,  $\ell + 1$ , 0) is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\ell-1} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda)) - \eta_{\ell}(\lambda) = \varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\ell} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda))$ .

**Lemma 6.8.** Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation  $i\mathcal{O}$  and the security of PPRF, for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid** (1,  $\log k - 1$ , 0) and **Hybrid** (1,  $\log k - 1$ , 3).

*Proof.* We follow the sequence of hybrids **Hybrid** (1,  $\log k - 1$ , 0) to **Hybrid** (1,  $\log k - 1$ , 3) using the same reasoning as in Lemma 6.3- Lemma 6.7. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\nu_{\log k - 1}(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** (1,  $\log k - 1$ , 3) is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - (\sum_{k=0}^{\log k - 2} \nu_k(\lambda) + \eta_k(\lambda)) - \nu_{\log k - 1}(\lambda) = \varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$

```
              ii. Sample fhe.pkv and compute ctrv as follows
                                     (fhe.pkv
                                              , fhe.skv) = FHE.KeyGen(1λ
                                                                              ; r
                                                                                ′
                                                                                v
                                                                                 )
                                                   ctrv = FHE.Enc(fhe.pkv
                                                                              , rv; r
                                                                                    ′′
                                                                                    v
                                                                                     )
             iii. Output PKv = (mh.pkv
                                           , fhe.pkv
                                                    , ctrv
                                                         )
```

Figure 4: Program batchSetup(log <sup>k</sup>) 2 : Hardwire fhe.pk<sup>u</sup>

Lemma 6.9. Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation iO, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (1, log k − 1, 3) and Hybrid (2, 0).

Proof. By the same reasoning as in Lemma [6.3](#page-48-0) for key K<sup>1</sup> and programs batchSetup(log <sup>k</sup>) 1 and batchSetup(log <sup>k</sup>) 2 , there exists a negligible function ν2,0(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid (2, 0) is ε(λ) − µ1(λ) − ν2,0(λ).

Lemma 6.10. Assuming the security of PPRF, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (2, 0) and Hybrid (2, 1)

Proof. By the same reasoning as Lemma [6.4](#page-48-1) for key K1, there exists a negligible function ν2,1(λ) s.t advantage of attacker in Hybrid (2, 1) is ε(λ)−µ1(λ)−ν2,0(λ)−ν2,1(λ) = ε(λ)−µ1(λ)−µ2(λ).

{52}------------------------------------------------
