Lemma 6.11. Assuming the security of Indistinguishability Obfuscation iO, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (2, 1) and Hybrid (3, 0).

Proof. By the same reasoning as in Lemma [6.3](#page-48-0) for key K<sup>1</sup> and programs batchSetup(log <sup>k</sup>) 2 and batchSetup(log <sup>k</sup>) 3 , there exists a negligible function ν3,0(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid (3, 0) is ε(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − ν3,0(λ).

Lemma 6.12. Assuming the security of PPRF, for any poly-size attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid (3, 0) and Hybrid (3, 1)

Proof. By the same reasoning as Lemma [6.4](#page-48-1) for key K3, there exists a negligible function ν3,1(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker in Hybrid (3, 1) is ε(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − ν3,0(λ) − ν3,1(λ) = ε(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − µ3(λ).

Lemma 6.13. Assuming the semantic security and evaluation correctness of FHE, collision resistance of HT, index hiding and somewhere argument of knowledge property of seBARGs, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable between all hybrids from Hybrid 4 to Hybrid 9.

{53}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Follows from Lemma 5.9 to Lemma 5.16. **Lemma 6.14.** Assuming the soundness of Commit-and-Measure protocol and the FHM protocol, **Hybrid 9** is a Commit challenge response protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors *Proof.* Follows from Lemma 5.17.

**Lemma 6.15.** The  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{M}_s$ , i.e,  $\mathfrak{M}_s^{\lambda}$  has negligible soundness.

*Proof.* Follows from Lemma 5.18 and Lemma 5.19. Therefore, we must have  $\varepsilon(\lambda) = \text{negl}(\lambda)$  and  $\mathfrak{M}_s^{\lambda}$  is a public coin protocol (except for the first message) BARG that satisfies the semi-adaptive soundness property. 

#### <span id="page-53-0"></span>Monotone Policy Batch Arguments for QMA 7

We first begin by defining a Monotone Policy Batch QMA language as an analogue to the definition presented [BBK<sup>+</sup>23, Section. 4]. Consider any Monotone Policy Batch language  $\mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$  (corresponding quantum relation  $\mathcal{R}$ ) defined using a function f with a Monotone Circuit Family  $\mathfrak{C}$  as follows

$$\mathcal{L}_{f}^{(k)} = \left\{ (x_1, \dots, x_k) : \exists (|\phi_1\rangle, \dots, |\phi_k\rangle) \text{ s.t } f(b_1, \dots, b_k) = 1, \text{ where } b_i = \mathcal{R}(x_i, |\phi_i\rangle) \right\}$$

Let  $C_f \in \mathfrak{C}$  be the circuit computing f. We impose the same restrictions on  $C_f$  as imposed by [BBK<sup>+</sup>23, Section. 4]. Crucially, we bound the width and size of the circuit to  $poly(\lambda)$ . We now, propose the following theorem.

**Theorem 7.1** (Interactive Monotone Policy Batch Arguments for QMA). Assuming the postquantum hardness of the learning with errors problem (LWE), there is a **6-round** private-coin interactive argument system for any monotone policy batch of k T-time quantum computations on inputs  $x_1, \ldots, x_k$  over a function f (corresponding monotone circuit  $C_f$ ) and corresponding  $N = \mathsf{poly}(T)$  qubit witnesses  $|\phi_1\rangle, \ldots, |\phi_k\rangle$ , where

- 1. The Prover P is a QPT algorithm that runs in time poly(k, N)
- 2. The Verifier V is a PPT algorithm that runs in time poly(log k, N)
- 3. The length of each message in the protocol is bounded by  $poly(\log k, N)$

The Interactive argument satisfies the completeness and a **Non-Adaptive** notion of soundness as defined below.

<span id="page-53-2"></span>**Definition 7.2** (Monotone BARG Completeness). For an interactive argument (P,V) with  $k = k(\lambda), N = N(\lambda), \text{ and } x = (x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k) \in \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}, \text{ and any corresponding quantum witnesses}$  $|\phi\rangle = (|\phi_1\rangle, |\phi_2\rangle, \dots, |\phi_k\rangle)$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t it holds for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\Pr\bigl[\operatorname{Out} \left\langle \mathsf{P}(x,|\phi\rangle),\mathsf{V}(x)\right\rangle = 1\bigr] \leq 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

<span id="page-53-1"></span>**Definition 7.3** (Non-Adaptive Soundness). For any  $k = k(\lambda)$ ,  $N = N(\lambda)$ , for any QPT attacker  $\mathsf{P}^{\star}$ , for any function f with a monotone circuit  $C_f$ , any set of instances  $x=(x_1,x_2,\ldots,x_k)\notin\mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$ , there exists a negligible function  $negl(\cdot)$  such that it holds for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,

$$\Pr\bigl[\operatorname{Out} \langle \mathsf{P}^{\star}(x), \mathsf{V}(x) \rangle = 1\bigr] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

{54}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-54-0"></span>7.1 Construction

#### Ingredients

1. Predicate Extractable Hash Family with Tags for bit-fixing predicates PEHT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex, ExtractTag) as in Definition [3.3.](#page-17-0) We modify the ExtractIndex function to output the lexicographically smallest index in J, i.e we define the following procedure:

ExtractIndex(td, v):

- (a) Parse td = J,sk.
- (b) Output (b, j, t) = FHE.Dec(sk, v)
- (c) Output j, or the lexicographically smallest index of J if j /∈ J.
- 2. Predicate Extractable Hash Family PEHash(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex) for the restricted set of bit-fixing predicates with succinct description. Let F be a family of bit-fixing predicates s.t there exists a poly-time TM M<sup>F</sup> that receives an input a circuit C<sup>f</sup> , description d<sup>f</sup> of a predicate f ∈ F and outputs the necessary set and an assignment (J, m). The TM M<sup>F</sup> performs the following operations:
  - (a) Parse d<sup>f</sup> = (x, i), where x ∈ {0, 1} k is an input to the circuit i ∈ [d] is a layer in the circuit C<sup>f</sup> .
  - (b) Let (b ⋆ 1 , b<sup>⋆</sup> 2 , . . . , b<sup>⋆</sup> <sup>M</sup>) be the values on all wires of C<sup>f</sup> . Here, M = |C<sup>f</sup> |.
  - (c) Let J ⊆ M be the set of wires in the i th layer of C<sup>f</sup> whose values b <sup>⋆</sup> are 0, and let m ∈ {0, 1} <sup>J</sup> be the all-zero vector
  - (d) Output (J, m)
- 3. Somewhere Extractable BARG or seBARG(Setup, Prove, Verify, Extract). We use of an instance of seBARG where Setup supports a constant number of indices instead of just one.
- 4. Commit-and-Measure Protocol Maha(Gen, Com, Open,Test, Out) as defined in Definition [3.21.](#page-25-0)
- 5. The κ-fold FHM post hoc verification protocol (PFHM, VFHM) as defined in Definition [3.30](#page-28-0) where κ is a polynomial in instance length.

To simplify the presentation, we make use of the following languages.

Definition 7.4 (Consistency Language). A consistency language Nvk1,vk<sup>2</sup> is defined as follows

$$\mathcal{N}_{\mathsf{vk}_1,\mathsf{vk}_2} = \big\{ (i,\mathsf{v}_1,\mathsf{v}_2) \mid \exists \ b,\alpha,\beta \ \text{such that PEHT.Verify}(\mathsf{vk}_1,\mathsf{v}_1,i,b,\alpha) = 1 \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT.Verify}(\mathsf{vk}_2,\mathsf{v}_2,i,b,\beta) = 1 \big\}$$

Note that we have overloaded the PEHT.Verify function to not need the tags. With this language, we want to ensure that the string used to compute v<sup>1</sup> and v<sup>2</sup> is the same. Let R<sup>N</sup> denote a relation for Nvk1,vk<sup>2</sup> s.t R<sup>N</sup> (i, v1, v2) = 1 iff (i, v1, v2) ∈ Nvk1,vk<sup>2</sup> . Let the corresponding circuit encoding this relation be C<sup>N</sup> .

Definition 7.5 (Batch Index Satisfiability TM χ). We define a TM χ(z, j, ω<sup>j</sup> ) for an input z = (C, x, pT.vk, pT.v, pH.vk, pH.v, h, c, r), to perform the following computations:

1. Parse z = (C, x, pT.vk, pT.v, pH.vk, pH.v, h, c, r) and x = (x1, x2, . . . , xk).

{55}------------------------------------------------

2. Parse the following

$$\begin{aligned} p\mathsf{T.vk} &= (\mathsf{pT.vk}_1, \mathsf{pT.vk}_2) \\ p\mathsf{H.vk} &= (\mathsf{pH.vk}_1, \mathsf{pH.vk}_2) \\ p\mathsf{H.v} &= (\mathsf{pH.v}_1, \mathsf{pH.v}_2) \\ \mathsf{h} &= (\mathsf{h}_1, \mathsf{h}_2) \end{aligned}$$

3. If  $1 \le j \le k$ :

- (a) Parse  $\omega_j = (w_j, b_j, \mathsf{op}_i, \rho_i^{(1)}, \rho_i^{(2)})$  where  $w_j = (\mathsf{op}_i^{(1)}, \mathsf{op}_i^{(2)}, t_i^{(1)}, t_i^{(2)}, b_i')$ .
- (b) Check if PEHT. Verify(pT.vk<sub>2</sub>, pT.v,  $j, b_j, w_j, op_j = 1$
- (c) Check if PEHash. Verify (pH.vk<sub>\beta</sub>, pH.v<sub>\beta</sub>, j, b<sub>j</sub>,  $\rho_j^{(\beta)}$ , C) = 1 for  $\beta \in [2]$ .
- (d) Check if PEHT. Verify  $(pT.vk_1, h_{\beta}, j, b'_j, op_j^{(\beta)}) = 1$  for  $\beta \in [2]$
- (e) If  $b'_{j} = 1$ , check if VerifyInstance $(x_{j}, t_{j}^{(1)}, t_{j}^{(2)}, c, r) = 1$
- 4. If  $k < j \le M$ :
  - (a) Compute the  $j^{\text{th}}$  gate of C,  $G_j = (j, j_1, j_2, g \in \{\text{AND, OR}\})$ .
  - (b) Parse  $\omega_j = \left(b_j, b_{j_1}, b_{j_2}, \left(\rho_j^{(\beta)}, \rho_{j_1}^{(\beta)}, \rho_{j_2}^{(\beta)}\right)_{\beta \in [2]}\right)$ .
  - (c) Check if PEHash. Verify (pH.vk<sub>\beta</sub>, pH.v<sub>\beta</sub>, j, b<sub>j</sub>,  $\rho_j^{(\beta)}$ , C) = 1 for  $\beta \in [2]$ .
  - (d) Check if PEHash. Verify (pH.vk<sub>\beta</sub>, pH.v<sub>\beta</sub>,  $j_{\alpha}$ ,  $b_{j_{\alpha}}$ ,  $\rho_{j_{\alpha}}^{(\beta)}$ , C) = 1 for  $\alpha, \beta \in [2]$
  - (e) Check if  $b_j = g(b_{j_1}, b_{j_2})$
- 5. If j = M is the output wire, check that  $b_j = 1$

Clearly, the description of  $\chi$  is constant. Compute  $\tau > \mathsf{poly}(M,k)$  large enough that the pseudocode above terminates.

**Definition 7.6** (Monotone Circuit Evaluation Language). A Monotone Circuit Evaluation language  $\mathcal{K}_{\chi}$  is the set of all instances that is accepted by the Turing machine  $\chi$  in time  $\tau$ .

#### **Protocol Specification**

- Setup $(1^{\lambda}, 1^{k}, 1^{N}, f) \to (crs, vk)$ : Let  $C_f$  be the monotone circuit for f.
  - Setup the commit-and-measure protocol Maha. Sample randomness  $r \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  and subsequently sample the basis string h.

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}) = \mathsf{Maha}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, h; r)$$

{56}------------------------------------------------

– For i ∈ [2], setup PEHT<sup>i</sup> as follows for any random subset J ⊂ [k] and m = 0<sup>J</sup>

$$\begin{split} (\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_i,\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk}_i,\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_i) &\leftarrow \mathsf{PEHT}_i.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,k,J,m) \\ \mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk} &= (\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_1,\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_2) \\ \mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk} &= (\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk}_1,\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk}_2) \\ \mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td} &= (\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1,\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_2) \end{split}$$

We have the tag length for (pT.hk1, pT.vk1, pT.td1) being set to N, the number of qubits in a single copy of the witness state. The tag length for (pT.hk2, pT.vk2, pT.td2) is bounded by a number T = O(N · poly(λ)).

– Let M = |C<sup>f</sup> | and df<sup>i</sup> is the description of an arbitrary predicate f<sup>i</sup> ∈ F. For i ∈ [2], generate

$$\begin{aligned} (\mathsf{pH.hk}_i, \mathsf{pH.vk}_i, \mathsf{pH.td}_i) &\leftarrow \mathsf{PEHash.Gen}(1^\lambda, M, d_{f_i}) \\ \mathsf{pH.hk} &= (\mathsf{pH.hk}_1, \mathsf{pH.hk}_2) \\ \mathsf{pH.vk} &= (\mathsf{pH.vk}_1, \mathsf{pH.vk}_2) \\ \mathsf{pH.td} &= (\mathsf{pH.td}_1, \mathsf{pH.td}_2) \end{aligned}$$

– Generate sB.crs<sup>1</sup> for seBARG<sup>1</sup> with any index j ∈ J

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.td}_1) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_{\mathcal{N}}|}, j)$$

Generate sB.crs<sup>2</sup> for seBARG<sup>2</sup> for the Monotone circuit evaluation language K<sup>χ</sup> and set I = {1, 2, N}

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \mathsf{sB.td}_2) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}, 1^M, 1^T, I)$$

Compute

$$\mathsf{sB.crs} = (\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \mathsf{sB.crs}_2), \qquad \mathsf{sB.td} = (\mathsf{sB.td}_1, \mathsf{sB.td}_2)$$

– Outputs the following.

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{crs} &= (\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{pT.hk}, \mathsf{pT.vk}, \mathsf{pH.hk}, \mathsf{pH.vk}, \mathsf{sB.crs}) \\ \mathsf{vk} &= (\mathsf{mh}, \mathsf{sk}, r) \end{split}$$

- Prove(crs, {xj}j∈[k] , {|ϕ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ ⊗κ }j∈[k] ): Here, the quantum state |ϕi⟩ is the quantum witness to the statement x<sup>i</sup> for some L ∈ QMA. The prover computes the string s s.t s<sup>i</sup> = (x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes) s.t f(s) = 1 and proceeds as follows:
  - Round 1: Parse crs to get (pT.hk1, mh.pk). If s<sup>j</sup> = 0, set y<sup>j</sup> =⊥, else compute

$$\begin{aligned} |\psi_j\rangle &\leftarrow \mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_j, |\phi_j\rangle^{\otimes \kappa}) \\ (y_j, |\mathsf{st}_j\rangle) &\leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Com}(\mathsf{mh.pk}, |\psi_j\rangle) \end{aligned}$$

Finally calculate h<sup>y</sup> as follows and send to the verifier.

$$\mathsf{h}_y = \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_1, s, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]})$$

{57}------------------------------------------------

- Round 3: Receive a bit  $c \in \{0,1\}$  from the verifier. Parse crs to get sB.crs<sub>1</sub>. Let

$$z_{j} = \begin{cases} \bot & s_{j} = 0, \ \mathsf{Maha.Open}(\ket{\mathsf{st}_{j}}, c) & s_{j} = 1 \end{cases}$$

Compute  $h_z$  as follows:

$$h_z = \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_1, s, \{z_i\}_{i \in [k]})$$

Generate the seBARG<sub>1</sub> proof and send  $(h_z, \pi_1)$  to the verifier.

$$\begin{aligned} &\forall j, \ \mathsf{op}_{j}^{y} &= \mathsf{PEHT}_{1}.\mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{pT.hk}_{1}, s, \{y_{j}\}_{j \in [k]}, j) \\ &\forall j, \ \mathsf{op}_{j}^{z} &= \mathsf{PEHT}_{1}.\mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{pT.hk}_{1}, s, \{z_{j}\}_{j \in [k]}, j) \\ &\forall j, \ v_{j} &= (\mathsf{op}_{j}^{y}, \mathsf{op}_{j}^{z}, y_{j}, z_{j}, s_{j}) \\ &\pi_{1} &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_{1}.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_{1}, \{(j, \mathsf{h}_{y}, \mathsf{h}_{z})\}_{j \in [k]}, \{v_{j}\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{aligned}$$

- Round 5: Receive  $r \in \{0,1\}^{\lambda}$  from the verifier. Perform the following
  - 1. Compute the values of all the wires of circuit  $C_f$ . Let these values be  $b_1, \ldots, b_M$  with the first k bits is set to  $s_i$ , i.e  $b_i = s_i$  for all  $i \in [k]$ .
  - 2. Parse crs to get  $(pT.hk_2, pT.vk_2, pH.hk_1, pH.hk_2, pH.vk_1, pH.vk_2, sB.crs_2)$
  - 3. Compute the following

$$\begin{aligned} \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]} &= \{(\mathsf{op}_j^y, y_j, \mathsf{op}_j^z, z_j, s_j)\}_{j \in [k]} \\ \mathsf{h}_w &= \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}_2, s, \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{aligned}$$

4. Compute the following

$$\begin{aligned} \mathbf{v}_i &= \mathsf{PEHash.Hash}(\mathsf{pH.hk}_i, (b_1, b_2, \dots, b_M), C_f), \qquad i \in [2] \\ \mathsf{pH.v} &= (\mathsf{v}_1, \mathsf{v}_2) \end{aligned}$$

5. For  $1 \leq j \leq k$ , perform the following tasks

$$\begin{aligned} &\mathsf{op}_j^w = \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Open}(\mathsf{pT.hk}_2, s, \{w_j\}_{j \in [k]}, j) \\ &\rho_j^{(i)} = \mathsf{PEHash.Open}(\mathsf{pH.hk}_i, b_j, j, C_f), \quad i \in [2] \end{aligned}$$

For  $k < j \le M$ , perform the following tasks for gate  $G_j = (j, j_1, j_2, g)$ 

$$\begin{array}{ll} \rho_j^{(i)} &= \mathsf{PEHash.Open}(\mathsf{pH.hk}_i, b_j, j, C_f), \quad i \in [2] \\ \rho_{j_\alpha}^{(i)} &= \mathsf{PEHash.Open}(\mathsf{pH.hk}_i, b_{j_\alpha}, j_\alpha, C_f), \quad \alpha, i \in [2] \end{array}$$

Construct the witness  $\omega_j$  for all  $j \in [M]$  as follows

$$\omega_{j} = \begin{cases} (w_{j}, s_{j}, \mathsf{op}_{j}^{w}, \rho_{j}^{(1)}, \rho_{j}^{(2)}) & 1 \leq j \leq k \\ \left(b_{j}, b_{j_{1}}, b_{j_{2}}, \left(\rho_{j}^{(\beta)}, \rho_{j_{1}}^{(\beta)}, \rho_{j_{2}}^{(\beta)}\right)_{\beta \in [2]}\right) & k < j \leq M \end{cases}$$

{58}------------------------------------------------

6. Compute the  $seBARG_2$  proof as follows

$$\begin{split} x &= (x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k) \\ \mathbf{h} &= (\mathbf{h}_y, \mathbf{h}_z) \\ \zeta &= (C_f, x, \mathsf{pT.vk}, \mathbf{h}_w, \mathsf{pH.vk}, \mathsf{pH.v}, \mathbf{h}, c, r) \\ \pi_2 &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Prove}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2, \{(j, \zeta)\}_{j \in [M]}, \{\omega_j\}_{j \in [M]}) \end{split}$$

7. Compute the consistency proof. Send  $(h_w, v_1, v_2, \pi_2, \pi_3)$  to verifier

$$\begin{aligned} \{u_j\}_{j \in [k]} &= \{(\mathsf{op}_j^w, \mathsf{op}_j^z, w_j, z_j, s_j)\}_{j \in [k]} \\ \pi_3 &\leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1, \{(j, \mathsf{h}_w, \mathsf{h}_z)\}_{j \in [k]}, \{u_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{aligned}$$

- Verify(crs, vk,  $\{x_j\}_{j\in[k]}$ ,  $h_y$ ,  $h_z$ ,  $h_w$ ,  $v_1$ ,  $v_2$ ,  $\pi_1$ ,  $\pi_2$ ,  $\pi_3$ ): Works as follows under normal (honest) execution.
  - Round 2: Receive  $h_y$  from prover. Sample bit c and send it to the prover.
  - Round 4: Receive  $h_z$  from prover. Parse crs to get  $sB.crs_1$  and check if

$$seBARG_1.Verify(sB.crs_1, \{(j, h_u, h_z)\}, \pi_1) = 1$$

If yes, parse vk and send r to the prover. Else, reject.

- **Final Verification**: Receive  $(h_w, v_1, v_2, \pi_2, \pi_3)$  from the prover. Parse crs to get  $(sB.crs_2, pT.vk_2, pH.vk_1, pH.vk_2)$ . Construct  $\zeta$  as presented previously. Accept if

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1,\{(j,\mathsf{h}_z,\mathsf{h}_w)\}_{j\in[k]},\pi_3) &= 1\\ \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2,\{(j,\zeta)\}_{j\in[M]},\pi_2) &= 1 \end{split}$$

Otherwise, reject.

**Remark 7.7.** We denote the above protocol as  $\mathfrak{R}$  and  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  is a  $\lambda$ -fold repetition.

#### <span id="page-58-0"></span>7.2 Security Analysis

**Completeness** For any monotone policy f for the instances  $x = (x_1, \ldots, x_k)$  the interaction results in the verifier accepting with non-negligible probability if  $x \in \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$  by the completeness properties of the underlying primitives. The completeness of  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  follows by a union-bound argument.

Round Complexity We observe that  $\mathfrak{R}$  and (therefore)  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  both have 5 rounds with setup. Alternatively, the verifier can do the setup and crs can be sent to the prover as the verifier's first message to get a 6 round Private Coin Interactive Monotone Policy BARG for QMA.

<span id="page-58-1"></span>Before we proceed to prove soundness, we define the following property for PEHTs.

**Definition 7.8.** A Predicate Extractable Hash Family with Tags w.r.t a bit-fixing predicate class  $\mathcal{F}$  satisfies the following property

{59}------------------------------------------------

**Lexicographically Smallest Index Extraction**: For any poly-sized adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , for any polynomial  $N = N(\lambda)$  and any bit fixing predicate described by a set  $J \subset [N]$  and string  $y \in \{0,1\}^J$ , for any two indices  $i_1, i_2 \in J$  s.t  $i_1 < i_2$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t for any  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ ,

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} \mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{td},\mathsf{v}) = i_2 \ \land \\ \mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{v},i_1,1-y_{i_1},t,\rho) = 1 \end{array} \right. : \begin{array}{l} (\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk},\mathsf{td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda,N,J,y) \\ (\mathsf{v},i_1,t,\rho) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{hk},\mathsf{vk}) \end{array} \right] \leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

<span id="page-59-0"></span>**Lemma 7.9.** Assuming the correctness of evaluation of FHE, the function PEHT.ExtractIndex(td, v) outputs the lexicographically smallest index  $i \in J$  as defined in Definition 7.8.

Proof. We refer the reader to the construction of the PEHT [BBK<sup>+</sup>23, Section. 5]. By the modification introduced in our construction, when PEHT.Extract(td, v) = 1, we always return the lexicographically smallest index. We are left to show that we do the same when PEHT.Extract(td, v) = 0. Now, for contradiction, assume that the output of ExtractIndex is an index  $i_2 \in J$  that is not the lexicographically smallest index (say the index is  $i_1$ , i.e.,  $i_1 < i_2$ ). Let v be the longest common prefix in the binary representations of  $i_1$  and  $i_2$ . At node v, both children  $v \parallel 0$  and  $v \parallel 1$  must return  $(0, i_1, t_{i_1})$  and  $(0, i_2, t_{i_2})$ , respectively. To compute the ciphertext at v, the function evaluated under FHE must choose the minimum index since both  $i_1, i_2 \in J$ . If ExtractIndex outputs  $i_2$ , we contradict the correctness of FHE.

**Soundness** The protocol  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  satisfies the notion of **non-adaptive soundness** as defined in Definition 7.3. We now provide the soundness proof.

**Theorem 7.10.** Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure protocol (Definition 3.23) and the FHM protocol (Definition 3.32), computational binding and extraction correctness of tag and index of PEHT, PEHash, and somewhere argument of knowledge property of seBARG, the protocol  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  satisfies non-adaptive computational soundness as defined in Definition 7.3.

Proof. The proof strategy for the construction proceeds as follows. First, we note that  $\mathfrak{R}$  is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol as per Definition 3.24. For non-adaptive soundness, the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  and attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  are given the instance  $x=(x_1,x_2,\ldots,x_k)\notin\mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$  and the monotone circuit  $C_f$  pair  $(x,C_f)$ . Additionally, the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  also receives the necessary set  $J^*\subset [k]$  for  $C_f$  as non-uniform advice. The necessary set  $J^*$  contains a subset of indices in [k] of which one (say i) must satisfy  $\mathcal{R}(x_i,|\phi_i\rangle)=1$  for  $C_f(x)=1$ . We say that the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  wins in the entire security game if the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  accepts for the pair  $(x,C_f)$  even though  $C_f(x)=0$ .

We set up a sequence of hybrids to show the consistency between the hash values in **Round 1** and **Round 3**. Then, we show that the extractions in **Round 5** are valid via a proof that follows the one presented in [BBK<sup>+</sup>23]. We argue further that the hashes calculated in **Round 3** and **Round 5** are consistent with each other. Finally, we show that the final hybrid has a negligible advantage over the original protocol. We proceed as follows:

**Hybrid 0**: Normal execution of the protocol

{60}------------------------------------------------

**Hybrid 1**: Same as previous execution except that we now enable extraction on the necessary set  $J^*$  for  $PEHT_1$ , i.e

$$(\mathsf{pT.hk}_1^{\star}, \mathsf{pT.vk}_1^{\star}, \mathsf{pT.td}_1^{\star}) \leftarrow \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}, k, J^{\star}, 0^{J^{\star}})$$

**Hybrid 2**: Same as previous execution, except we add the following check in **Round 4** of the protocol

$$\mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y) = 0 \land \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_z) = 0$$

**Hybrid 3**: We now update the protocol by extracting the tags and indices in **Round 4** on the verifier's end. Reject if  $j_1, j_2 \notin J^*$ 

$$\begin{split} j_1 &= \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{h}_y) \quad y_{j_1}^\star = \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{h}_y) \\ j_2 &= \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{h}_z) \quad z_{j_2}^\star = \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{h}_z) \end{split}$$

**Hybrid 4**: We reject if  $j_1 \neq j_2$  in Round 4.

**Hybrid 5**: Same as previous execution except that we now enable extraction on the necessary set  $J^*$  for PEHT<sub>2</sub>, i.e.

$$(\mathsf{pT.hk}_2^{\star}, \mathsf{pT.vk}_2^{\star}, \mathsf{pT.td}_2^{\star}) \leftarrow \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda}, k, J^{\star}, 0^J)$$

**Hybrid 6**: Same as previous execution except we add the following check in the final verification round.

$$\mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star,\mathsf{h}_w) = 0$$

**Hybrid 7**: We update the protocol by extracting the tag and index in the final verification round. Reject if  $j_3 \notin J^*$ 

$$j_3 = \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT.td}_2^\star, \mathsf{h}_w)$$
 
$$w_{j_3} = (\mathsf{op}_{j_3}^{y\star}, \mathsf{op}_{j_3}^{z\star}, y_{j_3}^{\star\prime}, z_{j_3}^{\star\prime}, s_{j_3}^\star) = \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{pT.td}_2^\star, \mathsf{h}_w)$$

**Hybrid 8**: We update the protocol to reject if any of the checks are false.

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y,j_3,s_{j_3}^\star,\mathsf{op}_{j_3}^{y\star}) &= 1 \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_z,j_3,s_{j_3}^\star,\mathsf{op}_{j_3}^{z\star}) &= 1 \land \\ \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_{j_3},y_{j_3}^{\star\prime},z_{j_3}^{\star\prime},c,r) &= 1 \land s_{j_3}^\star &= 1 \end{split}$$

**Hybrid 9**: We reject if  $j_2 \neq j_3$  and  $j_1 \neq j_2$  in the final verification round.

We show that **Hybrid 9** is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol that has orthogonal projectors. We then show that the attacker's advantage has changed negligibly from **Hybrid 0** to **Hybrid 9**, ensuring that  $\mathfrak{R}^{\lambda}$  cannot have non-negligible soundness. To begin, let us assume that the attacker's advantage at **Hybrid 0** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ . We wish to show that  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$  is a negligible function.

<span id="page-60-0"></span>**Lemma 7.11.** Assuming the predicate hiding property of  $PEHT_1$ , for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**.

{61}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* Let us assume that the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 1** has a non-negligible advantage over an attacker in **Hybrid 0**. We construct a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  to break the predicate-hiding property of PEHT using  $\mathcal{A}$ . Let  $\mathcal{C}$  be the challenger for the predicate hiding property for PEHT<sub>1</sub>. We perform the following operations

$$\begin{split} J &\leftarrow \mathcal{B}(1^k) \\ (\mathsf{pT.hk}_1', \mathsf{pT.vk}_1') &\leftarrow \mathcal{C}(J, J^\star) \\ (\mathsf{h}_v', \mathsf{h}_z') &\leftarrow \mathcal{A}(\mathsf{pT.hk}_1', \mathsf{pT.vk}_1') \end{split}$$

If the challenger accepts in the final round, then  $\mathcal{B}$  guesses that  $J^*$  was used to generate  $(\mathsf{pT.hk}_1', \mathsf{pT.vk}_1')$  rather than J contradicting the predicate hiding property of  $\mathsf{PEHT}_1$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_1(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in  $\mathbf{Hybrid}\ \mathbf{1}$  is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$ 

<span id="page-61-0"></span>**Lemma 7.12.** Assuming extraction correctness of PEHT<sub>1</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2** 

Proof. Let us assume that the attacker in **Hybrid 2** has non-negligible advantage over the attacker in **Hybrid 1**. Since  $x \notin \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$  and  $C_f(x) = 0$ , we can allow a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  to interact trivially with the attacker in **Hybrid 2** to break the extraction correctness property of PEHT. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_2(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 2** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ 

<span id="page-61-1"></span>**Lemma 7.13.** Assuming index extraction correctness of PEHT<sub>1</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage doesn't change in **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3** 

*Proof.* Any attacker that can output indices  $j_1, j_2 \notin J$  must break the index extraction correctness property of PEHT. As the probability of this event is 0, the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** 3 is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ 

<span id="page-61-2"></span>**Lemma 7.14.** Assuming index hiding property and somewhere argument of knowledge properties for  $seBARG_1$ , consistency of extraction, and consistency of tag extraction for  $PEHT_1$ , for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 3** and **Hybrid 4**.

Proof. For contradiction, assume that the advantage of the attacker between **Hybrid 3** and **Hybrid 4** is  $\mu_3(\lambda)$ , a non-negligible function. Let the advantage in the individual hybrids be  $\delta_3(\lambda)$  and  $\delta_4(\lambda)$  respectively, s.t  $\mu_3(\lambda) = \delta_4(\lambda) - \delta_3(\lambda)$ . We say that  $\mathcal{A}$  wins if the attacker can output two indices  $j_1, j_2 \in [k]$  in **Hybrid 3** s.t  $j_1 \neq j_2$  in **Hybrid 4**. We define a series of experiments that are performed by the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  in both the hybrids. We denote each experiment as  $\mathsf{Expt}_{x,0}$  denoting that the experiment is performed on **Hybrid x**. They are as follows

- 1. Expt<sub>x,0</sub>: Update **Hybrid** x by guessing an index  $i^* \in [k]$  s.t  $i^* = \min(j_1, j_2)$ . Abort otherwise.
- 2.  $\mathsf{Expt}_{x,1}$ : Update the previous experiment and enable extraction for  $\mathsf{seBARG}_1$  on index  $i^\star$ , i.e

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_1^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_1.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^k, 1^{|\mathcal{C}_\mathcal{N}|}, i^\star)$$

{62}------------------------------------------------

3.  $\mathsf{Expt}_{x,2}$ : Update the previous experiment to extract the witness for the seBARG proof  $\pi_1$ 

$$(\mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{y\prime}, \mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{z\prime}, y_{i^\star}^\prime, z_{i^\star}^\prime, s_{i^\star}^\prime) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{sB.td}_1^\star, \{(j, \mathsf{h}_y, \mathsf{h}_z)\}_{j \in [k]}, \pi_1)$$

Without loss of generality, let  $i^* = j_1$ , i.e we assume that  $j_1 < j_2$ . We verify the validity of the witness at the end of **Round 4**. Abort otherwise

PEHT<sub>1</sub>.Verify(pT.vk<sub>1</sub>\*, h<sub>y</sub>, 
$$i^*$$
,  $s'_{i^*}$ , op<sup>y'</sup> <sub>$i^*$</sub> ) = 1  
PEHT<sub>1</sub>.Verify(pT.vk<sub>1</sub>\*, h<sub>z</sub>,  $i^*$ ,  $s'_{i^*}$ , op<sup>z'</sup> <sub>$i^*$</sub> ) = 1

4.  $\mathsf{Expt}_{x,3}$ : Update the previous experiment by aborting if  $s'_{i^\star} = 0$ .

Claim 7.15. The advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  between experiments  $\mathsf{Expt}_{3,0}$  and  $\mathsf{Expt}_{4,0}$  is given by

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,0}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,0}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\mu_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|}$$

*Proof.* This follows from a simple probability argument since  $i^*$  is essentially chosen uniformly at random from the set  $J^*$  and  $|J^*| \leq k$ .

<span id="page-62-0"></span>Claim 7.16. Assuming index hiding property of seBARGs, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in  $\mathsf{Expt}_{4,1}$  and  $\mathsf{Expt}_{3,1}$ , i.e there is a negligible function  $\nu_1(\lambda)$  s.t

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,1}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,1}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\mu_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_1(\lambda)$$

*Proof.* For any negligible function  $\nu_{1,0}(\lambda)$ , we have the following

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,1}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,0}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\delta_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_{1,0}(\lambda)$$

following from the proof of Lemma 4.7. Similarly, we can show that there exists a negligible function  $\nu_{1,1}(\lambda)$  s.t

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,1}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,0}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\delta_4(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_{1,1}(\lambda)$$

The claim follows from a simple triangle inequality with  $\nu_1(\lambda) = \nu_{1,1}(\lambda) - \nu_{1,0}(\lambda)$ .

Claim 7.17. Assuming the somewhere argument of knowledge property of seBARG<sub>1</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in  $\mathsf{Expt}_{4,2}$  and  $\mathsf{Expt}_{3,2}$ , i.e there is a negligible function  $\nu_2(\lambda)$  s.t

$$\left| \Pr \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,2}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \Pr \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,2}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\mu_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_1(\lambda) - \nu_2(\lambda)$$

*Proof.* Follows similarly from the proof of Lemma 4.8 and Claim 7.16.

Claim 7.18. Assuming the consistency of extraction property of PEHT<sub>1</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in  $\mathsf{Expt}_{4,3}$  and  $\mathsf{Expt}_{3,3}$ , i.e there is a negligible function  $\nu_3(\lambda)$  s.t

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,3}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,3}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \ge \frac{\mu_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_1(\lambda) - \nu_2(\lambda) - \nu_3(\lambda)$$

{63}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* We first consider a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that interacts with a challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  for  $\mathsf{Expt}_{3,3}$ .  $\mathcal{B}$  produces an opening  $(\mathsf{h}_y, i^\star, y'_{i^\star}, \mathsf{op}^{y\prime}_{i^\star})$  by interacting trivially with  $\mathcal{A}$  s.t we have

$$\Pr\left[\begin{array}{l} \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y) = i^\star \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y,i^\star,s_{i^\star}',y_{i^\star}',\mathsf{op}_{i^\star}^{y\prime}) = 1 \end{array}\right] \geq \frac{\delta_3(\lambda)}{|J^\star|} - \nu_1(\lambda) - \nu_2(\lambda)$$

contradicting the consistency of extraction property of PEHT<sub>1</sub>. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\nu_{3,0}(\lambda)$  s.t

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,3}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{3,2}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\delta_3(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_{1,0}(\lambda) - \nu_{2,0}(\lambda) - \nu_{3,0}(\lambda)$$

Similarly, we have

$$\left| \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,3}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] - \mathsf{Pr} \big[ \mathsf{Expt}_{4,2}^{\mathcal{A}} \big] \right| \geq \frac{\delta_4(\lambda)}{|J^{\star}|} - \nu_{1,1}(\lambda) - \nu_{2,1}(\lambda) - \nu_{3,1}(\lambda)$$

The claim follows by a simple triangle inequality with  $\nu_3(\lambda) = \nu_{3,1}(\lambda) - \nu_{3,0}(\lambda)$ .

Claim 7.19.  $\mu_3(\lambda)$  must be a negligible function.

*Proof.* Follows from Lemma 7.9, since  $s'_{i^*}$  is the same across both  $h_y$  and  $h_z$  and  $i^*, j_2 \in J$ .

Therefore, the advantage of attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 4** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 7.20.** Assuming the predicate hiding property of PEHT<sub>2</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 4** and **Hybrid 5**.

*Proof.* Follows from the proof of Lemma 7.11. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_4(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 5** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 7.21.** Assuming extraction correctness of PEHT<sub>2</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 5** and **Hybrid 6** 

*Proof.* Follows from the proof of Lemma 7.12. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_5(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 6** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \mu_5(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 7.22.** Assuming index extraction correctness of PEHT<sub>1</sub>, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage doesn't change in **Hybrid 6** and **Hybrid 7** 

*Proof.* Follows from Lemma 7.13. The advantage of the attacker 
$$\mathcal{A}$$
 in **Hybrid 7** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda) - \mu_4(\lambda) - \mu_5(\lambda)$ .

<span id="page-63-0"></span>**Lemma 7.23.** Assuming the index hiding and extraction correctness properties of  $seBARG_2$ , extraction consistency, and consistency of tag extraction properties of  $PEHT_2$  and the predicate hiding property of PEHash, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in  $\mathbf{Hybrid}\ \mathbf{7}$  and  $\mathbf{Hybrid}\ \mathbf{8}$ 

{64}------------------------------------------------

Proof. The proof for the above lemma amounts to proving somewhere argument of knowledge for Monotone Policy Batch SNARGs where the witness is the extraction output of PEHT2.ExtractTag. We provide the proof in Appendix [B](#page-84-0) (see also [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23, Theorem. 7.1]). Therefore, there exists a negligible function µ6(λ) s.t, the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid 8 is ε(λ)−µ1(λ)−µ2(λ)− µ3(λ) − µ4(λ) − µ5(λ) − µ6(λ).

Lemma 7.24. Assuming index hiding property and somewhere argument of knowledge properties for seBARG1, extraction correctness, consistency of extraction, for any QPT attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid 8 and Hybrid 9.

Proof. This follows from the proof of Lemma [7.14](#page-61-2) with the edit that we use both pT.vk<sup>⋆</sup> <sup>1</sup> and pT.vk<sup>⋆</sup> 2 to check the extraction correctness property of seBARG1. Therefore, there exists a negligible function µ7(λ) s.t the advantage of attacker A in Hybrid 9 is ε(λ)−µ1(λ)−µ2(λ)−µ3(λ)−µ4(λ)− µ5(λ) − µ6(λ) − µ7(λ).

Lemma 7.25. Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure Protocol (Maha) and the FHM protocol, Hybrid 9 (R′ ) is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors.

Proof. This follows from Lemma [4.10](#page-34-2) where we replace the special index i <sup>⋆</sup> with the index j = j<sup>1</sup> = j<sup>2</sup> = j<sup>3</sup> in the final round of the protocol.

Lemma 7.26. The λ-fold repetition of Hybrid 9, i.e (R′ ) λ , has negligible soundness.

Proof. This follows from Theorem [3.27.](#page-27-1)

Lemma 7.27. The λ-fold repetition of R, i.e R<sup>λ</sup> , has negligible soundness.

Proof. The proof follows the proof of Lemma [4.12](#page-35-1) by replacing seBARG index i <sup>⋆</sup> with the index j = j<sup>1</sup> = j<sup>2</sup> = j<sup>3</sup> which is extracted at the final verification round. Therefore, we note that R<sup>λ</sup> satisfies the non-adaptive soundness property as defined in Definition [7.3](#page-53-1)

# <span id="page-64-0"></span>8 Public Coin Monotone Policy Batch Arguments for QMA

In this section, we outline a public coin monotone policy batch argument system for QMA. The protocol is public coin except for the verifier's first message, i.e. the setup phase. This property allows for round collapsing under the Quantum Random Oracle Model (see Section [9\)](#page-69-0). More formally, this result can be stated as follows:

Theorem 8.1 (Designated Verifier Monotone Policy Batch Arguments for QMA). Assuming the post-quantum hardness of the learning with errors problem (LWE), there is a 6-round publiccoin designated verifier interactive argument system for any monotone policy batch of k T-time quantum computations on inputs x1, . . . , x<sup>k</sup> over a function f (corresponding monotone circuit C<sup>f</sup> ) and corresponding N = poly(T) qubit witnesses |ϕ1⟩, . . . , |ϕk⟩, where

- 1. The Prover P is a QPT algorithm that runs in time poly(k, N)
- 2. The Verifier V is a PPT algorithm that runs in time poly(log k, N)

{65}------------------------------------------------

3. The length of each message in the protocol is bounded by poly(log k, N)

The public coin protocol satisfies the completeness as defined in Definition [7.2](#page-53-2) and a Non-Adaptive notion of soundness as defined in Definition [7.3.](#page-53-1)

## <span id="page-65-0"></span>8.1 Construction

#### Ingredients

- 1. Predicate Extractable Hash Family with Tags for bit-fixing predicates PEHT(Gen, Hash, Open, Verify, Extract, ExtractIndex, ExtractTag).
- 2. Fully Homomorphic Encryption or FHE(KeyGen, Eval, Enc, Dec)
- 3. ϵ-state preserving Arguments of Knowledge AoK as defined in Definition [3.16.](#page-23-2)
- 4. Commit-and-Measure Protocol Maha(Gen, Com, Open,Test, Out) as defined in Definition [3.21.](#page-25-0)
- 5. The κ-fold FHM post hoc verification protocol (PFHM, VFHM) as defined in Definition [3.30](#page-28-0) where κ is a polynomial in instance length.

#### Protocol Specification

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> , 1 k , 1 <sup>N</sup> , f) → (crs, vk): Let C<sup>f</sup> be the monotone circuit for the predicate f.
  - Setup the commit-and-measure protocol Maha. Sample randomness r \$← {0, 1} <sup>λ</sup> and subsequently sample the basis string h using the randomness r.

$$(\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{mh.sk}) = \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^{\lambda}; r)$$

– Setup PEHT as follows for any random subset J ⊂ [k] and m = 0<sup>J</sup>

$$(\mathsf{pT.hk}, \mathsf{pT.vk}, \mathsf{pT.td}) \leftarrow \mathsf{PEHT.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, k, J, m)$$

We have the tag length for (pT.hk, pT.vk, pT.td) being set to N.

– Setup FHE as follows

$$(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{fhe.sk}) \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE.KeyGen}(1^{\lambda})$$

Compute the following

$$\mathsf{ct}_r \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk},r)$$

- Sample the AoK hash key aok.hk
- Outputs the following.

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{crs} &= (\mathsf{mh.pk}, \mathsf{pT.hk}, \mathsf{pT.vk}, \mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{ct}_r, \mathsf{aok.hk}) \ \mathsf{vk} &= (\mathsf{fhe.sk}) \end{aligned}$$

• Prove(crs, {xj}j∈[k] , {|ϕ<sup>j</sup> ⟩ ⊗κ }j∈[k] ): Here, the quantum state |ϕi⟩ is the quantum witness to the statement x<sup>i</sup> for some L ∈ QMA. The prover computes the string s s.t s<sup>i</sup> = (x<sup>i</sup> ∈ Lyes) and proceeds as follows:

{66}------------------------------------------------

- Round 1: Parse crs to get (pT.hk, mh.pk). If  $s_j = 0$ , set  $y_j = \perp$ , else compute

$$|\psi_j\rangle \leftarrow \mathsf{P}_{\mathsf{FHM}}(x_j, |\phi_j\rangle^{\otimes \kappa})$$
  
 $(y_j, |\mathsf{st}_j\rangle) \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Com}(\mathsf{mh.pk}, |\psi_j\rangle)$ 

Finally calculate  $\mathsf{h}_y$  as follows and send to the verifier.

$$h_y = \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}, s, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]})$$

- Round 3: Receive a bit  $c \in \{0,1\}$  from the verifier. Parse crs to get sB.crs. Let

$$z_{j} = \begin{cases} \bot & s_{j} = 0, \ \mathsf{Maha.Open}(\ket{\mathsf{st}_{j}}, c) & s_{j} = 1 \end{cases}$$

Compute  $h_z$  as follows:

$$h_z = \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{Hash}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{hk}, s, \{z_j\}_{j \in [k]})$$

Compute the evaluation of the circuit  $C_f$  as follows:

$$\forall j, \ \mathsf{ct}_j \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_j, y_j, z_j, c, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_r)$$

$$\mathsf{ct} \leftarrow \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, C_f(\cdot), \{\mathsf{ct}_j\}_{j \in [k]})$$

Send  $(ct, h_z)$  to the verifier. Perform an AoK for the language K defined as follows:

$$\begin{split} \exists \ w \ \text{s.t.} \ \mathsf{h}_y &= \mathsf{PEHT}(\mathsf{pT.hk}, s, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \ \land \\ \mathsf{h}_z &= \mathsf{PEHT}(\mathsf{pT.hk}, s, \{z_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \ \land \\ \mathsf{ct}_j &\leftarrow \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_j, y_j, z_j, c, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_r) \ \land \\ \mathsf{ct} &\leftarrow \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, C_f(\cdot), \{\mathsf{ct}_j\}_{j \in [k]}) \end{split}$$

where

$$w = (s, \{y_j\}_{j \in [k]}, \{z_j\}_{j \in [k]}, \{\mathsf{ct}_j\}_{j \in [k]})$$

- Verify(crs, vk,  $\{x_j\}_{j\in[k]}$ ,  $h_y$ ,  $h_z$ , ct): Works as follows under normal (honest) execution.
  - Round 2: Receive  $h_y$  from the prover. Sample bit c and send it to the prover.
  - Final Verification: Receive  $(ct, h_z)$  from the prover. Parse vk to get fhe.sk. Check if

$$\mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk},\mathsf{ct}) = 1$$

If yes, perform an AoK for the language K. Accept if AoK was successful.

**Remark 8.2.** We use  $\mathfrak{G}$  to denote the above protocol and  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  to denote the  $\lambda$ -fold repetition of  $\mathfrak{G}$ .

## <span id="page-66-0"></span>8.2 Security Analysis

Completeness Completeness of  $\mathfrak{R}$  follows immediately from the completeness properties of the underlying building blocks. For any monotone policy f for instances  $x = (x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k), C_f$  the interaction results in the verifier accepting with non-negligible probability if  $x \in \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$ . If  $x_i \in \mathcal{L}_{yes}$ , then VerifyInstance $(x_i, \cdot, \cdot, \cdot, \cdot, \cdot) = 1$  with non-negligible probability. The AoK interaction in the case of of an honest prover results in the prover being accepted. Therefore, if  $x = (x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k) \in \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$ , the verifier accepts with high probability. The completeness of  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  is followed by a union-bound argument.

{67}------------------------------------------------

Round Complexity We observe that  $\mathfrak{G}$  and (therefore)  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  both have  $\mathbf{5}$  rounds with setup. Alternatively, the verifier can do the setup and  $\mathbf{crs}$  can be sent to the prover as the verifier's first message to get a  $\mathbf{6}$  round Public Coin designated verifier interactive monotone policy BARG for QMA.

**Soundness** The protocol  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  satisfies the notion of non-adaptive computational soundness as defined in definition 7.3. We now provide the soundness proof.

**Theorem 8.3.** Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure protocol (Definition 3.23) and the FHM protocol (Definition 3.32), computational binding and extraction correctness of tag and index of PEHT, and the extraction correctness of  $\epsilon$ -state preserving AoK, the protocol  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  satisfies non-adaptive computational soundness as defined in Definition 7.3.

Proof. The proof strategy proceeds as follows. First, we note that  $\mathfrak{R}$  is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol as per Definition 3.24. For non-adaptive soundness, the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  and the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  are given the instance  $x = (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_k) \notin \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$  and the monotone circuit  $C_f$  pair  $(x, C_f)$ . Additionally, the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  also receives the necessary set  $J^* \subset [k]$  for  $C_f$  as non-uniform advice. We first ensure that the hash values are consistent across **Round 1** and **Round 3**. Since AoK allows the full extraction of all witnesses, the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  can verify each instance and check if the root ciphertext was computed correctly. Finally, we show that the final hybrid has a negligible advantage over the original protocol. From this we follow the arguments presented in Section 4 to show that the protocol  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  has negligible soundness. We proceed as follows

**Hybrid 0**: Original Protocol

**Hybrid 1**: Same as the previous execution, except that we now enable extraction on the necessary set  $J^*$  for PEHT, i.e.,

$$(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{ht}^{\star},\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{vk}^{\star},\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}^{\star}) \leftarrow \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^{\lambda},k,J^{\star},0^{J^{\star}})$$

**Hybrid 2**: Enable AoK full-extraction, i.e. extract the witness as  $(\tilde{\tau}^*, \omega^*) \leftarrow E^{\mathcal{A}(\cdot, \cdot)}$ 

**Hybrid 3**: We have the following structure for the extracted witness

$$\omega^{\star} = \left(s^{\star}, s', \{y_j^{\star}, z_j^{\star}, \mathsf{ct}_j^{\star}\}_{j \in [k]}\right)$$

Reject if any of the following checks fail

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{h}_y &= \mathsf{PEHT.Hash}(\mathsf{pT.hk}^\star, s^\star, \{y_j^\star\}_{j \in [k]}) \; \land \\ \mathbf{h}_z &= \mathsf{PEHT.Hash}(\mathsf{pT.hk}^\star, s', \{z_j^\star\}_{j \in [k]}) \; \land \\ \forall j, \; \mathsf{ct}_j^\star &= \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_j, y_j^\star, z_j^\star, c, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_r) \; \land s^\star = s' \end{split}$$

**Hybrid 4**: We now update the protocol by extracting the tags and indices in final verification round. Reject if  $j_1, j_2 \notin J^*$ 

$$\begin{split} j_1 &= \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}^\star, \mathsf{h}_y) \quad y_{j_1}' = \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}^\star, \mathsf{h}_y) \\ j_2 &= \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}^\star, \mathsf{h}_z) \quad z_{j_2}' = \mathsf{PEHT}.\mathsf{ExtractTag}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}^\star, \mathsf{h}_z) \end{split}$$

{68}------------------------------------------------

**Hybrid 5**: Replace the FHE.Dec for ct by performing the following check

$$\forall j, \ b_j = \text{VerifyInstance}(x_j, y_j^{\star}, z_j^{\star}, c, r)$$

$$C_f(b_1, b_2, \dots, b_k) = 1$$

**Hybrid 6**: We now replace  $\mathsf{ct}_r$  in the  $\mathsf{crs}$  with  $\mathsf{ct}_0 = \mathsf{FHE}.\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, 0^\lambda)$  and let the protocol continue as in the previous hybrid.

We now prove that the attacker's advantage changes negligibly through the sequence of hybrids assuming the soundness properties of the cryptographic building blocks being used. Subsequently, we show that **Hybrid 6** is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol that has orthogonal projectors. We then show that since the attacker's advantage has changed negligibly from **Hybrid 0** to **Hybrid**  $\mathbf{6}$ ,  $\mathfrak{G}^{\lambda}$  cannot have non-negligible soundness. To begin, let us assume that the attacker's advantage in breaking the non-adaptive soundness property of **Hybrid 0** is  $\delta(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 8.4.** Assuming the predicate hiding property of PEHT, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**.

*Proof.* This proof follows from the proof of Lemma 7.11. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_1(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 1** is  $\delta(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$ 

**Lemma 8.5.** Assuming that the AoK is an  $\epsilon$ -state preserving AoK, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2**.

*Proof.* An attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  with a non-negligible advantage across **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2** can differentiate between the output distributions of *Real* and *Simulated* games of the AoK, thus contradicting the  $\epsilon$ -state preserving nature of AoK. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_2(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 2** is  $\delta(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 8.6.** Assuming the extraction correctness of the  $\epsilon$ -state preserving AoK, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3**.

*Proof.* An attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  with a non-negligible advantage across **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3** must be able to produce an accepting transcript  $\tilde{\tau}^*$  with witness  $\omega^*$  s.t

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{h}_y \neq \mathsf{PEHT.Hash}(\mathsf{pT.hk}^\star, s^\star, \{y_j^\star\}_{j \in [k]}) \vee \\ \mathbf{h}_z \neq \mathsf{PEHT.Hash}(\mathsf{pT.hk}^\star, s', \{z_j^\star\}_{j \in [k]}) \vee \\ \forall j, \ \mathsf{ct}_j^\star \neq \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{fhe.pk}, \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_j, y_j^\star, z_j^\star, c, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_r) \ \vee s^\star \neq s' \end{split}$$

However, this contradicts the Extraction correctness property of the  $\epsilon$ -state preserving AoK. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_3(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 3** is  $\delta(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 8.7.** Assuming the index extraction correctness of PEHT, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage doesn't change in **Hybrid 3** and **Hybrid 4** 

*Proof.* This follows the proof of Lemma 7.13. Therefore, the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid** 4 is  $\delta(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

{69}------------------------------------------------

Lemma 8.8. Assuming correctness of FHE evaluation, for any QPT-attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid 4 and Hybrid 5.

Proof. An attacker A with non-negligible advantage across Hybrid 4 and Hybrid 5 must imply

$$\mathsf{FHE.Dec}(\mathsf{fhe.sk},\mathsf{ct}) = 1 \ \land \ C_f(b_1,b_2,\ldots,b_k) = 0$$

a contradiction to the correctness of FHE evaluation. Therefore, there exists a negligible function µ4(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid 5 is δ(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − µ3(λ) − µ4(λ).

Lemma 8.9. Assuming the semantic security of the FHE scheme, for every QPT attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid 5 and Hybrid 6.

Proof. This proof follows from Lemma [5.16](#page-44-0) where we update the security game to B send (r, 0 λ ) and run the verification as per Hybrid 5. Thus, there exists a negligible function µ5(λ) s.t the advantage of the attacker A in Hybrid 6 is δ(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − µ3(λ) − µ4(λ) − µ5(λ).

Lemma 8.10. Assuming the soundness of the Commit-and-Measure Protocol (Maha) and the FHM protocol, Hybrid 6 (G′ ) is a Commit-Challenge-Response protocol with computationally orthogonal projectors.

Proof. This follows from Lemma [4.10](#page-34-2) where we replace the special index i <sup>⋆</sup> with the index j<sup>1</sup> ∈ J ⋆ in the final round of the protocol.

Lemma 8.11. The λ-fold repetition of Hybrid 6, i.e (G′ ) λ , has negligible soundness.

Proof. This follows from Theorem [3.27.](#page-27-1)

Lemma 8.12. The λ-fold repetition of G, i.e G<sup>λ</sup> , has negligible soundness.

Proof. The proof follows the proof of Lemma [4.12](#page-35-1) by replacing the seBARG index i <sup>⋆</sup> with the index j<sup>1</sup> ∈ J ⋆ . Therefore, we note that G<sup>λ</sup> satisfies the non-adaptive soundness property as defined in Definition [7.3.](#page-53-1)

# <span id="page-69-0"></span>9 Non-Interactive Batch Arguments in QROM

<span id="page-69-1"></span>This section discusses the construction of non-interactive batch arguments in QROM starting from the public coin BARG presented in Section [5.](#page-36-0) In [\[DFMS19\]](#page-78-6), the authors showed how to reprogram a QROM adaptively on a single input. Consequently, this led to the conclusion that the Fiat-Shamir transformation of a Σ-protocol is as secure in the QROM as in the standard model. Following this, in [\[DFM20\]](#page-78-7), the results were extended to show the re-programmability of QROM on multiple inputs to establish the security of Fiat-Shamir transformation of multi-round public coin interactive protocols in the QROM. We state this result as the following theorem.

{70}------------------------------------------------

**Theorem 9.1** (Measure and Reprogram with multiple inputs and enforced extraction order [DFM20]). Let n be a positive integer, and let  $\mathcal{X}_0, \mathcal{X}$ , and  $\mathcal{Y}$  be finite non-empty sets. A black-box polynomial time (n+1)-stage quantum algorithm  $\mathcal{S}$  exists, satisfying the following property. Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an arbitrary quantum oracle algorithm that makes q queries to a uniformly random  $H:(\mathcal{X}_0 \cup \mathcal{Y}) \times \mathcal{X} \to \mathcal{Y}$  and that outputs a tuple  $\mathbf{x} = (x_0, \dots, x_n) \in (\mathcal{X} \cup \mathcal{X}_0)$  and a (possibly quantum) output z. Then, for any  $\mathbf{x}^o \in (\mathcal{X} \cup \mathcal{X}_0)$  without duplicate entries and for any predicate V:

$$\Pr_{\Theta} \left[ \mathbf{x} = \mathbf{x}^{o} \wedge V(x, \Theta, z) : (\mathbf{x}, z) \leftarrow \langle \mathcal{S}^{\mathcal{A}}, \Theta \rangle \right]$$

$$\geq \frac{n!}{(2q + n + 1)^{2n}} \Pr_{H} \left[ \mathbf{x} = \mathbf{x}^{o} \wedge V(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{h}^{H, \mathbf{x}}, z) : (x, z) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{H} \right] - \epsilon_{\mathbf{x}^{o}}$$

where  $\mathbf{h}^{H,\mathbf{x}} = (h_1^{H,\mathbf{x}}, \dots, h_n^{H,\mathbf{x}})$  denotes the hash chain defined as  $h_1^{H,\mathbf{x}} = H(x_0, x_1)$  and  $h_i^{H,\mathbf{x}} = H(h_i^{H,\mathbf{x}}, x_i)$  for  $1 \le i \le n$ , and  $h_i^{H,\mathbf{x}} = H(h_i^{H,\mathbf{x}}, x_i)$  when summed over all  $\mathbf{x}^o$ .

We are now ready to show the main result which is obtained by applying the Fiat-Shamir transformation to the designated verifier public coin interactive batch argument system constructed in Section 5 and Section 8.

<span id="page-70-0"></span>**Theorem 9.2.** If a constant round public coin (except for the first message) batch protocol (P, V) satisfies non-adaptive soundness, then the result of application of Fiat-Shamir transform on it is the protocol  $(P_{FS}, V_{FS})$  which also satisfies non-adaptive soundness.

*Proof.* Suppose the protocol (P, V) has 2n + 1 rounds and since it is public coin except for the first message, the verifier can be considered to consist of two circuits  $(V_0, V_1)$ . Let the circuit  $C_f$  be the monotone circuit defining the language  $\mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$ . Given a batch of instances  $\mathbf{x} = (x_1, \dots, x_k, C_f)$ , the verifier uses random coins r to compute the first message as  $s_0 = V_0(\mathbf{x}, r)$ . The remaining verifier messages are random strings  $s_1, \dots, s_n$ . We denote the prover messages as  $t_0, \dots, t_n$ . Finally, the verifier outputs the result of circuit  $V_1(\mathbf{x}, r, s_0, t_0, s_1, t_1, \dots, s_n, t_n)$ .

Let us now consider a random oracle  $H: ((\mathcal{X} \times \mathsf{S}) \cup \mathsf{S}) \times ([n] \times \mathcal{T}) \to \mathsf{S}$ , where  $\mathcal{X}$  is the space of (batch of) instances,  $\mathsf{S}$  is the space of verifier messages, and  $\mathcal{T}$  is the space of prover messages.  $(P_{FS}, V_{FS})$  proceeds as follows. First,  $V_{FS}$  outputs  $s_0 = V_0(\mathbf{x}, r)$ . Next, the prover produces  $t_0 = P(\mathbf{x}, s_0)$ . Subsequently, it generates remaining verifier messages as  $s_i = H((\mathbf{x}, s_0), (i, t_{i-1}))$  and prover messages as  $t_i = P(\mathbf{x}, s_i)$ . Finally, the verifier verifies the correctness of each  $s_i$  and outputs  $V(\mathbf{x}, r, s_0, t_0, \ldots, s_n, t_n)$ .

Consider an instance  $\mathbf{x}$  such that  $C_f(x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k) \neq 1$ , i.e  $x_1, x_2, \dots, x_k \notin \mathcal{L}_f^{(k)}$ . Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be an adversary that is initialised with  $(\mathbf{x}, s_0)$  and  $\epsilon(r)$  be its success probability defined as follows. Note that it can generate  $s_1, \dots, s_n$  using no additional information.

$$\epsilon(r) = \Pr_H \left[ (\mathbf{x}, s_0, t_0, s_1, t_1, \dots, s_n, t_n) = 1 : (t_0, \dots, t_n) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^H \right]$$

We make use of Theorem 9.1 to get the following bound, where  $\delta(r)$  is the success probability of a simulator  $\mathcal{S}^{\mathcal{A},r}(s_1,\ldots,s_n)$ .

$$\delta(r) \geq \frac{1}{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)} \epsilon(r) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

{71}------------------------------------------------

But, due to the non-adaptive soundness of (P, V ) it must be the case that Er[δ(r)] = negl(λ). Therefore, we can write the following.

$$\operatorname{negl}(\lambda) \geq \frac{1}{\operatorname{poly}(\lambda)} \mathbb{E}_r[\epsilon(r)] - \operatorname{negl}(\lambda) \ \Longrightarrow \ \epsilon(r) = \operatorname{negl}(\lambda)$$

Corollary 9.3 (Designated verifier Non-Interactive Monotone Policy BARG for QMA in QROM). Assuming the post-quantum hardness of learning with errors (LWE) problem and post-quantum fully homomorphic encryption (FHE), there exists a designated verifier non-interactive monotone policy batch argument system for QMA in the quantum random oracle model (QROM) which satisfies the completeness (Definition [7.2\)](#page-53-2) and non-adaptive soundness properties (Definition [7.3\)](#page-53-1)

Theorem 9.4. If a constant round public coin (except for the first message) batch protocol (P, V ) satisfies semi-adaptive soundness, then the result of application of Fiat-Shamir transform on it is the protocol (PF S, VF S) which also satisfies semi-adaptively sound.

Proof. The proof of this theorem proceeds similarly to Theorem [9.2](#page-70-0) with slight modifications to incorporate the semi-adaptive soundness nature of the batch protocol (P, V ). The first message s<sup>0</sup> is generated with the additional input of i ⋆ . Finally, we invoke the semi-adaptive soundness property of the batch argument (P, V ) to conclude that (PF S, VF S) is also semi-adaptively sound.

Corollary 9.5 (Designated Verifier Non-Interactive BARG for QMA in QROM). Assuming the postquantum hardness of learning with errors (LWE) problem and post-quantum fully homomorphic encryption (FHE), there exists a non-interactive batch argument system for QMA in the quantum random oracle model (QROM) which satisfies the completeness and semi-adaptive computational soundness properties defined in Section [3.20.](#page-24-1)

# <span id="page-71-0"></span>10 Public Verifiability using Differing inputs iO

In this section, we outline a canonical construction where we transform any designated verifier proof system to a publicly verifiable proof system. For simplicity, we show the construction for a generic privately verifiable non-interactive SNARG(Gen, P, Ver) system for a given language L. More formally, this result can be stated as follows:

Theorem 10.1 (Publicly verifiable proof system). Assuming the security of Differing Inputs Obfuscation diO, Puncturable PRFs, and the underlying privately verifiable proof system SNARG(Gen, P, V), there exists a publicly verifiable proof system pvSNARG(Gen <sup>g</sup>, <sup>P</sup>e, <sup>V</sup>e) that satisfies a non-adaptive notion of soundness.

## <span id="page-71-1"></span>10.1 Construction

We construct the pvSNARG(Gen <sup>g</sup>, <sup>P</sup>e, <sup>V</sup>e) by obfuscating the two programs: one to generate the prover's key pk, and one to verify the instance x given proof π. We rely on PPRF security and diO security to achieve a non-adaptive notion of soundness. However, we believe this transformation can be extended to get somewhere soundness by changing the verification program to output 0 for the semi-adaptively chosen instance i ⋆ .

{72}------------------------------------------------

#### Ingredients

- 1. Privately verifiable SNARG(Gen, P, V) proof system.
- 2. Differing inputs Obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$  as defined in Definition 3.14.
- 3. Puncturable PRF family PPRF(Eval, Puncture, PuncEval) with output length  $\ell(\lambda)$  as defined in Definition 3.9.

#### **Protocol Specification**

•  $\widetilde{\mathsf{Gen}}(1^{\lambda}, x) \to \mathsf{crs}$ : It first samples a PPRF key  $K \leftarrow \{0, 1\}^{\lambda}$ , and is hard-wired in the following procedures.

$$P_{\mathsf{pk}}(x) : \begin{bmatrix} 1. & \text{Compute } (\mathsf{pk}_x, \mathsf{vk}_x) = \mathsf{SNARG.Gen}(1^\lambda, F_K(x)) \\ 2. & \text{Output } \mathsf{pk}_x. \end{bmatrix}$$

$$1. & \text{Compute } (\mathsf{pk}_x, \mathsf{vk}_x) = \mathsf{SNARG.Gen}(1^\lambda, F_K(x))$$

$$2. & \text{Run } b_{x,\pi} = \mathsf{SNARG.V}(\mathsf{vk}_x, x, \pi)$$

$$3. & \text{Output } b_{x,\pi}.$$

Compute the obfuscation of the above procedures

$$\widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{pk}} \leftarrow \mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}(1^{\lambda}, P_{\mathsf{pk}}), \quad \widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{V}} \leftarrow \mathsf{di}\mathcal{O}(1^{\lambda}, P_{\mathsf{V}})$$

Output  $crs = (\widetilde{P}_{pk}, \widetilde{P}_{V}).$ 

•  $\widetilde{\mathsf{P}}(\mathsf{crs}, x, w) \to \pi$ : Parse  $\mathsf{crs}$  to get  $\widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{pk}}$ . Perform the following steps and output  $\pi$ :

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{pk}_x &= \widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{pk}}(x) \\ \pi &\leftarrow \mathsf{SNARG.P}(\mathsf{pk}_x, x, w) \end{aligned}$$

•  $\widetilde{\mathsf{V}}(\mathsf{crs}, x, \pi) \to 0/1$ : Parse  $\mathsf{crs}$  to get  $\widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{V}}$ . Output  $\widetilde{P}_{\mathsf{V}}(x, \pi)$ 

## <span id="page-72-0"></span>10.2 Security Analysis

Completeness The completeness of the protocol follows directly from the underlying cryptographic primitives. The obfuscated program  $\widetilde{P}_{V}$  accepts with overwhelming probability whenever SNARG.V accepts. Therefore if  $x \in \mathcal{L}$ , the verifier accepts with high probability.

**Soundness** The protocol  $pvSNARG(\widetilde{Gen}, \widetilde{P}, \widetilde{V})$  satisfies the notion of non-adaptive soundness. We now provide the soundness proof.

**Theorem 10.2.** Assuming the security of Differing Inputs Obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$ , Pucturable PRFs, and the non-adaptive soundness of the underlying SNARG proof system, the protocol  $pvSNARG(\widetilde{Gen}, \widetilde{P}, \widetilde{V})$  satisifies non-adaptive computational soundness.

*Proof.* The proof strategy is as follows. For non-adaptive soundness, we are given an instance  $x^* \notin \mathcal{L}$ . First we make the PPRF puncturable on this instance  $x^*$ . We then proceed to update the  $\widetilde{P}_{pk}$  to an alternative program that outputs a special pk for  $x^*$ . From here, we update the verification program to output 0 on input  $x^*$ . If at any point, the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage, we contradict the security of  $di\mathcal{O}$  or Puncturable PRFs. We proceed as follows

{73}------------------------------------------------

Hybrid 0: Same as the original protocol

Hybrid 1: Sample pkx<sup>⋆</sup> as follows:

$$(\mathsf{pk}_{x^\star}, \mathsf{vk}_{x^\star}) = \mathsf{SNARG}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, F_K(x^\star))$$

Hardwire pkx<sup>⋆</sup> , puncture PPRF at x = x <sup>⋆</sup> and obfuscate the following programs in the crs

Hardwired values: 
$$\mathsf{pk}_{x^{\star}}$$
(a) If  $x = x^{\star}$ , output  $\mathsf{pk}_{x^{\star}}$ .
(b) Else:

i. Compute 
$$(\mathsf{pk}_x, \mathsf{vk}_x) = \mathsf{SNARG}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, F_{K\{x^\star\}}(x))$$
.

ii. Output pk<sup>x</sup>

$$P'_{\mathsf{V}}(x,\pi): \begin{tabular}{ll} \textbf{Hardwired values: } \forall \mathsf{k}_{x^{\star}} \\ (a) & \underline{\text{If } x = x^{\star} \text{ output SNARG.V}(\forall \mathsf{k}_{x^{\star}}, x, \pi).} \\ (b) & \underline{\text{Else:}} \\ & \text{i. Compute } (\mathsf{pk}_{x}, \forall \mathsf{k}_{x}) = \mathsf{SNARG.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, F_{K\{x^{\star}\}}(x)) \\ & \text{ii. Output SNARG.V}(\forall \mathsf{k}_{x}, x, \pi). \end{tabular}$$

Hybrid 2: Generate the keys for the instance x <sup>⋆</sup> using fresh randomness r ← {0, 1} ℓ(λ) ,

$$(\mathsf{pk}_{x^\star}, \mathsf{vk}_{x^\star}) = \mathsf{SNARG}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, r)$$

We obfuscate the following in the crs

Hardwired values: 
$$\mathsf{pk}_{x^*}$$
(a) If  $x = x^*$ , output  $\mathsf{pk}_{x^*}$ .

(b) Else:
\ni. Compute  $(\mathsf{pk}_x, \mathsf{vk}_x) = \mathsf{SNARG}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda, F_{K\{x^*\}}(x))$ .
\nii. Output  $\mathsf{pk}_x$ 

Hardwired values:  $\mathsf{vk}_{x^*}$ 
(a) If  $x = x^*$  output  $\mathsf{SNARG}.\mathsf{V}(\mathsf{vk}_{x^*}, x, \pi)$ .

P ′′ <sup>V</sup>(x, π) : (b) Else: i. Compute (pk<sup>x</sup> , vkx) = SNARG.Gen(1<sup>λ</sup> , FK{x⋆} (x)) ii. Output SNARG.V(vkx, x, π).

Hybrid 3: We obfuscate the following program in the crs

$$P'''_{\mathsf{V}}(x,\pi): \begin{array}{c} \text{(a)} \ \underline{\mathrm{If} \ x=x^{\star}, \ \mathrm{output} \ 0.} \\ \text{(b)} \ \overline{\mathrm{Else}} \\ \text{i. Compute} \ (\mathsf{pk}_{x}, \mathsf{vk}_{x}) = \mathsf{SNARG.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, F_{K\{x^{\star}\}}(x)) \\ \text{ii. Output } \ \mathsf{SNARG.V}(\mathsf{vk}_{x}, x, \pi) \end{array}$$

{74}------------------------------------------------

For contradiction, assume that the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in breaking the non-adaptive soundness property is  $\varepsilon(\lambda)$ , a non-negligible function. We have the following lemmas

**Lemma 10.3.** Assuming the security of differing inputs obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$ , for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**.

Proof. First, we observe that the input/output behavior of  $P_{pk}(x)$  and  $P'_{pk}$  is identical. Similarly, we observe that  $P_{V}$  and  $P'_{V}$  are identical. We only puncture the randomness to the SNARG.Gen algorithm at  $x^*$ . For contradiction, assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across **Hybrid 0** and **Hybrid 1**. Consider a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that receives an obfuscated program  $\widetilde{P}_{pk}$  from a challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  that samples at random one of  $P_{pk}$  or  $P'_{pk}$  with aux set to any additional public information that is generated by the SNARG.Gen. We note that if  $P_{pk}$  is chosen, we are operating in **Hybrid 0**, while if  $P'_{pk}$  is chosen, we are operating in **Hybrid 1**. If  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage between the two hybrids, the reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  can guess which program was used under obfuscation. This contradicts the security of  $di\mathcal{O}$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_1(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 1** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda)$ .

**Lemma 10.4.** Assuming the security of the PPRF, for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2**.

Proof. First, we observe that the only change between **Hybrid 1** and **Hybrid 2** is the randomness used to generate the (pk, vk) pairs for the adaptively chosen instance  $x^*$  in the programs  $P'_{pk}$  and  $P''_{pk}$  (similarly for  $P'_{V}$  and  $P''_{V}$ ). For contradiction, assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across the two hybrids. We can construct a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that uses  $\mathcal{A}$ . The algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  interacts with  $\mathcal{A}$  trivially to produce the output  $(F_{K\{x^*\}}(x), r)$  and successfully differentiates between the two with non-negligible probability, a contradiction to the security of PPRFs. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_2(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 2** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda)$ .  $\square$ 

**Lemma 10.5.** Assuming the security of Differing Inputs Obfuscation  $di\mathcal{O}$ , for any poly-size attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3**.

Proof. First, we observe that the input/output behavior of  $P''_{V}$  and  $P'''_{V}$  differs only on the input  $x^{\star}$ . For all  $x \neq x^{\star}$ , both  $P''_{V}$  and  $P'''_{V}$  must behave exactly the same. Note that  $P'''_{V}$  is well defined since we are quantifying over all  $x^{\star}$ ,  $x^{\star}$  must be known to the reduction algorithm. For contradiction, assume that  $\mathcal{A}$  has non-negligible advantage across **Hybrid 2** and **Hybrid 3**. Since non-negligible advantage is greater than inverse polynomial advantage, we can invoke extraction on the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ . Let the extraction output  $\mathcal{E}^{\mathcal{A}}$  be x'. If  $x^{\star} \neq x'$ , we break the security of  $di\mathcal{O}$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mu_3(\lambda)$  s.t the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  in **Hybrid 3** is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mu_1(\lambda) - \mu_2(\lambda) - \mu_3(\lambda)$ .

At this point, the advantage of the attacker is  $\varepsilon(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ , where

$$\mathsf{negl}(\lambda) = (\mu_1 + \mu_2 + \mu_3)(\lambda)$$

If  $x^* = x'$ , the challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  performs the following steps

$$(\mathsf{pk}_{x^{\star}}, \mathsf{vk}_{x^{\star}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{SNARG.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, r) \\ b \leftarrow \mathsf{SNARG.V}(\mathsf{vk}_{x^{\star}}, x^{\star}, \pi)$$

If b=1, we break the non-adaptive soundness of the underlying SNARG proof system, a contradiction. Therefore, the attacker's advantage in **Hybrid 0** must be negligible, i.e  $\varepsilon(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ 

{75}------------------------------------------------
