## <span id="page-81-0"></span>A Public Coin Succinct Arguments for QMA

In this section, we outline the barriers to achieving a six round succinct argument system for QMA by modifying the argument system presented in [BKL<sup>+</sup>22]. We choose this version of the protocol for ease of presentation and thus require a post-quantum indistinguishability obfuscation as an underlying assumption. However, the analysis for [GKNV24] and [MNZ24] follow similarly as they rely on AoK-style full extraction. The key idea is to reduce the number of interactions by interleaving the messages of the AoK system [CMSZ22] with the messages of the commitment procedure. We first outline a quantum knowledge extraction procedure for Classical Proof-of-Knowledge (PoK) systems to motivate our final construction. Finally, we present two succinct argument systems for QMA (eight and six rounds respectively) and note the barriers to the six-round construction.

#### <span id="page-81-1"></span>A.1 Quantum Knowledge extraction from Classical PoK systems

Knowledge extraction from  $\Sigma$ -protocols - three round protocols between classical participants with uniform random messages from the verifier - can be done via rewinding a prover and extracting the witness from two accepting transcripts.  $\Sigma$ -protocols have two flavors: Proof-of-Knowledge and Argument-of-Knowledge. PoKs have statistical soundness guarantees while AoKs offer computational soundness. The rewinding procedure does not translate trivially to quantum provers as any intermediate measurement disturbs the quantum states used and thus we cannot obtain two accepting transcripts. Unruh's [Unr12] work addresses these concerns for  $\Sigma$ -protocols that satisfy the following special properties

**Definition A.1** (Special Soundness). A  $\Sigma$ -protocol (P, V) for a relation R has special soundness iff there exists a deterministic poly-time algorithm  $\mathsf{K}_0$  s.t: For any two accepting conversations (com, ch, resp), and (com, ch', resp') for x s.t  $ch \neq ch'$  are two challenges from the challenge-space C, we have  $w := \mathsf{K}_0(x, com, ch, resp, ch', resp')$  satisfies  $(x, w) \in R$ 

**Definition A.2** (Strict Soundness). A  $\Sigma$  protocol (P, V) for a relation R has strict soundness iff for any two accepting conversations (com, ch, resp) and (com, ch, resp') for x, we must have resp = resp'.

<span id="page-81-2"></span>The central result of the paper [Unr12] can be summarized by the following theorem below

**Theorem A.3.** Let (P, V) be a  $\Sigma$ -protocol for a relation R with challenge space C. Fix a function c s.t for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}, x \in \{0,1\}^*$ , we have  $|C(\lambda,x)| \geq c(\lambda)$ . Assume that (P,V) has special soundness and strict soundness. Then (P, V) is quantum extractable with a knowledge error  $1/\sqrt{c}$ 

The proof crucially relies on the following assumptions:

- 1. The strict soundness property must be statistical in nature, i.e there cannot be multiple accepting transcripts for the same challenge.
- 2. The cheating prover can be purified to defer all measurements to the end of the protocol and thus can be applied as a unitary operation on any initial state.
- 3. For a given challenge space, each challenge unitary must be orthogonal.

At a high-level, the proof generates two accepting transcripts uses the canonical extractor  $K_0$  for the two challenges  $ch, ch' \in C$ . To ensure that the transcripts for ch, ch' are accepting, one of the

{82}------------------------------------------------

hybrids in the proof performs a measurement by purifying the cheating prover's operations and checking if the final result is acceptance. Strict soundness ensures that the post-measurement state is undisturbed after these measurements. Special soundness ensures that the extractor K<sup>0</sup> exists and is efficient.

Remark A.4. Unruh argues that it is unclear whether the extractor works for an Argument-of-Knowledge system with computational strict soundness. This would imply that any output measurement state for resp may be in a superposition of multiple eigenstates. Thus, any measurement via the purified cheating prover will disturb the post measurement state.

## <span id="page-82-0"></span>A.2 Succinct Argument systems for QMA

#### Version 1

1. Define the circuit C : {0, 1} log <sup>N</sup> → {0, 1} s.t C(i) = h<sup>i</sup> and C is obfuscated. The Verifier computes and sends (ctr, mh.pk, H) to the prover.

$$\mathsf{mh.pk} \leftarrow \mathsf{Maha.Gen}(1^\lambda, C; r)$$
  $\mathsf{ct}_r = \mathsf{FHE.Enc}(\mathsf{FHE.pk}, r)$   $H \leftarrow \{\mathcal{H}_\lambda\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}}$ 

where {Hλ}λ∈<sup>N</sup> is a collapsing hash family (see [\[CMSZ22,](#page-78-1) Definition 3.9]).

- 2. The prover computes the proof state |ψ⟩ using the FHM protocol and commits to it using mh.pk. Let (y, |st⟩) be the output of the Maha.Com procedure. The prover initializes the AoK for the relation R<sup>G</sup> (see Defintion [4.2\)](#page-30-1) then computes ˆy = H(y) and sends it to the prover.
- 3. The verifier samples a random question Q<sup>1</sup> for the AoK and sends them to the prover
- 4. The prover responds with the responses R<sup>1</sup> to the AoK
- 5. The verifier checks if the AoK responses R<sup>1</sup> are valid. Then it samples a random bit c ∈ {0, 1} and sends it to the prover.
- 6. The prover runs z ← Maha.Open(|st⟩, c) and computes ˆz = H(z). Subsequently, the prover computes ct<sup>P</sup> = FHE.Eval(FHE.pk, VerifyInstance(x, y, z, c, ·), ctr) and sends (ˆz, ct<sup>P</sup> ) to the verifier.
- 7. The verifier runs an AoK check for the language

$$\begin{split} \exists w_1, w_2, \mathsf{ct}_P \,\, \mathsf{s.t} \,\, \hat{y} &= H(w_1) \,\, \land \\ \hat{z} &= H(w_2) \,\, \land \\ \mathsf{ct}_P &= \mathsf{FHE.Eval}(\mathsf{FHE.pk}, \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x, w_1, w_2, c, \cdot), \mathsf{ct}_r) \end{split}$$

So, the verifier samples a random question Q<sup>2</sup> for the AoK and sends it to the prover.

- 8. The prover responds with responses R<sup>2</sup> for the AoK.
- 9. The verifier checks if the AoK responses R<sup>2</sup> are valid and checks if ct<sup>P</sup> decrypts to 1.

{83}------------------------------------------------

#### Version 2

- 1. The Verifier computes and sends (ctr, mh.pk, H) to the prover as done previously.
- 2. The prover computes ˆy = H(y) and sends it to the prover as done previously.
- 3. The verifier samples a random question Q<sup>1</sup> for the AoK, a random bit c, and sends (c, Q1).
- 4. The prover computes ˆz, ct<sup>P</sup> , and computes the responses to the AoK R<sup>1</sup> and sends (ˆz, ct<sup>P</sup> , R1)
- 5. The verifier checks if the AoK responses R<sup>1</sup> are valid. Then, the verifier samples a random question Q<sup>2</sup> for the AoK and sends it to the prover.
- 6. The prover responds with responses R<sup>2</sup> for the AoK.
- 7. The verifier checks if the AoK responses R<sup>2</sup> are valid and checks if ct<sup>P</sup> decrypts to 1.

The highlighted items in Version 1 of the protocol are the additional two messages that are introduced by the AoK for the relation RG. The highlighted items in Version 2 of the protocol shows how the challenge bit c of the Maha protocol is interleaved with the AoK messages. At first glance, the Version 2 of the protocol has six rounds as compared to eight rounds for Version 1. Moreover, the completeness of both protocols follow from the completeness properties of the primitives used in the construction. For the soundness proof for Version 2 of the protocol consider a reduction algorithm B for a cheating QPT prover A as below.

- 1. A challenger C to the VerifyInstance predicate samples the (mh.pk, ctr) as outlined above and sends it to the reduction algorithm B.
- 2. The reduction algorithm receives (mh.pk, ctr) and samples a random collapsing hash function H. Finally, it sends (mh.pk, ctr, H) to the cheating prover A.
- 3. The prover computes the message ˆy and sends it to the reduction algorithm B.
- 4. The reduction algorithm B runs the AoK extractor to extract the output y from the prover A with a random bit c ′ ∈ {0, 1} and forwards y to the challenger C.
- 5. The challenger samples a random bit c ∈ {0, 1} and sends it to B which then forwards this to the prover A.
- 6. The prover computes the message ˆz and sends it to the reduction algorithm B.
- 7. The reduction algorithm runs the AoK extractor to extract the output (y ′ , z) from the prover A and sends z to the challenger C.

The reduction algorithm ensures that y = y ′ and if A succeeds in convincing the verifier of the Version 2 of the protocol, we have a reduction algorithm that breaks the soundness guarantees of the VerifyInstance predicate. However, a few subtleties make the above proof strategy infeasible. Firstly, the knowledge-system used in the version of the protocol above is an Argument-of-Knowledge and not a Proof-of-Knowledge and thus has computational soundness. Therefore, we cannot use Unruh's result as a black-box.

{84}------------------------------------------------

To attempt a rewinding proof as in Theorem [A.3,](#page-81-2) we can allow the reduction algorithm B access to operate separately on the registers Y, Z of the cheating prover for the Commit-and-Measure protocol based on specific control qubits. Let c <sup>⋆</sup> ∈ {0, 1} be the challenge sent by the reduction algorithm B in round 3 of the protocol. Let Π<sup>c</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> be the projector that measures whether the prover gets accepted for challenge c <sup>⋆</sup> and outputs the value of y that gets accepted. In essence, we run the cheating prover A with input c <sup>⋆</sup> and measure if we get an accepting transcript and only then measuring Y in the computational basis. We proceed as in Theorem [A.3](#page-81-2) and extract y, which can be then forwarded to the challenger C.

For the proof strategy to work, we would require Π<sup>c</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> to not significantly disturb the registers Y and Z, i.e we need it to obey strict soundness. It is unclear, however, that the Mahadev measurement protocol satisfies such statistical guarantees. The soundness guarantees for the protocol are computational in nature and thus any measurement by the projector Π<sup>c</sup> <sup>⋆</sup> can potentially disturb the opening register Z. We do not know (to the best of our knowledge) any ways to navigate this issue other than using Version 1 of the protocol.

# <span id="page-84-0"></span>B Proof of Lemma [7.23](#page-63-0)

Lemma B.1. Assuming the index hiding and extraction correctness properties of seBARG2, extraction correctness, extraction consistency, and consistency of tag extraction properties of PEHT<sup>2</sup> and the predicate hiding property of PEHash, for any QPT attacker A, the attacker's advantage is indistinguishable in Hybrid 7 and Hybrid 8

Proof. Recall the current state of the hybrids. We have

- 1. Enabled extraction for PEHT<sup>1</sup> on the non uniform advice of the necessary set J ⋆ .
- 2. Ensured that the string s used for h<sup>y</sup> and h<sup>z</sup> is consistent across Round 1 and Round 3.
- 3. Enabled the extraction for PEHT<sup>2</sup> on J <sup>⋆</sup> and checked if j<sup>3</sup> ∈ J ⋆

Let us define a relation R<sup>t</sup> s.t Rt(x<sup>j</sup> , w<sup>j</sup> ) = 1 with op y⋆ j , opz⋆ j , y⋆′ j , z⋆′ j , s<sup>⋆</sup> j ) = w<sup>j</sup> iff the following checks are true

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_y,j,s_j^\star,\mathsf{op}_j^{y\star}) &= 1 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_1.\mathsf{Verify}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{h}_z,j,s_j^\star,\mathsf{op}_j^{z\star}) &= 1 \ \land \\ \mathsf{VerifyInstance}(x_j,y_j,z_j,c,r) &= 1 \ \land \ s_j^\star &= 1 \end{split}$$

We say that attacker A wins with non-negligible, say µ6(λ), advantage across Hybrid 7 and Hybrid 8 if he can output Rt(xj<sup>3</sup> , wj<sup>3</sup> ) = 0 and still get accepted by the challenger C. We are essentially proving somewhere argument of knowledge for Monotone Policy Batch SNARGs with key ideas coming from the proof of [\[BBK](#page-75-5)+23, Theorem. 7.1]. Let d be the depth of the circuit C<sup>f</sup> . For all i ∈ [d] denote J<sup>i</sup> ⊂ [M] the wires in the i th layer (excluding the input layer) that have values 0. We now define a sequence of intermediate hybrids in which we update the Setup algorithm to enable extraction for PEHash. Let the intermediate set of hybrids be denoted by Hyb4i,j 0≤i,j≤d where we make the following updates

{85}------------------------------------------------

 $\mathsf{Setup}_{i,j}(1^k, 1^k, 1^N, f)$  does the following:

- (a) Define predicates  $d_{f_1} = (b, i)$  and  $d_{f_2} = (b, j)$ , where  $b \in \{0, 1\}^k$  is defined by  $b_i = \mathbf{1}_{i \notin J^*}$ .
- (b) Generate  $(\mathsf{pH.hk}^{\star}_{\beta}, \mathsf{pH.vk}^{\star}_{\beta}, \mathsf{pH.td}^{\star}_{\beta}) \leftarrow \mathsf{PEHash.Gen}(1^{\lambda}, M, d_{f_{\beta}}) \text{ for } \beta \in [2] \text{ and } M = |C_f|.$

We have the following claim

<span id="page-85-0"></span>Claim B.2. Assuming the predicate hiding property of PEHash, the QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  must have an advantage that is indistinguishable between  $\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i}$  and  $\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i-1}$  for all  $i \in [d]$ .

*Proof.* This follows from the proof of Lemma 7.11. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t. the advantage of  $\mathcal A$  in  $\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i-1}$  is  $\mu_4(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ 

For every  $i \in [d]$  and for every  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ , denote

$$\begin{split} \nu_i(\lambda) &= \nu_i^{(1)}(\lambda) \triangleq \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i-1}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathcal{R}_t(x_{j_3}, w_{j_3}) = 0 \; \wedge \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{v}_1, C_f) = 0 \end{array} \right], \\ \nu_{i-1}^{(2)} &\triangleq \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i-1}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathcal{R}_t(x_{j_3}, w_{j_3}) = 0 \; \wedge \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star, \mathsf{v}_2, C_f) = 0 \end{array} \right], \\ \nu_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda) &\triangleq \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{0,0}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathcal{R}_t(x_{j_3}, w_{j_3}) = 0 \; \wedge \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star, \mathsf{h}_w) = 0 \end{array} \right] \end{split}$$

<span id="page-85-1"></span>Claim B.3. Assuming the index hiding and somewhere argument of knowledge property of seBARG<sub>2</sub>, and extraction consistency of PEHash, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t. for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$  and i > 1

$$\nu_i^{(1)}(\lambda) \le \nu_{i-1}^{(2)}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

*Proof.* By simple probability arguments, we have

$$\nu_i^{(1)}(\lambda) \leq \nu_i^{(2)}(\lambda) + \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i,i-1}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} j_3 \notin J^\star \ \lor \ \mathcal{R}_t(x_{j_3}, w_{j_3}) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star, \mathsf{v}_1, C_f) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star, \mathsf{v}_2, C_f) = 1 \end{array} \right] \leq \nu_i^{(2)}(\lambda) + \delta_{i,i-1}(\lambda)$$

For contradiction, assume  $\delta_{i,i-1}(\lambda)$  is non-negligible. Let the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  win if it triggers the above condition. We have the following for any index j' in the  $i^{th}$  level of  $C_f$ .

$$\Pr\left[\mathcal{A} \text{ wins} \land j' = \mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pH.td}_1^\star, \mathsf{v}_1)\right] = \frac{\delta_{i,i-1}(\lambda)}{\mathrm{width}(C_f)} \geq \frac{\delta_{i,i-1}(\lambda)}{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)} = \delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda)$$

The challenger enables extraction on the set  $\{j'=j'_0,j'_1,j'_2\}$  for seBARG<sub>2</sub> where  $j'_1,j'_2$  are the inputs to the gate  $G_{j'}$ 

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^M, 1^T, \{ \textbf{\textit{j}'}, \textbf{\textit{j}}_1^\prime, \textbf{\textit{j}}_2^\prime \})$$

We characterize the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$  into different types of attackers. Let  $\mathcal{A}_1$  be an attacker for the index hiding property of seBARG<sub>2</sub>.

{86}------------------------------------------------

Claim B.4. Assuming index hiding property of seBARG<sub>2</sub>, the QPT attacker  $A_1$  must have an advantage that is indistinguishable from the advantage of A

*Proof.* This follows from the proof of Lemma 4.7. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t. the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  is  $\delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ 

From here, we extract a witness at the location j' using seBARG extraction, i.e.,

$$\omega_{j'}^{\star} = \left(b_{j'}^{\star\prime}, b_{j'_1}^{\star\prime}, b_{j'_2}^{\star\prime}, \left(\rho_{j'}^{\star(\beta)}, \rho_{j'_1}^{\star(\beta)}, \rho_{j'_2}^{\star(\beta)}\right)_{\beta \in [2]}\right)$$

We may also get  $\omega_{j'_1}^{\star}$  and  $\omega_{j'_2}^{\star}$  as potential witnesses. Let  $\mathcal{A}_2$  be an attacker for the somewhere argument of knowledge property for seBARG<sub>2</sub>, while  $\mathcal{A}_3$  an attacker for the consistency of extraction property of PEHT<sub>2</sub>. We now have the following claims.

Claim B.5. Assuming the somewhere argument of seBARG<sub>2</sub> and computational binding of PEHash, the QPT attacker  $A_2$  must have an advantage that is indistinguishable from A.

*Proof.* This follows from the proof of Lemma 4.8. Thus, we have  $\chi(\zeta, j'_{\alpha}, \omega^{\star}_{j'_{\alpha}}) = 1$  for  $\alpha \in \{0, 1, 2\}$ . Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t. the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  is  $\delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ 

<span id="page-86-0"></span>Claim B.6. Assuming the extraction correctness, consistency of extraction, of PEHash, the QPT attacker  $A_3$  must have an advantage that is indistinguishable from the advantage of A.

*Proof.* Consider a reduction algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  that interacts with a challenger  $\mathcal{C}$  for the consistency of extraction property. Suppose that we have  $b_{j'}^{\star\prime} = 1 = \bar{m}_{j'}$ . Since  $j' = \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pH.td}_1^{\star}, \mathsf{v}_1) \in J^{\star}$ , we must have

PEHash.Extract(pT.td<sub>1</sub>
$$^{\star}$$
, v<sub>1</sub>) = 0

otherwise  $\mathcal{B}$  trivially uses  $\mathcal{A}_3$  to produce  $(\mathsf{v}_1,j',b_{j'}^{\star\prime},\rho_{j'}^{\star})$  and break the consistency of extraction property of PEHash. Therefore, there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t. the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  is  $\delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda) - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

At this point, we have shown that Gate  $G_{j'}$  has the right output value. However, since  $b_{j'}^{\star} = 0$  (by assumption), one of the child nodes must be evaluated incorrectly. We now have the following for any random value of  $\alpha \in \{1, 2\}$ 

$$\Pr\left[\mathcal{A} \text{ wins} \land j' = \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{ExtractIndex}(\mathsf{pH.td}_1^{\star},\mathsf{v}_1) \land b_{j'_{\alpha}}^{\star\prime} > b_{j'_{\alpha}}^{\star}\right] \geq \frac{\delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda)}{2} - \mathsf{negI}(\lambda)$$

$$= \delta''_{i,i-1}(\lambda)$$

Since the actual value on wire  $b_{j'}^{\star}$  and the extracted value  $b_{j'}^{\star}$  don't match, we must have the above probability equation to hold for at least one of the input wires to gate  $G_{j'}$ . Claim B.7. Assuming the extraction consistency of PEHash, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , the advantage of the attacker is negligible for all i > 1

*Proof.* This follows from Claim B.6. Therefore, we must have the advantage of the attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ ,  $\delta''_{i,i-1}(\lambda)$ , be negligible.

Since 
$$\delta''_{i,i-1}(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$
, we must have  $\delta'_{i,i-1}(\lambda) = \delta_{i,i-1}(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ .

At this point in the proof we have shown that if layer i is computed correctly, then layer i-1 must also be computed correctly. However, we are yet to move from layer i to i-1 for the first instance of PEHash. For this, we make use of the following claim.

<span id="page-87-0"></span>Claim B.8. Assuming the index hiding and somewhere argument of knowledge property of seBARG<sub>2</sub>, and extraction consistency of PEHash, for every QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t, for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\nu_{i-1}^{(2)}(\lambda) \le \nu_{i-1}^{(1)}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

*Proof.* By predicate hiding property of PEHash (Claim B.2), we have

$$\nu_{i-1}^{(2)}(\lambda) \leq \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i-1,i-1}} \big[ \mathsf{PEHash.Extract}(\mathsf{pH.td}_2^\star, \mathsf{v}_2) = 0 \big] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

We have by simple probability arguments

$$\begin{split} \nu_{i-1}^{(2)}(\lambda) & \leq \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i-1,i-1}} \Big[ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{v}_1) = 0 \Big] \; + \\ & \qquad \qquad \mathsf{Pr} \\ & \qquad \qquad \mathsf{Hyb4}_{i-1,i-1}^{} \left[ \begin{array}{c} \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star,\mathsf{v}_2) = 0 \; \wedge \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_1^\star,\mathsf{v}_1) = 1 \end{array} \right] + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda) \end{split}$$

The reason why we can omit the check PEHash.Extract(pH.td<sub>2</sub> $^{\star}$ ,  $v_2$ ) = 0 in the first term is because they represent the same event. Moreover, we would want to drop it in order to establish the following inequality by using the predicate hiding property (by Claim B.2)

$$\Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{i-1,i-1}} \left[ \mathsf{PEHash.Extract}(\mathsf{pH.td}_1^\star,\mathsf{v}_1) = 0 \right] \leq \nu_{i-1}^{(1)} + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Thus, we finally have

$$\nu_{i-1}^{(2)}(\lambda) \le \nu_{i-1}^{(1)} + \delta_{i-1}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

where  $\delta_{i-1}(\lambda)$  is defined as the probability that the first instance of PEHash extracts a value 1 on layer i-1, while the second instance of PEHash extracts the value of 0. We prove that  $\delta_{i-1}$  is negligible for all i > 1 by extracting on a random index  $j^*$  in the  $(i-1)^{\text{th}}$  layer of  $C_f$ , i.e.,

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^M, 1^T, \{1, 2, j^\star\})$$

The proof for the same proceeds as done Claim B.3. Thus, we must have  $\delta_{i-1}(\lambda) = \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ 

At this point, we have shown that  $\nu_d(\lambda) \leq \nu_0^{(2)}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . We are yet to show that the input layer is consistent with the output. First, we show that the Extract output of PEHT<sub>2</sub> is consistent with the output. We prove it through the following claim.

Claim B.9. Assuming the extraction correctness of PEHT, for any QPT attacker  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a negligible function  $\mathsf{negl}(\cdot)$  s.t for all  $\lambda \in \mathbb{N}$ 

$$\nu_0(\lambda) = \nu_0^{(2)}(\lambda) \le \nu_{pT}(\lambda) + \text{negl}(\lambda)$$

{88}------------------------------------------------

Proof. From the steps of Claim [B.8,](#page-87-0) we have the following

$$\nu_0^{(2)}(\lambda) \le \nu_0^{(1)}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$$

Thus, we have

$$\nu_0(\lambda) \leq \nu_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda) + \Pr_{\mathsf{Hyb4}_{0,0}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} j_3 \notin J^\star \ \lor \ \mathcal{R}_t(x_{j_3}, w_{j_3}) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHash}.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pH}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star, \mathsf{v}_2, C_f) = 0 \ \land \\ \mathsf{PEHT}_2.\mathsf{Extract}(\mathsf{pT}.\mathsf{td}_2^\star, \mathsf{h}_w) = 1 \end{array} \right] \leq \nu_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda) + \delta(\lambda)$$

Assume for contradiction that δ(λ) is non-negligible. By definition, we have both v<sup>2</sup> and h<sup>w</sup> extractable on (J ⋆ , 0 J ⋆ ). Thus, by extraction correctness, we must have δ(λ) = negl(λ)

Finally, we show that the witness extracted through ExtractTag of PEHT<sup>2</sup> is consistent with the witness extracted by the seBARG2.

Claim B.10. Assuming the index hiding and somewhere argument of knowledge of seBARG2, the consistency of extraction properties of PEHT2, for any QPT attacker A, we must have νpT(λ) as a negligible function for all λ ∈ N.

Proof. We have the following for any index j <sup>⋆</sup> ∈ [k]:

$$\Pr[\mathcal{A} \text{ wins } \wedge j^{\star} = j_3] = \frac{\nu_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda)}{k} = \frac{\nu_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda)}{\mathsf{poly}(\lambda)} = \nu'_{\mathsf{pT}}(\lambda)$$

The challenger enables extraction on the set {1, 2, j⋆} for seBARG2.

$$(\mathsf{sB.crs}_2^\star, \mathsf{sB.td}_2^\star) \leftarrow \mathsf{seBARG}_2.\mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, 1^M, 1^T, \{1, 2, \textit{\textbf{j}}^\star\})$$

From here, we follow the arguments of Claim [B.3](#page-85-1) and assert that νpT(λ) must be negligible.

Thus we have ν (2) 0 (λ) = negl(λ), thereby implying that νd(λ) = µ6(λ) = negl(λ), a contradiction. Therefore, there exists a negligible function µ6(λ) s.t the advantage of attacker A in Hybrid 8 is ε(λ) − µ1(λ) − µ2(λ) − µ3(λ) − µ4(λ) − µ5(λ) − µ6(λ).
